1801 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Larock v. Bd. of Supervisors


No. 151 C.D. 2008, No. 246 C.D. 2008


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


961 A.2d 916; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 596


September 8, 2008, Argued 
December 5, 2008, Decided 
December 5, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Larock v. Bd. of Supervisors of Sugarloaf Twp., 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2283 (Pa., Oct. 28, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 3389-C of 2002. Common Pleas Court of the County of Luzerne. Ciavarella, Jr., J.
LaRock v. Bd. of Supervisors, 866 A.2d 1208, 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 34 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Board of Supervisors and taxpayers appealed an order by the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County (Pennsylvania) which granted a request for a curative amendment to a Sugarloaf Township Zoning Ordinance filed by the property owners. At issue was whether the Ordinance, which permitted a quarry only in one zoning district where all minerals had been mined that could be economically extracted, was consistent with 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 10603(i).


OVERVIEW: The property owners originally requested a variance as to three tracts of land in an area which had been rezoned. The Board dismissed the challenge to the validity of the Ordinance and denied the application and petition for a curative amendment filed by the property owners. On appeal, the trial court took additional testimony on the suitability of the site for mining, which the Board had not allowed to be presented. The trial court reversed the Board. The appellate court held it was within the trial court's discretion to allow additional testimony as the Board incorrectly precluded the property owners from presenting evidence with regard to the suitability of the proposed site. That prevented the Board from considering both the credibility and suitability issues. Although the trial court relied heavily on the fact that there were no minerals to be recovered in the I-1 District in determining that the Ordinance was invalid, it failed to note that the underlying reason for that was the minerals had already been extracted. Just because the I-1 District was mined out did not mean that the Ordinance was exclusionary or unconstitutional. The Ordinance was reasonable.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court was reversed.


 

1802 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Borough of Wilkinsburg v. Colella


No. 173 C.D. 2008


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


961 A.2d 265; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 588


October 16, 2008, Argued 
November 26, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Borough of Wilkinsburg v. Colella, 601 Pa. 703, 973 A.2d 1007, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 1174 (2009)
Related proceeding at, Decision reached on appeal by Colella v. Borough of Wilkinsburg, 2013 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 555 (2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. SA07-22. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. O'Reilly, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant borough appealed a judgment entered by the Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) that sustained the statutory appeal of appellee top-scoring applicant, thereby requiring his appointment as captain of the borough fire department.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that the former promotion provision of the Pennsylvania Second Class County Code, former 16 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 4516, required a civil service commission to promote the highest scoring applicant on a written examination. The promotion provision (§ 1184) of the Pennsylvania Borough Code, specifically, 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 46184(a), was nearly identical to former § 4516. The plain language of § 1184, which expressly addressed original positions, commanded the conclusion that the ability to select from a list of the highest scoring applicants was limited to original positions, not to promotions. The borough's position that it had discretion to choose from the top three scoring applicants was contrary to the rules of statutory construction, as it would render § 1188 (53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 46188) of the Pennsylvania Borough Code superfluous. Promotion was not synonymous with original appointments or vacancies. The borough had to appoint the top-scoring applicant to the position of captain of the borough fire department.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


 

1803 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Boron v. Pulaski Twp. Bd. of Supervisors


No. 1555 C.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


960 A.2d 880; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 574


October 15, 2008, Argued 
November 19, 2008, Decided 
November 19, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 70098 of 2006, M.D. Common Pleas Court of the County of Lawrence. Motto, President Judge.
Boron v. Pulaski Twp. Bd. of Supervisors, 2007 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 264, 83 Pa. D. & C.4th 10 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, an owner and his sexually oriented business, appealed the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lawrence County (Pennsylvania), which suspended the business's license for one year. The trial court affirmed the decision of appellee township board of supervisors that the business violated a township ordinance that forbid sexually oriented businesses from conducting business on a "state recognized holiday."


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the issue was whether the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague because it did not identify what constituted a "state recognized holiday." The appellate court concluded that the business's due process challenge was not barred by the doctrine of res judicata because the constitutionality of Pulaski Township, Pa., Ordinance No. 2000-5-22, § 18 (2000) was not at issue in a previous case where the business challenged a different phrase and a different provision of the ordinance. In addition, the business was expressly authorized to use the statutory procedure in 2 Pa.C.S. § 753(a) to litigate the constitutionality of the ordinance. The ordinance was unconstitutionally vague because it provided no guidance on how to determine whether a particular day was a "state recognized holiday." The phrase "state recognized holiday" could mean a day when state offices were closed or a day shown as a holiday on a printed calendar. The ordinance did not explain how to determine whether a holiday was "state recognized," and it did not direct that 44 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 11 was dispositive of which days of the year were to be considered "state recognized holidays."


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court was reversed.

 

1804 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Moscato v. State Bd. of Med.


No. 2290 C.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


965 A.2d 1234; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 658


September 8, 2008, Argued 
October 23, 2008, Decided 
October 23, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Publication Ordered January 14, 2009.
Reargument denied by, En banc Moscato v. State Bd. of Med., 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 974 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Dec. 12, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 1961-49-06. State Agency: State Board of Medicine.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner physician, who began treating a patient after he entered into a romantic and sexual relationship with her, sought review of an order of respondent State Board of Medicine (Pennsylvania), which suspended his medical license for two years and required him to take a continuing education course on sexual boundary issues.


OVERVIEW: The court rejected petitoner's argument that 49 Pa. Code § 16.110(b) and (c), which prohibited a physician's sexual behavior with a patient other than his spouse, violated the Equal Protection Clauses of the Fourteenth Amendment and Pa. Const. art. I, § 26. There was nothing irrational about the Board drawing a line between physicians treating spouses and treating non-spouses. Next, the Board properly applied § 16.110(b) as written. The regulation did not address when the relationship between patient and physician started, but only whether there was a current or former patient/physician relationship. Next, the Board found that there was substantial evidence presented to show that petitioner interfered with a psychiatrist's treatment of the patient, amounting to unprofessional conduct under 63 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 422.41. The Board based its decision on evidence that petitioner did not coordinate the patient's prescriptions with the psychiatrist when there was a potential for harmful drug interaction. Finally, without evidence indicating that a two-year suspension was made in bad faith, fraudulent, capricious or an abuse of power, the court would not overturn the Board's decision.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Board's order.


 

1805 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Schuylkill Prods. v. Commonwealth DOT


No. 353 M.D. 2008


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


962 A.2d 1249; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 621


September 29, 2008, Argued 
October 22, 2008, Decided 
October 22, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Publication Ordered December 19, 2008.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Transportation (DOT), filed preliminary objections in opposition to a petition for review filed in the original jurisdiction of the appellate court (Pennsylvania) by petitioners, two contractors, including contractor one, and their officers (contractors), which alleged that the DOT's debarment proceedings against contractor one were improper and without authority.


OVERVIEW: After two employees of contractor one pled guilty to taking kickbacks from a minority business enterprise, the contractors were notified of an intent to debar them under Management Directive 215.9. The appellate court held that the DOT had promulgated 67 Pa. Code § 457.13 and § 457.14 relating to suspension or debarment and the debarment appeals procedure. Whether the Management Directive was a regulation was not helpful in deciding the case. The Executive Order/Contractor Responsibility Program at 4 Pa. Code §§ 7-501-7-505, did not discuss debarment, but spoke in general terms about contractor responsibility. The Contractor Responsibility Program did not track Executive Order 1990-3. Thus, the DOT's preliminary objections were denied. The claim that contractor one did not exhaust its administrative remedies because the DOT had not yet addressed its challenges to the interpretation and application of the debarment procedures, was rejected. Because contractor one was challenging the validity of debarment procedures/regulations and the outcome would directly and immediately affect the day to day business of its operations, it did not have to exhaust its administrative remedies.


OUTCOME: The DOT's preliminary objections were denied.

 

1806 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Clarke v. House of Representatives


No. 493 M.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


957 A.2d 361; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 464


March 12, 2008, Argued 
September 26, 2008, Decided 
September 26, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Clarke v. Commonwealth, 2008 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 410 (2008)
Affirmed by Clarke v. House of Representatives, 602 Pa. 222, 980 A.2d 34, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 1845 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner councilpersons sued respondents house, senate, and association, seeking a declaration that each of seven gun ordinances may have taken immediate effect, that 53 Pa.C.S. § 6120 was unconstitutional, and that 53 Pa.C.S. § 6120 did not apply to any of the ordinances to the extent they did not regulate the carrying or transporting of firearms. The house, the senate, and the association filed preliminary objections.


OVERVIEW: The house filed preliminary objections based on ripeness and failure to state a claim upon which relief could have been granted. The senate filed preliminary objections based on lack of standing, res judicata, collateral estoppel, non-justiciability, failure to state a claim upon which relief may have been granted, and scandalous or impertinent matter. The association filed preliminary objections based on lack of standing, res judicata, scandalous, or impertinent information, non-justiciability, and failure to state a claim. The court found that regulation of firearms was a matter of concern in all of Pennsylvania, and the general assembly, not city councils, was the proper forum for the imposition of such regulation. Both 53 Pa.C.S. § 6120 and binding precedent made clear regulation of firearms was an area of statewide concern over which the general assembly had assumed sole regulatory power. Where a state statute preempted local governments from imposing regulations on a subject, any ordinances to the contrary were unenforceable. 53 Pa.C.S. § 6120 clearly preempted local governments from regulating the lawful ownership, possession, and transportation of firearms.


OUTCOME: The preliminary objections of the house, the senate, and association were sustained and the complaint was dismissed with prejudice.

 

1807 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Boniella v. Commonwealth


No. 791 C.D. 2008


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


958 A.2d 1069; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 460


July 25, 2008, Submitted 
September 25, 2008, Decided 
September 25, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by, Injunction denied by, Sub nomine at In re Boniella, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 313 (Pa., Feb. 24, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 324 M.D. 2007. Common Pleas Court of the County of Fayette. Warman, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed, pro se, from an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Fayette County (Pennsylvania) which denied his motion for the return of a handgun. The issue was whether defendant was entitled to appointed counsel in the hearing on his motion and whether the trial court erred in finding he was not entitled to lawful possession of the handgun.


OVERVIEW: The handgun was confiscated by the police when defendant was arrested for counterfeiting and related charges. Defendant filed a motion in the trial court seeking the return of the handgun, and a hearing was scheduled. Defendant filed a pre-hearing motion requesting appointment of counsel; however, that motion was denied by the trial court because it was a civil, not criminal, matter. The trial court concluded, however, that defendant was prohibited from possessing a firearm under 18 U.S.C.S. §§ 921-931. The appellate court held that the trial court did not err in denying defendant's request for the appointment of counsel. It also rejected defendant's argument that counterfeiting was a "business offense" for purposes of the Federal Gun Control Act. The plain language of the counterfeiting statute revealed that the government was not required to prove an effect on consumers or competition as an element of the offense. The trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion for return of property. Defendant could not establish that he was entitled to "lawful" possession of the handgun because, under 18 U.S.C.S. § 922, defendant was prohibited from receiving or possessing a firearm.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court was affirmed.

 

1808 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. East Brunswick Twp.


No. 476 M.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


956 A.2d 1100; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 456


June 9, 2008, Submitted 
September 23, 2008, Decided 
September 23, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Objection sustained by, Objection overruled by, Motion to strike denied by, Motion to strike granted by Commonwealth v. East Brunswick Twp., 2009 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1118 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Aug. 21, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney general (AG) filed a petition for review in the nature of a complaint for declaratory judgment and injunctive relief to invalidate a town ordinance. The AG simultaneously filed an application for summary relief pursuant to Pa.R.A.P. 1532(b). Respondent township filed preliminary objections and sought dismissal of the petition.


OVERVIEW: The AG contended that the Solid Waste Management Act, 35 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 6018.101-1003, and the Nutrient Management Act, 3 Pa.C.S. §§ 501-522, established uniform statewide standards for the regulation of the application of sewage sludge and other such nutrients to land, which precluded the East Brunswick Township Sewage Sludge Ordinance's inconsistent regulation of such activities. The subject ordinance allowed individuals to apply sewage sludge after obtaining a permit, but prohibited any corporation from doing so. The court concluded that Act 38, Chapter Three of the Pennsylvania Agriculture Code, was constitutional and did not exempt the ordinance from its prohibitory terms. The court also found that the AG had standing, despite the township's claim that the AG was doing battle on behalf of a private corporation, noting that 3 Pa.C.S. § 313(b) expressly authorized the AG's action. While the court concluded that summary relief was unavailable in cases of first impression, it did concluded that the AG was not entitled to summary relief, finding it unclear that application of sewage sludge was a "normal agricultural operation" and thus, that the AG was entitled to judgment.


OUTCOME: The township's preliminary objections were overruled. The AG's request for summary relief was denied.

 

1809 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Parente


No. 1575 C.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


956 A.2d 1065; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 397


May 6, 2008, Argued 
September 9, 2008, Decided 
September 9, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. CL/SA 712-07. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Gallo, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) which sentenced him to pay a fine totaling $ 250 based upon the adjudication of guilt for defendant's violation of Pittsburgh, Pa., Code Ordinances § 601.04(e)(2).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested after he was found using a sound system composed of two loudspeakers, an amplifier, and a microphone outside an abortion clinic. The appeals court found there was sufficient evidence to sustain the conviction. Defendant himself characterized the sound system as "a portable sound system," and an officer testified that the noise created by that system was clearly audible more than seventy-five feet away. As a result, defendant's claim that his conviction was not supported by sufficient evidence and that it was contrary to law was without merit. He failed to establish that enforcement of the Ordinance substantially burdened his right to the free exercise of religion in violation of § 4 (71 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2404) of the Pennsylvania Religious Freedom Protection Act, 71 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 2401-2407. Defendant never testified that his activities of counseling and preaching to people approaching the clinic constituted activities fundamental to his religion as provided in § 3 (71 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2403) of the Act. At best, his testimony merely established that he engaged in these activities based upon his religious beliefs or that they flowed from a religious mission.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

1810 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Roytburd v. Commonwealth


No. 332 F.R. 2006


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


958 A.2d 1064; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 567


July 3, 2008, Submitted 
September 5, 2008, Decided 
September 5, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Publication Ordered November 12, 2008.
Affirmed without opinion by Roytburd v. Commonwealth, 601 Pa. 103, 971 A.2d 1125, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 950 (Pa., May 26, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer brought a proceeding against respondent State, seeking review of a determination of the Pennsylvania Board of Finance and Revenue (board) which denied his appeal of a determination of the Department of Revenue Board of Appeals, which upheld an assessment of past due income tax, penalties, and interest by the Department of Revenue.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer argued that he was not obligated to pay income taxes because he did not engage in taxable activities. The taxpayer earned compensation income from his company, and may or may not have earned income from interest, dividends, or gains from asset sales. The taxpayer argued that he was not subject to the income tax because he was not a federal employee. The court held that, under 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7302(a), persons were subject to income tax if they were "resident individuals." No reference, explicit or implied, was made in this section that meant that the state income tax applied only to federal employees. Contrary to the taxpayer's argument, the Sixteenth Amendment did not bar taxation of income obtained through the use of personal property. The Sixteenth Amendment only related to the taxing power of Congress, and not that of the states. Finally, since the taxpayer received compensation income during the tax years at issue, he received a privilege for which he was subject to the income tax. The taxpayer was a "resident individual" as defined by 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7301(p), and thus, was subject to the income tax pursuant to 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7302(a).


OUTCOME: The order of the board was affirmed.

 

1811 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


W. Mifflin Area Sch. Dist. v. Zahorchak


No. 371 M.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


956 A.2d 1040; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 391


June 11, 2008, Argued 
September 4, 2008, Decided 
September 4, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reversed by, Remanded by W. Mifflin Area Sch. Dist. v. Zahorchak, 2010 Pa. LEXIS 2174 (Pa., Sept. 29, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: All parties sought summary relief in petitioner school districts' declaratory judgment action challenging the constitutionality of §§ 5 and 14 of 2007 Pa. Laws 278 (Act 45), codified as 24 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 11-1113(b.2) and 16-1607.1, as in violation of Pa. Const. art. III, § 32 and Pa. Const. art. II, § 1. Respondents were the secretary of education, the department of education, a city school district, and a city education association.


OVERVIEW: Respondent district had been placed on an education empowerment list based on low test performance under 24 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 17-1702-B. The special board of control appointed to oversee respondent district under 24 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 6-692 decided to eliminate respondent district's high school program, and students in respondent district enrolled in high schools in petitioners. The court granted summary relief to respondents. Because § 16-1607.1(a) had criteria related rationally to the objective of identifying school districts with educational shortcomings and providing assistance if necessary when a district eliminated its high school program, that provision did not violate Pa. Const. art. III, § 32, which was similar to equal protection. Section 11-1113(b.2) was rationally related to a legitimate state interest of ensuring that qualified teachers remained in public school service. No unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority had occurred under Pa. Const. art. II, § 1 as the basic policy choices were made by the legislature concerning geographic proximity and dispersal of students among two or more other districts.


OUTCOME: The court granted summary relief to respondents and denied summary relief to petitioners. The petition for review was dismissed with prejudice.

 

1812 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Summer's Best Two Weeks v. Dep't of Conservation & Natural Res.


No. 222 M.D. 2006


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


954 A.2d 94; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 334


April 7, 2008, Argued 
July 25, 2008, Decided 
July 25, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to the court's original jurisdiction, petitioner camp filed a petition for review in equity against respondent Pennsylvania Department of Conservation and Natural Resources (DCNR). It sought injunctive relief and a declaration that the DCNR's decision prohibiting it from conducting regular whitewater rafting trips violated due process. Both parties filed motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The camp conducted whitewater rafting trips on a river that passed through a state park. In 2001, the DCNR determined that the camp was engaged in commercial activity as an unlicensed outfitter and therefore under 17 Pa. Code § 11.209(a)(5) could not guide trips on the river unless it used a licensed commercial outfitter. The court stated that the camp conceded that it was engaged in commercial activity; therefore, the initial inquiry had to be whether the DCNR was interpreting and applying the regulation to the camp in a reasonable manner. The court held that it was. The park here was a state park, and the DCNR had the authority under 71 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1340.313(a) to regulate activities within its own parks. Section 11.209(a)(5) specifically and unequivocally prohibited all commercial activity in any state park without the written permission of the DCNR. Thus, the camp was precluded from engaging in commercial activity inside the state park without the DCNR's written permission as a matter of regulatory interpretation. Because of this conclusion, it was unnecessary to address the camp's constitutional arguments.


OUTCOME: The court granted the DCNR's cross-motion for summary judgment and entered judgment in its favor. It denied the camp's motion for summary judgment.

 

1813 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Council 13, AFSCME v. Commonwealth


No. 322 M.D. 2008


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


954 A.2d 706; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 378


June 27, 2008, Heard 
July 23, 2008, Decided 
July 23, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Publication Ordered August 12, 2008.
Affirmed in part and reversed in part by, Appeal dismissed by, in part Council 13 v. Commonwealth, 604 Pa. 352, 986 A.2d 63, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2771 (Pa., Dec. 28, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, three unions, filed a petition for review by way of a declaratory judgment that respondents, the Commonwealth and various officials, were not "legally required" under Pa. Const. art. III, § 24 and the federal Fair Labor Standards Act (FLSA), 29 U.S.C.S. §§ 201-219, to agree on a framework for a general appropriation act for the current fiscal year in order to avoid Commonwealth employees being furloughed. Summary relief was sought.


OVERVIEW: The unions represented thousands of Commonwealth employees who were not exempt from the wage and hour requirements of the FLSA. Such employees, deemed FLSA covered non-critical employees, would have allegedly been furloughed on the first date of the new fiscal year if the Governor and General Assembly had not agreed on the framework for a general appropriation act for the 2008-2009 fiscal year by the day prior thereto. The unions sought a declaration that the furlough plan was not legally required, as claimed by the Governor, if a budget was not in place in a timely manner. They relied on their claim that the FLSA superseded Pa. Const. art. III, § 24, such that there was no impediment to the Governor's continued compensation of FLSA-covered employees who worked during a budget impasse. The court initially noted that although technically moot, the matter was capable of repetition, and that the unions' matter was properly raised under 42 Pa.C.S. § 7541(a). It held that there was no conflict between the FLSA and § 24, such that the FLSA did not preempt § 24 and allow payment of employee wages without the necessary budgeted appropriation.


OUTCOME: The court denied the unions' request for a declaration that the Governor could have raided the State's treasury to make payroll, and it denied the Governor's request for a declaration regarding the use of treasury funds without an appropriation. The court granted the Governor's request to declare that he was authorized to furlough employees due to lack of funds.

 

1814 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



In re Opening a Private Rd.


No. 2214 CD 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


954 A.2d 57; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 319


May 7, 2008, Argued 
July 11, 2008, Decided 
July 11, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by In re O'Reilly, 601 Pa. 86, 971 A.2d 486, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 740 (2009)
Vacated by, Remanded by In re Opening a Private Rd. ex rel. O'Reilly, 5 A.3d 246, 2010 Pa. LEXIS 2272 (Pa., 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. GD 04-02972. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Fike, II, J.
In re Opening a Private Rd. ex rel. Timothy P. O'Reilly over Lands of Hickory on the Green Homeowners Ass'n, 2007 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 450 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a homeowners association and its constituent property owners, sought review of a decision of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny (Pennsylvania), which denied their preliminary objections to appellee property owner's request for appointment of a board of viewers to open a private road under the Private Road Act, 36 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 1781-2891, so that his landlocked parcels could have access to the nearest public road.


OVERVIEW: Appellants claimed that the Private Road Act was an unconstitutional taking of property for a private purpose in violation of the Fifth Amendment to the United States Constitution and Pa. Const. art. I, §§ 1 and 10. On an interlocutory appeal based on the public importance of the issue, the court affirmed. After reviewing the legislative history of the Private Road Act, the court found that private roads were considered part of the public road system; because six percent was given to the original landowners, an incorporeal burden was created on all the Commonwealth's land that, in accordance with the Act, private roads were to be allowed to be laid when necessary. Even if the court used a traditional takings analysis to determine the constitutionality of the Act, a taking would be seen as having a public purpose where the real or fundamental purpose behind a taking primarily benefited the public; because, as the trial court held, the public would be served by allowing access to property that would not be otherwise used, the Act did not violate either the federal or state constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order and remanded the matter to the trial court for the appointment of viewers.


 

1815 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Sands Bethworks Gaming, LLC v. Pa. Dep't of Revenue


No. 577 M.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


958 A.2d 125; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 459


June 12, 2008, Argued 
July 11, 2008, Decided 
July 11, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Publication Ordered September 24, 2008.
Affirmed by Sands Bethworks Gaming, LLC v. Pa. Dep't of Revenue, 600 Pa. 511, 968 A.2d 763, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 706 (Pa., Apr. 29, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Pennsylvania Department of Revenue filed a motion to quash and preliminary objections to petitioner licensee's petition for review, which invoked original and appellate jurisdiction and challenged the method by which costs incurred by the Pennsylvania Gaming Control Board were assessed and the time frame in which payments to local municipalities were calculated.


OVERVIEW: The Department's Board of Appeals returned, for lack of jurisdiction, the slot machine licensee's petition alleging that 61 Pa. Code § 1001.6(d)(3) and the Department's letters unfairly calculated the share of general administrative costs gaming facilities paid based on a pro rata share of revenues and that the local share assessment under 61 Pa. Code § 1001.5(c)(1) was inconsistent with the Gaming Act, 4 Pa.C.S. §§ 1101-1904. The Department's Board of Finance and Revenue (BFR) returned the appeal for lack of jurisdiction. The court sustained the Department's preliminary objections and dismissed the licensee's petition for review. The court did not have original jurisdiction, pursuant to 42 Pa.C.S. § 761(a)(1), over the pre-enforcement challenge because the licensee was not yet operating, the Department had not yet calculated its gross terminal revenue or levied an assessment, and there was a full and complete adequate administrative remedy involving the refund of funds found to be excessive. The court denied the motion to quash the appeal, which the BFR dismissed, and held that the BFR lacked the authority to hear pre-enforcement challenges to departmental policies or regulation.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the preliminary objections, denied the motion to quash, affirmed the finding of the BFR that it did not have jurisdiction to hear the administrative appeals, and dismissed the petition for review.

Howard v. Commonwealth


No. 96 M.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


957 A.2d 332; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 457


April 18, 2008, Submitted 
July 11, 2008, Decided 
July 11, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Publication Ordered Septempber 24, 2008.
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Transferred by Howard v. Wetzel, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 79299 (M.D. Pa., June 6, 2013)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Without prejudice, Application granted by Commonwealth v. Howard, 2014 Pa. LEXIS 1469 (Pa., June 12, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, the governor and the secretary of the Commonwealth and the Speaker of the House and the President Pro Tempore of the Senate, made preliminary objections to petitioner's first amended petition for review, complaint in declaratory judgment, and bill of equity (petition), which challenged the constitutionality of 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 6322 and 6355 regarding the transfer of criminal matters from adult to juvenile court.


OVERVIEW: In 1991, petitioner, who was 15 years old, was arrested for the murder and robbery of a taxi driver. Petitioner was tried as an adult after an unsuccessful attempt to be tried as a juvenile and was convicted in 1992 of criminal homicide/felony murder, criminal conspiracy, and robbery. The conviction was affirmed on direct appeal in 1994. Requests for relief under the Post Conviction Relief Act, 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9541-9546, were also unsuccessful. The court sustained in part and overruled in part the preliminary objections. While the governor and secretary were properly served via certified mail pursuant to Pa.R.A.P. 1514(c) with the petition for review, they were not indispensable parties under Pa.R.C.P. No. 235 and Pa.R.A.P. 521 as, inter alia, the governor only signed the amendments into law. Petitioner had no standing to bring the action under Pa.R.C.P. No. 1028(a)(5) as he did not commit his October 1991 delinquent act on or after the effective date of 1995 Pa. Laws 1127 (Act 33), 120 days from November 17, 1995, and he was not convicted under its provisions. Based on precedent, the same provisions were upheld against a constitutional challenge, so the petition was dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court overruled the preliminary objections as to service on the governor and the secretary but sustained the objections to inclusion of the governor and the secretary as indispensable parties, petitioner's standing, and failure to state a claim. The petition was dismissed on the basis that the law would not permit a recovery.

5708 K&T, Inc. v. Pa. Liquor Control Bd.


No. 2055 C.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


951 A.2d 1232; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 272


April 4, 2008, Submitted 
June 13, 2008, Decided 
June 13, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 00200 March Term, 2007. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Greenspan, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Pennsylvania State Police, Bureau of Liquor Control Enforcement (Bureau) challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee applicant's appeal and reversed the decision of the Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board to sustain citations the Bureau issued to the applicant for violating § 407 of the Liquor Code, 47 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 4-407 (2005).


OVERVIEW: The Philadelphia City Council denied the applicant's request for an off premises sales permit for its restaurant. The Bureau issued citations to the application for violating § 4-407 (2005), alleging that the applicant sold malt or brewed beverages for consumption off premises. The Board sustained the citations. The trial court ruled that it could not enforce citations that were issued under a procedure that was considered unconstitutional. The appellate court held that the trial court properly found that the citations were not valid because they were based upon the unconstitutional process in § 4-407 (2005). Section 4-407 (2005) was unconstitutional from the date of its enactment, July 7, 2005, which was prior to the date of the alleged offenses. Therefore, the controlling law on the date of the offenses was § 407 of the Liquor Code, 47 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 4-407 (2004), by virtue of which no permit was required to sell one bottle or one can of beer to take out. Because the applicant appealed the Board's decision to the trial court, the trial court was correct in relying upon the fact that the permit process had been found unconstitutional prior to making a decision.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the order of the trial court.

Mazin v. Bureau of Prof'l & Occupational Affairs


No. 321 M.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


950 A.2d 382; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 269


March 14, 2007, Submitted 
June 10, 2008, Decided 
June 10, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Mazin v. Bureau of Prof'l & Occupational Affairs, 599 Pa. 684, 960 A.2d 457, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1959 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.
Mazin v. Bureau of Prof'l & Occupational Affairs, 2008 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 392 (2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner dentist filed an amended petition for review against respondent Department of State and State Board of Dentistry members, alleging, inter alia, negligence and seeking mandamus and declaratory relief from the Board's order assessing a fine. The Department filed preliminary objections to the court's original jurisdiction. After the fine order was affirmed, resolution of the preliminary objections was assigned to the panel.


OVERVIEW: The court lacked original jurisdiction over the negligence claim where the complaint included a distinct count seeking money damages based upon the Board's alleged negligence, and under applicable case law, the court could not address either the merits of the count or the threshold question of whether the Board enjoyed sovereign immunity. The dentist was not entitled to injunctive relief as the Board had altered its procedures for requests to extend the time to comply with the continuing education requirements and the dentist had not shown that the changes did not resolve the difficulties he had allegedly encountered. The preliminary objection to the mandamus count was sustained as the dentist failed to show that he was entitled to such relief. The court lacked jurisdiction over the declaratory judgment action for similar reasons. Moreover, the appellate decision had answered the questions that the request raised. Finally, the court lacked jurisdiction over the Americans with Disabilities Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 12131 et seq., claim as the relief sought was limited to money damages and attorney fees, and such relief was not encompassed within the court's original jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The preliminary objections to the injunctive, mandamus, and declaratory relief requests were sustained. The remaining claims were transferred to the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania). Jurisdiction was relinquished.

In re S. E. Cent. Bus. Dist. Redevelopment # 1


No. 478 C.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


946 A.2d 1143; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 177


March 12, 2008, Argued 
April 22, 2008, Decided 
April 22, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by In re Condemnation of Land (Petition of Yarnall), 2009 Pa. LEXIS 281 (Pa., Feb. 18, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 05-8011. Common Pleas Court of the County of Delaware. Clouse, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed the landowner's preliminary objections to the declaration of taking filed by appellee, a redevelopment authority of a city. The declaration filed by the authority stated that the property was being condemned to acquire and redevelop educational buildings, including charter school buildings.


OVERVIEW: The landowner owned the property at issue, which was being used by him as a strip club. The property was located in a redevelopment district, which the city's planning commission had certified as blighted. The landowner's preliminary objections to the declaration primarily asserted that the authority condemned the property for the private, for-profit use of a redeveloper. The court held that the fact that the authority selected the redeveloper and negotiated with him a contract for the sale and redevelopment of the property before the condemnation did not establish the landowner's claim that the authority took the property for the redeveloper's private use. The court noted that the fact that the redeveloper may profit from the sale and redevelopment of the property did not negate the redeveloper's contribution to the elimination of the blight in the redevelopment area and there was no support for the landowner's conjecture that the public benefit was only incidental to the redeveloper's profits. The court also found that the landowner waived several issues on appeal by not raising them in his preliminary objections.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order dismissing the landowner's preliminary objections.

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 2006-3624. Common Pleas Court of the County of Cambria. Leahey, J.
Jae v. Good, 918 A.2d 848, 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 143 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Mar. 6, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate sought review of an order from the Common Pleas Court of the County of Cambria (Pennsylvania), which dismissed a civil rights complaint under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 that he filed against appellees, employees of the Department of Corrections, challenging the conditions where he was incarcerated. The trial court dismissed the complaint under the Prison Litigation Reform Act (PLRA), 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 6601-6608.


OVERVIEW: The inmate filed a civil rights complaint, alleging that the mail policy at the correction institution where he was incarcerated violated his constitutional right to read books that posed no threat to others. His request to proceed in forma pauperis was denied and the complaint was dismissed as frivolous under Pa.R.C.P. No. 240(j). On appeal, the dismissal was reversed. The trial court, on remand, found that 14 of the inmate's prior civil prison condition actions were dismissed as frivolous, malicious, or not stating a viable claim under § 6602(f) of the PLRA, 42 Pa.C.S. § 6602(f). I, §§ 1 and 26 based on the rational basis test. The trial court properly applied the Pennsylvania PLRA, and there was no violation of the Ex Post Facto Clause of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 and Pa. Const. art. I, § 17, as the PLRA was procedural. Use of actions filed by the inmate prior to the enactment of the PLRA for purposes of three strikes was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant objectors appealed a decision by appellee zoning board of adjustment (ZBA) to grant variances to appellee property owner and appellee advertising company to permit the erection of an outdoor advertising sign. The Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania) quashed the objectors' appeal on grounds that the objectors lacked standing to challenge the ZBA's determination. The objectors appealed.


OVERVIEW: The property owner and the advertising company contended that the objectors, who were associations or individuals, were not aggrieved persons and, thus, lacked standing to appeal the ZBA's decision under § 17.1 of the Pennsylvania First Class City Home Rule Act, 53 Pa. Finally, they argued that they had standing as aggrieved persons either because the associations' boundaries covered the district where the sign was to be erected or because members of the associations had homes in the general area. On appeal, the court found that the objectors' appeal was properly quashed because neither the associations nor the individuals showed that they were aggrieved by the ZBA's decision to allow the sign.


OUTCOME: The order of the court of common pleas was affirmed.

Danzilli v. Lomeo


No. 1449 CD. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


944 A.2d 813; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 123


February 13, 2008, Argued 
March 17, 2008, Decided 
March 17, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. GD 06-30525. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Horgos, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Allegheny County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania) denied a motion for judgment on the pleadings and dismissed appellant deputy mayor's declaratory judgment action seeking a determination that a trust holding municipal funds for retiree medical benefits fell within the meaning of "pension and retirement funds" under the Pennsylvania Probate, Estates, and Fiduciaries Code, 20 Pa.C.S. § 7301. The deputy mayor appealed.


OVERVIEW: The municipality was obligated to pay medical benefits for its retirees after they retired. To fund those obligations, it created a trust to set aside funds to pay medical benefits to its employees after they retired. To keep borrowing costs low, the municipality sought to invest the trust funds according to the Pennsylvania Probate, Estates, and Fiduciaries Code, 20 Pa.C.S. § 7301 et seq. (PEF Code), which would allow investment in corporate stocks and bonds. Appellee mayor did not believe that the trust funds could be invested that way. The deputy mayor sought a declaration that the trust funded a "municipal pension or retirement plan" under 20 Pa.C.S. § 7301. After the trial court agreed with the mayor's position, the appellate court noted that the PEF Code did not define "municipal pension plan" or "retirement plan." It found, however, that statutory definitions in the Municipal Pension Plan Funding Standard and Recovery Act, 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 895.101 et seq., and the treatment of retiree medical benefits in other contexts supported the conclusion that a trust funding retiree medical benefits was a pension or retirement plan pursuant to 20 Pa.C.S. § 7301.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

Richardson v. Beard


No. 329 M.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


942 A.2d 911; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 20


October 26, 2007, Submitted 
January 18, 2008, Decided 
January 18, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Richardson v. Beard, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 2310 (Pa., Dec. 19, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate filed a pro se action for review against respondent official in which petitioner challenged the Department of Corrections' Release of Information Policy as it pertained to charges for the reproduction of inmate medical records. Respondent filed a preliminary objection in the nature of a demurrer.


OVERVIEW: The court rejected respondent's argument that petitioner's claims should have been raised initially in a grievance. 42 Pa.C.S. § 6603(a) applied only when prison litigation alleged in whole or in part a violation of federal law, which was not the case here. Next, the policy was not a regulation as defined in 45 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1102(12). It did not establish a standard of conduct that had the force of law, and it left the Department free to exercise discretion to follow or not follow the policy in an individual case. Thus, it was not subject to the administrative requirements of the Commonwealth Documents Law, 45 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 1101-1602. Petitioner had not stated a claim under 28 Pa. Code § 115.29. He had not alleged that he was denied access to his records, that he requested copies of his medical records and was charged unreasonable fees, or that he was a patient and that the Department operated a health care facility within the meaning of the Health Care Facilities Act, 35 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 448.101 - 448.904b. Finally, the bare allegations that the fees were inflated and unjustified, without a context in which the fees had been applied, failed to state a claim.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the demurrer and dismissed the petition for review.

Pinnacle Health Sys. v. Dep't of Pub. Welfare


Nos. 24-29 C.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


942 A.2d 189; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 14


December 7, 2007, Submitted 
January 16, 2008, Decided 
January 16, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by Pinnacle Health Sys. v. Dep't of Pub. Welfare, 2009 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 227 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Mar. 14, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. W06-3102 - W06-3107. State Agency: Bureau of Hearings and Appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner hospital system sought administrative review of respondent Pennsylvania Department of Public Welfare's (DPW) finding that its psychiatric care was below accepted medical standards. The Bureau of Hearings and Appeals (Pennsylvania) (BHA) adopted an administrative law judge's (ALJ) decision that this standard of care was daily examination by a psychiatrist, and denied the hospital's appeal. The hospital sought judicial review.


OVERVIEW: The hospital said 55 Pa. Code § 1101.75(a)(7)'s falling "below accepted medical treatment standard" language was unconstitutionally vague. The appellate court held the standard of meeting "accepted medical treatment standards" was general but gave sufficient notice to care providers that to receive Medicaid reimbursement, they had to ascertain the accepted standard in the medical community and comply with it. DPW's expert's testimony was legally sufficient to support a finding that failure to examine psychiatric patients on a daily basis was below accepted medical treatment standards because the hospital stipulated the witness was an expert, and the weight given his testimony was for the ALJ. The expert's ascertainment of the level of care in other institutions was relevant in finding the "accepted medical treatment standard." He properly reviewed medical records from other hospitals, and his opinion was also based on his education and experience as a psychiatrist for numerous acute inpatient facilities. The ALJ's determination of the credibility of experts was not disturbed on appeal.


OUTCOME: The BHA's decision was affirmed.

Buckwalter v. Borough of Phoenixville


No. 1361 C.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


940 A.2d 617; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 7


December 10, 2007, Argued 
January 8, 2008, Decided 
January 8, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by Buckwalter v. Borough of Phoenixville, 598 Pa. 210, 955 A.2d 1014, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1437 (2008)
Reversed by Buckwalter v. Borough of Phoenixville, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 2756 (Pa., Dec. 28, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 07-00522. Common Pleas Court of the County of Chester. Gavin, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant borough councilman appealed an order entered by the Common Pleas Court of the County of Chester (Pennsylvania), which granted a motion for judgment on the pleadings filed by appellee borough in a declaratory judgment action filed by the councilman seeking a declaration that, to the extent that a pay ordinance eliminated the compensation of council members during their terms of office, it violated Pa. Const. art. III, § 27 (§ 27).


OVERVIEW: The appellate court held that Baldwin v. Philadelphia, which held that a municipal ordinance was not a law for purposes of § 27, controlled. Under Baldwin, because a municipal ordinance was not a "law" within the meaning of former Pa. Const. art. III, § 13 (now § 27), a mid-term salary increase could be effected by ordinance without violating that particular constitutional provision. The councilman's claim that the constitutional prohibition against mid-term changes in compensation for public officials that was imposed on the Pennsylvania general assembly by § 27 had to apply with equal force to the borough was rejected. The cases cited by the councilman were distinguished, or did not address the application of § 27 to municipal ordinances. The claim that § 1001 of the Pennsylvania Borough Code, 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 46001 (§ 1001), had to be read to incorporate the restriction of § 27 was rejected. Section 1001 only codified the 126-year-old precedent established in Baldwin. Finally, the councilman's claim, after a discussion of Blackstone, that contrary to the holding in Baldwin, a municipal ordinance also had to be considered to be a "law" was rejected.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


 

1828 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Miller v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd. (Electrolux)


No. 552 C.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


940 A.2d 603; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 25


October 29, 2007, Argued 
January 4, 2008, Decided 
January 4, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Miller v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd. (Electrolux), 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1252 (Pa., Aug. 7, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. A06-1629. State Agency: Workers' Compensation Appeal Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner widow filed a claim petition seeking to enforce a Compromise and Release Agreement. A workers' compensation judge (WCJ) denied the petition. Respondent Pennsylvania Workers' Compensation Appeal Board affirmed the WCJ's decision. The widow appealed.


OVERVIEW: The deceased claimant sustained a right ankle injury at work. The claimant's employer offered to settle the claim for $ 25,000. The employer required Medicare approval to effectuate the Compromise and Release Agreement. The claimant died before the Agreement was executed. On appeal, the widow argued that the employer's insistence on getting Medicare approval stalled the process and imposed a condition that was not required by the Pennsylvania Workers' Compensation Act. The court concluded that the failure to satisfy the statutory requirements of § 449 of the Act, 77 Pa. Stat. Ann. §1000.5, precluded approval of the Agreement. The death of the claimant before the requirements of signature, notary and hearing was not recognized in the Act as providing any exception. Contrary to the widow's claim, Medicare's approval of the Agreement was a legitimate condition upon which the parties failed to agree. Because the Agreement was never finalized, there was no Agreement for the WCJ to approve. Finally, the court concluded that there was no due process violation, as the compromise and release of workers' compensation claims was not a constitutionally protected interest.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the Board was affirmed.

Locust Lake Vill. Prop. Owners Ass'n v. Monroe County Bd. of Assessment Appeals


No. 738 C.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


940 A.2d 591; 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 24


October 30, 2007, Argued 
January 4, 2008, Decided 
January 4, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 3664 April Term 2006. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Glazer, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant homeowners association brought an action against appellee taxing authorities. The Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County (Pennsylvania) denied the association's claim for a retrospective refund of taxes paid on land owned by the association. The association appealed.


OVERVIEW: The association sought a refund of real estate tax paid for the tax years 2001 to 2003. The trial court held that a refund could not be issued in any tax year for which the association had not challenged its assessment. The court stated that a taxpayer could not claim a refund under the Tax Refund Law, 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 5566b, when the taxpayer had a remedy available under any other statute. Here, 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 5453.704 provided the association with a statutory remedy to contest the assessments for which it now sought a refund. Accordingly, the association was not entitled to a retrospective tax refund under the Tax Refund Law for 2001 to 2003. Next, the Pennsylvania Uniform Planned Community Act, 68 Pa.C.S. §§ 5101-5414, did not place the burden on the taxing authorities to investigate, on their own initiative, the status of each parcel in a planned community as either a unit or a common facility. It was a taxpayer's burden to prove its right to a tax exemption.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

City of Philadelphia v. SEPTA


No. 1578 C.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


937 A.2d 1176; 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 699


October 31, 2007, Argued 
December 14, 2007, Decided 
December 14, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. July Term, 2007 Nos. 3915 and 1919. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Di Vito, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellees, a city and its mayor, sued appellant Southeastern Pennsylvania Transportation Authority (SEPTA), challenging its decision to eliminate paper transfers. The Philadelphia County Common Pleas Court (Pennsylvania) vacated SEPTA's decision, finding it was arbitrary and capricious. SEPTA appealed; appellees moved to dismiss the appeal as moot.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that SEPTA decided to eliminate paper transfers without any study of the impact on those who would be most adversely affected. In view of the real potential for harm to those who most heavily relied upon SEPTA, the trial court concluded that SEPTA's decision was "capricious" and was a manifest and flagrant abuse of its discretion. While the instant appeal was pending, SEPTA approved a new resolution that superseded the challenged plan and restored paper transfers. The resolution also indicated that SEPTA might revisit the paper transfer issue if, on appeal, its decision to eliminate them was affirmed. The instant court held that SEPTA's approval of the resolution rendered its appeal moot. The appeal did not involve whether, in the abstract, SEPTA could eliminate paper transfers, but whether the trial court erred in concluding that SEPTA abused its discretion in eliminating transfers under the facts of this particular case. If SEPTA again voted to eliminate transfers and appellees again challenged the action, the challenge would arise under a different set of facts.


OUTCOME: SEPTA's appeal was dismissed as moot.

Ballerino v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd. (Darby Borough)

Appealed from No. A06-2114. Workers' Compensation Appeal Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Workers' Compensation Appeal Board (Pennsylvania) entered a decision that calculated petitioner volunteer firefighter's disability compensation by using the statutory formula established for voluntary firefighters rather than calculation proposed by the volunteer firefighter. The volunteer firefighter appealed.


OVERVIEW: The volunteer firefighter sustained a disabling injury while working for the employer. That entitled the volunteer firefighter to collect disability compensation of a certain weekly amount, using the formula for volunteer firefighters injured in the line of duty, 77 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1031(b). That formula presumed that the wages for calculating disability benefits was at least equal to the Statewide average weekly wage. The volunteer firefighter asserted that his weekly earnings from his work as a truck driver that he was unable to earn while on total disability should have been added to his presumed statutory wage. Under his proposed methodology, he was compensated in excess of the presumed statutory wage for volunteer firefighters. A workers' compensation judge denied the claim petition upon finding that presumed wages and actual wages could not be stacked. The Workers' Compensation Appeal Board affirmed. The appellate court found that the statute that allowed wages from different employers to be added, 77 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 582, could not be applied in the case of volunteer firefighting since that work was not performed under a "concurrent contract" of employment.


OUTCOME: The Workers' Compensation Appeal Board's decision was affirmed.


 

1832 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Commonwealth v. Hoffman


No. 850 C.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


938 A.2d 1157; 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 693


October 9, 2007, Submitted 
December 11, 2007, Decided 
December 11, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 71-75, 77-78 SA 2006. Common Pleas Court of the County of Mercer. Fornelli, President Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of sentence entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Mercer County (Pennsylvania) which sentenced defendant to pay fines totaling $ 700.00 based upon the adjudication of guilt for defendant's seven violations of City of Sharon, Penn., Codified Ordinances § 829.09.


OVERVIEW: Because defendant's convictions were based upon a void ordinance, the court agreed that its continued enforcement would have violated defendant's due process rights. The court found that the applicable statutory provisions restricted the collection of municipal obligations to those directly associated with the particular property for which the taxes or claims were owed. City of Sharon, Penn., Codified Ordinances § 829.09 imposed a disability on a delinquent property owner, not on delinquent property. The ordinance was a tax collection device not authorized by the Third Class City Code. The city lacked the express, implied and necessary power to enact the provisions in the ordinance to withhold licenses and permits as a means of collecting real estate taxes and municipal debt. As a result, the provisions in the ordinance authorizing the imposition of civil fines and imprisonment were void. Because the judgment of sentence was based upon those void provisions in the ordinance, it had to be reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of sentence and discharged defendant.


 

1833 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Thorek v. DOT, Bureau of Driver Licensing


No. 288 C.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


938 A.2d 505; 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 648


September 7, 2007, Submitted 
December 4, 2007, Decided 
December 4, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Thorek v. DOT, Bureau of Driver Licensing, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 991 (Pa., June 26, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 10536 of 2006. Common Pleas Court of the County of Luzerne. Lokuta, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commercial licensee challenged a judgment of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Luzerne (Pennsylvania), which dismissed his statutory appeal of a one-year disqualification of his commercial vehicle operating privileges pursuant to 75 Pa.C.S. § 1611(a)(1) of the Vehicle Code by appellee, a state department of transportation (department).


OVERVIEW: The licensee was convicted for driving under the influence of alcohol while operating an automobile (DUI). The licensee asserted that the department did not have the authority to disqualify him from operating a commercial vehicle because an Accelerated Rehabilitative Disposition (ARD) was not a conviction under 75 Pa.C.S. § 1603. On appeal, the court found that § 1603 expressly authorized the department to disqualify the licensee based upon his acceptance into ARD. Licensee's attempt to rely on 75 Pa.C.S. § 6501(c), which did not include ARD in the definition of "conviction," violated 75 Pa.C.S. § 1602(b), which directed that 75 Pa.C.S. § 1603 prevailed in a conflict. The suspension of his license was civil in nature and was separate from his criminal DUI proceeding. Thus, the legislature did not impermissibly infringe on the court's authority to define "conviction." The greater harm that could be caused by commercial vehicles justified the imposition of harsher sanctions on commercial drivers than non-commercial drivers. Thus, there was no equal protection violation under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, Penn. Const. art. I, § 26, or Penn. Const. art. III, § 32.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Lutes v. Fayette County Bd. of Assessment Appeals


No. 594 C.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


936 A.2d 573; 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 633


October 9, 2007, Argued 
November 30, 2007, Decided 
November 30, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 2605 of 2002 G.D. Common Pleas Court of the County of Fayette. Warman, J.
Jordan v. Fayette County Bd. of Assessment Appeals, 782 A.2d 642, 2001 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 624 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2001)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer filed a class action suit against appellee county board of assessment appeals, seeking refunds for overpayment of property taxes under an allegedly unconstitutional dual assessment scheme. The Fayette County Common Pleas Court (Pennsylvania) denied the taxpayer's petition for rule to show cause and dismissed her appeal. The taxpayer sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer sought retroactive relief for past overpayments based on alleged over-assessments under the county's dual assessment scheme. The board lowered her current assessment, but denied her claim for retroactive relief. The trial held the board could not make changes to the valuation of property for the years preceding the current assessment, and that, if no appeal was taken from an assessment of taxes within the time allowed by law, the assessment became binding and conclusive. The appellate court agreed. While 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 5020-511, 5453.703c, 5566b, and 5566c provided for tax refunds, they did not authorize retroactive assessment appeals. Therefore, because the taxpayer failed to challenge any assessments before the board during the time prescribed by law, neither the trial court nor the appellate court had subject matter jurisdiction over her appeal and thus could not provide relief. Finally, requests for tax refunds could not be pursued by class action.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Marcavage v. Rendell


No. 195 M.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


936 A.2d 188; 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 616


June 13, 2007, Argued 
November 15, 2007, Decided 
November 15, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by, Adopted by Marcavage v. Rendell, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1210 (Pa., July 23, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.
Marcavage v. Rendell, 888 A.2d 940, 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 755 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner arrestees sued respondents, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania and several of its officials, alleging 2002 Pa. Laws 143 (Act 143), which amended § 2710 (18 Pa.C.S. § 2710) of the Pennsylvania Crimes Code, violated Pa. Const. art. III, §§ 1, 2, 3, and 4. Both sides filed motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Act 143 amended 18 Pa.C.S. § 2710, relating to the offense of ethnic intimidation, to include acts of malicious intention toward a person's actual or perceived sexual orientation, gender, or gender identity, when such conduct occurred in the course of the commission of certain other offenses. Petitioners filed this action after they were arrested and charged under Act 143 for evangelizing against individuals who were participating in a gay rights event. Although the charges were dismissed, petitioners asserted that they intended to engage in the same type of activity in the future. They argued that Act 143 violated Pa. Const. art. III, § 1, which provided that no bill could be altered or amended, on its passage through either House, as to change its original purpose. The court held that the original purpose of the bill which ultimately became Act 143, viewed in reasonably broad terms, was to criminalize crop destruction. As ultimately enacted, Act 143 expanded the classification of persons protected under the offense of ethnic intimidation. Based on the text of the initial bill, the amendments did not retain the original purpose of the bill.


OUTCOME: Petitioners' motion for summary relief was granted and respondents' motion for summary relief was denied. Act 143 of 2002 was declared unconstitutional and therefore null and void.

R.H.S. v. Allegheny County Dep't of Human Servs., Office of Mental Health


No. 1338 C.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


936 A.2d 1218; 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 622


August 31, 2007, Submitted 
November 15, 2007, Decided 
November 15, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by, En banc, Rehearing denied by, En banc R.H.S. v. Allegheny County Dep't of Human Servs., Office of Mental Health, 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 10 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Jan. 8, 2008)
Appeal denied by Steinmetz v. Allegheny County Dep't of Human Servs., 598 Pa. 753, 954 A.2d 579, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1127 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. GD03-013654. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Folino, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant patient sued appellee department, and others, alleging various causes of action including, inter alia, intentional torts resulting from the submission of an "Act 77 Historical" data form (Act 77 form) to the Pennsylvania State Police (PSP) relating to the patient's involuntary commitment to a mental health facility. The Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny (Pennsylvania) dismissed the action. The patient appealed.


OVERVIEW: The patient alleged that false information was submitted to the PSP by the Act 77 form. The appellate court found that, despite the patient's allegations otherwise, the employee submitting the form possessed the required physician's certification under 18 Pa.C.S. § 6105(c)(4). Since the employee attached the physician's certification to the Act 77 form, the patient's claim failed. The trial court's reference to the patient's prior lawsuit did not provide the basis for its ruling on preliminary objections. As such, no basis for reversal was apparent. There was no authority for an award of monetary damages for the patient's claimed violations of the Pennsylvania Constitution. The legality of the patient's commitment was not at issue, and the patient failed to allege that a judicial order nullifying her commitment existed. Without a judicial order, the PSP was without authority to expunge a mental health record. Also, the PSP was an indispensable party as to this particular claim for injunctive relief. The patient failed to demonstrate how the timing of the immunity defense affected the ultimate conclusion of immunity. Absent a showing of prejudice, there was no reversible error.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


 

1837 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


County Comm'rs Ass'n v. Dinges


No. 58 M.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


935 A.2d 926; 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 602


September 6, 2007, Argued 
November 8, 2007, Decided 
November 8, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, counties and an association of county commissioners, filed an action seeking a declaratory judgment to establish the proper formula for determining the salary of full-time district attorneys. Respondents, state officials and full-time district attorneys, filed preliminary objections to the petition.


OVERVIEW: A week after 2005 Pa. Laws 170, No. 44 (Act 44), setting the salaries for full-time district attorneys as well as judges, was enacted, the County Code was amended through 2005 Pa. Laws 266, No. 57 (Act 57). Act 44 was later repealed, but the Pennsylvania Supreme Court held that the repeal was unconstitutional as to judges' salaries. The court first held that it had jurisdiction over the district attorneys. They had a direct interest in their compensation and thus had to be made parties under 42 Pa.C.S. § 7540(a). Next, Act 44 and Act 57 provided irreconcilably different formulas for calculating compensation for full-time district attorneys. Because Act 57 had been enacted last, under 1 Pa.C.S. § 1935, it controlled. Furthermore, the subsequent repeal of Act 44 was of no moment. As of July 14, 2005, the date Act 57 was executed, 16 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1401, part of Act 57, governed compensation for district attorneys. Because Act 44's formula for determining compensation never applied after Act 57 became effective, district attorneys' salaries were not affected by the repeal of Act 44, and petitioners' assertion that the repeal might violate Pa. Const. art. III, § 27 failed.


OUTCOME: The court sustained respondents' preliminary objections and dismissed the petition.

Humphrey v. Dep't of Corr.


No. 640 M.D. 2006


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


939 A.2d 987; 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 692


August 10, 2007, Submitted 
September 26, 2007, Decided 
September 26, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Publication Ordered December 11, 2007.
Affirmed by, in part, Appeal denied by, in part Humphrey v. Commonwealth Dep't of Corr., 598 Pa. 191, 955 A.2d 348, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1297 (Pa., Aug. 18, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sued respondent Pennsylvania Department of Corrections (DOC), alleging its regulation violated state and federal laws and impermissibly restricted his constitutional rights. DOC filed preliminary objections, alleging the inmate (1) violated Pa.R.C.P. No. 1019(i) by not attaching the writings on which the claim was based to the petition; (2) failed to state a claim for relief; and (3) failed to exhaust administrative remedies.


OVERVIEW: The inmate alleged he filed a request under Pennsylvania's Right-to-Know Law (RTKL), 65 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 66.2-.9, with the Pennsylvania Department of State for blank Uniform Commercial Code (UCC) forms, and that DOC confiscated his UCC forms as contraband under DC-ADM 803-3. He filed a grievance; a grievance officer held his grievance failed to state a valid legal reason to possess the UCC forms. This petition followed. The court excused the inmate's failure to attach a copy of DC-ADM 803-3 to his brief, as required by Pa. R. Civ. P. 1019(i), due to his pro se status, his submission of the writing with his brief, and because DOC possessed the writing. The court held that as DC-ADM 803-3 had already been found constitutional, and as UCC forms were not "public records" under 65 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 66.1 of the RTKL, the inmate had not shown a clear right to relief; therefore, his mandamus claim failed. And as his petition failed to allege that he appealed the grievance officer's decision to DOC or even that he appealed any DOC order to the instant court, he failed to exhaust all administrative remedies.


OUTCOME: DOC's preliminary objections were overruled to the extent that they addressed the inmate's failure to conform to Pa.R.C.P No. 1019(i). The preliminary objections were sustained to the extent that they addressed the inmate's failure to state a claim on which mandamus relief could be granted and his failure to exhaust all administrative remedies. The inmate's petition was dismissed.

Schuylkill Twp. v. Pa. Builders Ass'n

COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


935 A.2d 575; 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 487


June 11, 2007, Argued 
September 6, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by Schuylkill Twp. v. Pa. Builders Ass'n, 596 Pa. 586, 947 A.2d 714, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 588 (2008)
Affirmed by Schuylkill Twp. v. Pa. Builders Ass'n, 2010 Pa. LEXIS 2387 (Pa., Oct. 19, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 05-09962. Common Pleas Court of the County of Chester. Judge Griffith, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an adjudication of the Secretary of Labor and Industry to invalidate a township ordinance because it conflicted with the Department of Labor and Industry's regulation establishing uniform construction standards in the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania. Appellee objectors has filed the challenge to the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The objectors were various builders associations and related entities. The ordinance required the installation of automatic sprinkler systems in most new construction in the township. Edwards v. County of Erie


No. 1223 C.D. 2006


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


932 A.2d 997; 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 477


February 6, 2007, Argued 
August 22, 2007, Decided 
August 22, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by, En banc Edwards v. County of Erie, 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 582 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Oct. 16, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 12113-2005. Common Pleas Court of the County of Erie. Bozza, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hoteliers sued appellees, a county, city, and convention center authority, alleging the Pennsylvania Third Class County Convention Center Authority Act, 16 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 2399.51-- 2399.73, and a county ordinance enacted thereunder violated the Due Process and Equal Protection Clauses of the U.S. and Pennsylvania Constitutions. The Erie County Common Pleas Court (Pennsylvania) upheld the Act and ordinance. The hoteliers appealed.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to the Act, the county created the convention center authority and enacted an ordinance imposing a room tax to be used, in part, to build a convention center. The authority used some of the tax revenues for planning and developing a hotel to be connected to the convention center. The trial court rejected the hoteliers' contention that the use of these tax revenues to develop that hotel imposed a disproportionate competitive burden on existing hotels. The trial court found from the evidence that the general increase in tourism resulting from the promotional marketing conducted with some of the room tax revenues would result in additional business for area hoteliers, such that, on balance, there was no palpably disproportionate burden on them to justify invalidating the tax. The appellate court held that these findings were amply supported by the record. It noted that the U.S. Supreme Court had rejected the proposition that tax-subsidized competition from a public entity that intruded on the profits of private enterprise was per se improper. The hoteliers' general objection to a publicly funded competitor did not justify the relief they sought.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.
