PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 3061 June Term 2004. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Dych, J.
Soc'y Created To Reduce Urban Blight v. Zoning Bd. of Adjustment, 2005 Phila. Ct. Com. Pl. LEXIS 478 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, an urban blight organization, challenged the order of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the decision of appellee, the Philadelphia Zoning Board of Adjustment, that granted a variance to appellee property owner in order for the property owner to erect five outdoor wrap around advertising signs on a vacant building. The property owner cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: The property owner's cross-appeal asserted that the city's sign ordinance was unconstitutional and sought to quash the organization's appeal based on a lack of standing. The court clarified that the organization had standing to appeal since retroactive application of the amendment to 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 13131.1, which excluded taxpayers who were not detrimentally harmed by a decision of a zoning board from the definition of aggrieved persons who had standing to challenge a zoning board decision, would be unconstitutional in that it would deprive the organization of its initial right of appeal. In addressing the variance granted to the property owner, the court held that the board's finding that the property owner established hardship was in error because the loss of rental income from disallowed outdoor advertising signs was not an unnecessary hardship. Further, the court found error with regard to the board's conclusion, and the trial court's affirmance, that the five signs were not contrary to the public interest since a planning commission member testified that the signs violated various sections of the city's sign ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court denied the property owner's motion to quash the organization's appeal and reversed the trial court's denial of the organization's appeal. The court also denied the property owner's cross-appeal.

 

1876 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


In re Nomination Paper of Rogers


No. 426 M.D. 2006


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


908 A.2d 948; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 513


August 30, 2006, Heard  
September 8, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Publication Ordered September 27, 2006.
Motions ruled upon by In re Nomination Paper of Rogers, 914 A.2d 457, 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 733 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Sept. 26, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: In re Nomination Paper of Rogers, 908 A.2d 942, 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 512 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A candidate filed suit alleging 25 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2911 of the Pennsylvania Election Code, which required a minor party candidate for statewide office to submit nomination papers containing signatures equal to two percent of the largest entire vote for any elected candidate in the last preceding state-wide election, violated the state and U.S. Constitutions. Objectors contended his challenge was barred by estoppel or laches.


OVERVIEW: Members of the candidate's party had unsuccessfully challenged the constitutionality of § 951 (25 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2911) of the Pennsylvania Election Code in federal court. The instant court held that the candidate was not estopped by the federal litigation, as he was not a party to it, nor was his suit barred by laches. The federal district and appellate courts, using the "balancing test" that applied in analyzing election statutes under the U.S. Constitution, had found that 25 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2911 was constitutional. The instant court agreed with their analysis, and reached the same conclusion under the "free and equal" clause of Pa. Const. art. I. § V. In determining whether election statutes violated Pa. Const. art. I. § V, a court was obliged to accord them a presumption of constitutionality, to be invalidated only for "gross abuse." The legislature, by enacting 25 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2911, intended to make ballot access by minor parties difficult, under the guise of maintaining an "uncluttered" ballot. This did not constitute a gross abuse of its power.


OUTCOME: The court held that the "two percent" requirement of 25 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2911 did not violate the U.S. Constitution or Pa. Const. art. I, § V.

 

1877 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Jubelirer v. Rendell


No. 490 M.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


904 A.2d 1030; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 440


April 5, 2006, Argued  
August 10, 2006, Decided  
August 10, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed in part and reversed in part by Jubelirer v. Rendell, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1332 (Pa., Aug. 19, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, the Pennsylvania Senate's President and the Pennsylvania House of Representative's Speaker, sued respondents, the Pennsylvania Governor and Treasurer and other state officials and agencies, seeking a declaration that Pa. Const. art. IV, § 16 barred the Governor from disapproving language in an appropriations bill. Respondents sought summary relief. The Treasurer and President filed preliminary objections; the latter moved to strike.


OVERVIEW: The Governor disapproved seven items in the appropriations bill pursuant to Pa. Const. art. IV, § 16. Petitioners took no steps to override the disapprovals but filed the instant suit. The court held that language in the answer filed by respondents concerning communications between staff in the Governor's Office and staff in the legislature during budget negotiations did not violate the Speech and Debate Clause of Pa. Const. art. II, § 15, because the answer did not describe or refer to any legislative speech or debate. Petitioners' allegation, that parts of respondents' memorandum of law were scandalous and impertinent, failed because Pa. R. Civ. P. 1028(a)(2) allowed preliminary objections to "pleadings" alleged to contain scandalous or impertinent matter, and a memorandum of law was not a pleading. Respondents were entitled to summary relief under Pa. R. App. P. 1532(b) because Pa. Const. art. IV, § 16 granted the Governor the power to disapprove the amount of the appropriation, the purpose for which the money was appropriated, or both. Pa. Const. art. IV, § 15 provided the sole mechanism by which the legislature could restore appropriations disapproved by the Governor.


OUTCOME: The court denied the President's preliminary objection and motion to strike, deemed the Treasurer's preliminary objection moot, and granted respondents' application for summary relief.

 

1878 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Oliviero v. Diven


No. 380 M.D. 2006


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


908 A.2d 933; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 555


August 3, 2006, Argued  
August 10, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Publication Ordered October 26, 2006.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, certain Republican voters (the voters) filed a motion for a preliminary injunction seeking to enjoin respondent election officials from certifying respondent write-in candidate (the candidate) as the Republican candidate in the general election for a state general assembly seat.


OVERVIEW: Prior to a state primary race, the voters filed an action to set aside the nomination petition of a candidate seeking nomination as the Republican candidate for a particular state legislature seat. The voters alleged that the signatures in the nomination petition were obtained fraudulently. The candidate then withdrew his nomination petition, and the court ordered election officials to not certify the candidate for the primary election. Thereafter, the candidate launched a write-in campaign and won the Republican nomination for the seat. The voters then filed the instant motion alleging, among other things, that permitting the candidate to run in the general election was contrary to the court's earlier order. The court denied the motion because it found that election officials had fully complied with the earlier directive and had not certified the candidate's name as the Republican candidate in the primary election. The order related only to the primary election and did not preclude officials from certifying the candidate as the Republican nominee in the general election. Having received the majority of the votes, the candidate rightfully earned the party's nomination for the seat.


OUTCOME: The court denied the voters' motion for a preliminary injunction.

Unified Sportsmen of Pa. v. Pa. Game Comm'n


No. 427 M.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


903 A.2d 117; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 397


June 9, 2006, Submitted on Briefs  
July 20, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Later proceeding at Unified Sportsmen of Pa. v. Pa. Game Comm'n, 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 277 (Pa. Commw. Ct., June 16, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a group of hunters and outdoorsmen, filed an action relating to deer hunting against respondents, the Pennsylvania Game Commission, the Pennsylvania Department of Conservation and Natural Resources (DCNR), and state officials, in which petitioners sought declaratory relief under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 7531-7541 and mandamus. Respondents filed preliminary objections.


OVERVIEW: The court first held that petitioners had standing under 34 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 322(c)(13), part of the Game Code, which clearly recognized the interests of sportsmen and protected an adequate opportunity to hunt and trap the Commonwealth's wildlife resources. Furthermore, petitioners had a substantial, direct, and immediate interest because they alleged that the Commission's policies and practices interfered with their ability to hunt by causing a depletion of the deer population. Next, based on the ambiguous nature of petitioners' pleading, it was not possible to discern a legal theory to support the relief requested. It appeared, however, that petitioners could state a claim for relief based on the Commission's alleged violations of its duties under § 322(a), (c)(11)-(13): to protect and preserve game, serve the interests of sportsmen, and provide an adequate opportunity to hunt and trap. Accordingly, the court would not dismiss the complaint, but would allow petitioners to file an amended complaint under Pa. R. Civ. P. 1033. Finally, the DCNR, its secretary, and the Governor were not proper parties. There was no actual controversy between them and petitioners.


OUTCOME: The court overruled the preliminary objection to standing. It sustained the preliminary objection in the nature of a demurrer and gave petitioners leave to amend their complaint. It sustained the preliminary objection to improper parties and dismissed the DCNR, its secretary, and the Governor from the action.

In re Penn-Delco Sch. Dist.


Nos. 2245 C.D. 2005, 2246 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


903 A.2d 600; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 389


June 9, 2006, Submitted  
July 14, 2006, Decided  
July 14, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by In re Appeal of Penn-Delco Sch. Dist., 2007 Pa. LEXIS 854 (Pa., Apr. 18, 2007)
Appeal denied by In re Appeal of Penn-Delco Sch. Dist., 2007 Pa. LEXIS 856 (Pa., Apr. 18, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 03-12685 and 03-12687. Delaware. Proud, J.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After appellant taxpayer purchased the subject properties, appellee school district filed two tax assessment appeals alleging that the properties were undervalued. The Delaware County Board of Assessment denied the appeals. The school district appealed to the trial court. The Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania) sustained the school district's appeal and increased the assessments. The taxpayer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer alleged that the trial court improperly increased the fair market value of the subject property. Among other things, the appellate court concluded that any lack of notice of the hearing was harmless as it did not prejudice the taxpayer, who had a fully opportunity to be heard. The appellate court further concluded that the increased assessment did not constitute an illegal spot reassessment in violation of the uniformity clause of Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1. The school district was not a body with power essential to the assessment process and thus, its use of a statutory appeal process did not constitute deliberate, purposeful discrimination in violation of the uniformity clause. The appellate court also found that the admission of the appraiser's supplemental report was proper since the report used the same evaluation criteria and methodology as the initial report, and the trial court held the record open to provide the taxpayer an opportunity to present evidence in response to the report. Finally, the appellate court disagreed that the trial court's decision was contrary to the evidence, noting that the taxpayer had stipulated to the appraiser's qualifications.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

1881 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Durkee Lumber Co., Inc. v. Dep't of Conservation & Natural Res.


No. 2321 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


903 A.2d 593; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 386


June 5, 2006, Argued  
July 13, 2006 Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied August 24, 2006.



DISPOSITION:  [**1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After respondent Department of Conservation and Natural Resources (Pennsylvania) rejected a bid by petitioner protestant on the grounds that the protestant had a record of noncompliance in state forest timber sales, the protestant filed a bid protest under 62 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1711.1. The Secretary of the Department denied the bid protest and affirmed the Department's decision to reject the bid. The protestant appealed.


OVERVIEW: After reviewing the parties' submissions, the Secretary found that no material dispute regarding the facts existed to necessitate the holding of a hearing on the bid protest. The protestant argued that the Secretary should have provided it with a hearing. The court stated that under § 1711.1(e), the Secretary had sole discretion to decide whether a hearing was necessary to decide a bid protest. Here, none of the submissions to the Secretary raised any questions of material fact, and the protestant had failed to dispute the contents of the documents despite having been given the opportunity to comment on them. When the protestant failed to dispute the contents of the documents, the Secretary properly assumed that the protestant did not dispute their factual accuracy and that a hearing was unnecessary. This was especially evident where the protestant never challenged its inclusion on the Contractor Responsibility Program list of contractors who had defaulted. Next, because a disappointed bidder on a government contract had no right to have a contract awarded to it, due process was not violated by the failure of the Secretary to hold a hearing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Secretary's decision.

Lynnebrook & Woodbrook Assocs. v. Borough of Millersville


No. 2281 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


911 A.2d 196; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 554


June 9, 2006, Argued  
July 12, 2006, Decided  
July 12, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Publication Ordered October 24, 2006.
Appeal granted by, in part, Appeal denied by, in part Lynnebrook & Woodbrook Assocs. v. Borough of Millersville, 592 Pa. 606, 927 A.2d 198, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 1311 (2007)
Reversed by, Remanded by Lynnebrook & Woodbrook Assocs. v. Borough of Millersville, 600 Pa. 108, 963 A.2d 1261, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 2263 (Pa., Dec. 17, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. CI-05-06102. Common Pleas Court of the County of Lancaster. Allison, J.
Lynnebrook & Woodbrook Assocs. v. Borough of Millersville, 903 A.2d 145, 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 423 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer filed a declaratory judgment action against appellee borough, alleging the borough's ordinance authorizing taxes on residential leases violated § 2 (53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 6902(1)) of the Pennsylvania Local Tax Enabling Act and requesting a refund of taxes paid. The Lancaster County Common Pleas Court (Pennsylvania) granted the borough's motion for judgment on the pleadings. The taxpayer appealed.


OVERVIEW: Millersville, Pa., Ordinance § 603 imposed a tax on residential leases The taxpayer argued the ordinance ran afoul of 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 6902(1), which prohibited taxing all "leases." The appellate court disagreed. A tax upon "leases" in general was not prohibited, only leases that were made in the manner as described by § 6902(1), which did not include the residential leases referred to in the ordinance. The taxpayer further alleged that the ordinance violated Pennsylvania's Uniformity Clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1, because it did not uniformly apply to all leases, but only residential to lease agreements, and that the borough did not provide any explanation for making the distinction. However, it was the taxpayer who had the initial burden of establishing that a reasonable distinction did not exist, and it did not plead any facts in support of its claim. Therefore, its constitutional challenge to the ordinance failed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

MSG Group, Inc. v. Dep't of Pub. Welfare


No. 2497 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


902 A.2d 613; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 350


June 5, 2006, Argued  
July 7, 2006, Decided  
July 7, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. Bid Protest No. 2005-BP-2. State Agency Department of Public Welfare.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Pennsylvania Department of Public Welfare (DPW) denied petitioner vendor's bid protest, finding it was untimely under 62 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1711.1(b) of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania Procurement Code. The DPW's Bureau of Hearings and Appeals (BHA) denied the vendor's appeal. The vendor sought judicial review.


OVERVIEW: DPW posted a request for quotes (RFQ) on the Pennsylvania Department of General Services (DGS) website, seeking bids on a service contract. Before it did so, the vendor had written to BHA, expressing interest in performing that work. Because the vendor failed to update the vendor master list to reflect its new address, it did not receive the bid packet sent to it by DPW, which, after the packet was returned as undeliverable, tried, without success, to determine the vendor's new address. The vendor argued that its due process rights were violated because the lack of notice denied it the opportunity to bid. The court disagreed. DPW's failure to unearth the vendor's correct address did not estop DPW from relying on the statute of limitations to defend against the bid protest, as the burden to provide the address was on the vendor, not DPW. DPW complied with the notice requirements 62 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 512(c)(1) by posting the RFQ and the bidding instructions on DGS's website. And under 62 Pa. Cons. Stat. 1711.1(l), the vendor could not avail itself of the right to proceed as a taxpayer in the court's original jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: DPW's final determination was affirmed.

Phila. Hous. Auth. v. AFSCME, Dist. Council 33, Local 934


No. 2405 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


900 A.2d 1043; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 330


November 16, 2005, Argued  
June 20, 2006, Decided  
June 20, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Vacated by, Remanded by Phila. Hous. Auth. v. AFSCME, Dist. Council 33, Local 934, 596 Pa. 207, 941 A.2d 1257, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 25 (2008)
Adhered to, On remand at Phila. Hous. Auth. v. Am. Fed'n. of State, County & Mun. Emples., Dist. Council 33, Local 934, 956 A.2d 477, 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 417 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 002280, August Term 2004. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Carrafiello, J.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant government employer sued appellee labor union, seeking to vacate an arbitration award that reinstated an employee who had been terminated for sexually harassing a co-worker. The Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia, Pennsylvania, denied the employer's petition; it appealed.


OVERVIEW: A co-worker alleged the employee hugged her, rubbed his penis against her buttocks, and said he wanted to have sex with her. The employer fired the employee; the union filed a grievance alleging that the employer violated the collective bargaining agreement (CBA) by discharging the employee without just cause. The CBA did not define the term "just cause." The arbitrator found that the employee sexually harassed the co-worker, but should not have been fired because (1) management condoned horseplay of a sexual nature, and (2) he stopped his harassment after being warned by a supervisor. The employer argued that the arbitration award was not rationally derived from the CBA because it deprived it of the ability to terminate an employee whose misconduct interfered with its ability to discharge one of its essential functions -- meeting its legal obligation to protect its employees from sexual harassment on the job. The appellate court agreed. The employee's conduct was so egregious that if the employer was unable to curtail it, it risked relinquishing control of the orderly functioning of its operations. It was not rational to conclude that the employer bargained for such a result.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

Piunti v. DOL & Indus., Unemployment Comp Bd. of Review


No. 482 M.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


900 A.2d 1017; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 320; Unemployment Ins. Rep. (CCH) P12,501


April 5, 2006, Argued  
June 13, 2006, Decided  
June 13, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Summary judgment denied by Piunti v. DOL & Indus., Unemployment Comp. Bd. of Review, 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 583 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Aug. 9, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner licensed attorneys filed an amended petition for review of 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 774 in the nature of a complaint for declaratory judgment in the trial court (Pennsylvania) against respondent Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Board of Review. The Board filed preliminary objections.


OVERVIEW: The attorneys challenged the constitutionality of § 214 (43 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 774) of the Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Law, 1936 Pa. Laws 2897, 43 Pa. Stat. Ann §§ 751-914. The trial court struck the allegation that § 702 (43 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 862) of the Law had been amended. The attorneys had standing to challenge 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 774 as they had been negatively impacted in a direct fashion by it. The attorneys' interest was substantial as the general public did not have a license to practice law. Their interest was direct and immediate as § 774 authorized non-attorneys to practice law in competition with them. The attorneys also had standing under the exception for issues that might otherwise go unchallenged. The attorneys also had standing as individuals who had taken an oath and pledged to defend Pennsylvania's Constitution. Finally, § 774 violated Pa. Const. art. V, § 10 as § 10 gave the state's highest court the exclusive jurisdiction over matters relating to the practice of law. Whether special circumstances existed that justified the representation of corporations in unemployment proceedings by non-attorneys was for the state's highest court to decide.


OUTCOME: The Board's first preliminary objection to the amended petition was granted and the references to § 702 (43 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 862) as having been amended were stricken. The Board's remaining two preliminary objections were overruled.

Warner v. Lawrence


No. 1929 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


900 A.2d 980; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 283


March 1, 2006, Argued  
June 2, 2006, Decided  
June 2, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 2514, July Term 2002. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Lachman, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a minor by his parent and natural guardian, challenged an order of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), dismissing his negligence claims. The issue was whether the Charter School Law, 24 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 17-1701-A - 17-1751-A, granted the same immunity that political subdivisions and local agencies enjoyed under the Political Subdivision Tort Claims Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 8541- 8542.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was injured while using his locker in a hallway while other students were playing in the hallway. Appellant contended that the school was negligent because it had failed to post hall monitors in the hallway. The lower court granted the school's motion to dismiss based on its immunity and the instant appeal followed. The court held that because the school, a Charter School, was so closely intertwined with the local school board, and because charter schools were exempt from certain regulatory requirements and were subject to statutes applicable to public schools, the lower court had properly determined that charter schools were immune from tort liability as political subdivisions or local agencies. In addition, appellant had raised a constitutional challenge, contending that the open courts provisions of Pa. Const. Art. I, § 11 required that he be allowed to sue, which contention the court rejected, observing that the constitutional provision also provided for sovereign immunity and legislative regulation of suits against the Commonwealth. In addition, because the constitutional challenge was actually a facial challenge, notice to the Attorney General had been required.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court.

Commonwealth v. Haagensen


No. 789 C.D. 2005, No. 790 C.D. 2005, No. 791 C.D. 2005, No. 792 C.D. 2005, No. 793 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


900 A.2d 468; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 287


May 9, 2006, Argued  
June 2, 2006, Decided  
June 2, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Haagensen v. Pa. State Police, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 24455 (W.D. Pa., Mar. 25, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 602, 603, 604, 605 and 608 of 2002, SA. Common Pleas Court of the County of Lawrence. Judge Motto, President Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment by the Common Pleas Court of the County of Lawrence (Pennsylvania) that found her guilty of interference with the lawful taking of wildlife, in violation of 34 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2302(a.1)(2), (7) of the Hunter Harassment Statute; defendant claimed, inter alia, that the Commonwealth did not establish that she violated the statute.


OVERVIEW: Defendant neither hunted nor permitted hunting on her property, and posted it each year. Nevertheless, she often observed hunters armed with rifles on her property. In December of 2001, defendant confronted various hunters screaming and yelling that they should get out because they were either trespassing on her property or illegally hunting. Within the span of one week, defendant received five citations for hunter harassment. The appellate court found that the trial court completely disregarded testimony relating to the legality of the hunting or the trespass on defendant's property. Defendant believed, either rightly or wrongly, that the hunters were engaging in the unlawful taking of wildlife, and merely sought to warn them not to trespass on her property or hunt illegally near her farm. There was nothing in the record to indicate that defendant ever knowingly interfered with lawful hunting. Because the Commonwealth's evidence failed to support a finding that defendant acted with the intent to interfere with the lawful taking of wildlife, the trial court erred in finding her guilty of violating § 2302(a.1)(2), (7).


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

GLS Capital, Inc. v. Davis


No. 235 C.D. 2005, No. 236 C.D. 2005, No. 237 C.D. 2005, No. 238 C.D. 2005, No. 239 C.D. 2005, No. 240 C.D. 2005, No. 241 C.D. 2005, No. 243 C.D. 2005, No. 244 C.D. 2005, No. 245 C.D. 2005, No. 246 C.D. 2005, No. 248 C.D. 2005, No. 249 C.D. 2005, No. 250 C.D. 2005, No. 251 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


899 A.2d 371; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 250


February 1, 2006, Argued  
May 15, 2006, Decided  
May 15, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. GD99-019109, GD00-17312, GD01-839, GD01-19148, GD97-14606, GD02-13080, GD00-13246, GD97-8409, GD98-17795, GD00-4576, GD00-5660, GD00-20549, GD01-3020, GD01-13043, GD02-21941. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Judge Horgos, J.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Allegheny County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania) granted appellee corporation's petition for rule to show cause effectively permitting the corporation to sell properties, whose owners had become delinquent in their county tax obligations, free and clear of all tax and municipal claims, liens, mortgages, charges, and estates encumbering the properties. Appellants, a school district, a borough, and a property owner, appealed.


OVERVIEW: A county entered into an agreement with the corporation through which the county assigned to the corporation many tax liens. The transfer of rights included all rights that the county had to collect and enforce such tax liens. Pursuant to the agreements, the corporation sought to obtain payments of the delinquent taxes; however, after failing to do so, it initiated proceedings against the properties. No owners contested the claims, and the court entered judgment for the corporation. After failing to obtain the judgment amounts, the corporation initiated an action to sell the properties at the "upset price" through sheriff's sales, which involved a bid that minimally covers costs, expenses, and all taxes and municipal claims. No party bid on the properties. The failure to received bids triggered the provision of so-called "second sales" of the properties to the highest bidder unencumbered by any lien, judgment, or other property interest. The trial court granted the corporation's petition for a rule to show cause allowing it to make such sales. On appeal, the appellate court found the petition was correctly granted because the law authorized the corporation to make such sales.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

Southeastern Chester County Refuse Auth. v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 1722 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


898 A.2d 680; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 216


April 5, 2006, Argued  
May 5, 2006, Decided  
May 5, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Land Use Appeal of Southeastern Chester County Refuse Auth. v. Bd. of Supervisors (In re Land Use Appeal of Southeastern Chester County Refuse Auth.), 2006 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 92 (2006)
Appeal denied by Southeastern Chester County Refuse Auth. v. Zoning Hearing Bd., 2007 Pa. LEXIS 837 (Pa., Apr. 17, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] No. 05-01041. Common Pleas Court of the County of Chester. Judge Griffith, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant applicant sued appellees, a township and its zoning hearing board, challenging the denial of its variance request and contending that sections of the township's zoning ordinance establishing perimeter setbacks and maximum height requirements were unconstitutional. The Chester County Common Pleas Court (Pennsylvania) affirmed the board's decision; the applicant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The applicant operated a landfill that did not conform to the zoning ordinances' perimeter setback requirement of 200 feet and the maximum height requirement of 40 feet. The board found the ordinances valid and denied the variance request based on evidence that the applicant's proposed expansion would increase truck traffic and adversely affect the environment. The appellate court held that as the applicant made both a variance request and a validity challenge in a single application and presented evidence relating to both in its case-in-chief, 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 10908(9)'s 100-day requirement for completing a hearing did not apply and the variance request could not be "deemed approved." The setback and height requirements were not preempted by the Pennsylvania Solid Waste Management Act. Substantial evidence supported the board's conclusions. The ordinances were not unconstitutional because they did not result in the arbitrary confiscation of the applicant's property. In view of credible testimony that the applicant could operate the landfill at a profit while complying with the setback and height requirements, it failed to show that the ordinances imposed unnecessary hardship.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.


 

1890 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Stilp v. Commonwealth


No. 513 M.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


898 A.2d 36; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 201


April 5, 2006, Submitted  
April 24, 2006, Decided  
April 24, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Stilp v. Commonwealth, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2958 (Pa., Dec. 27, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer filed an amended petition for review under the original jurisdiction of the trial court (Pennsylvania) seeking a declaratory judgment that respondent Pennsylvania Auditor General failed to perform an audit of the Pennsylvania general assembly in violation of his duties. Respondents, including the general assembly and the Pennsylvania Legislative Audit Advisory Commission, filed amended preliminary objections.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer averred that because of Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 10, the general assembly insulated itself from being independently audited and that the Auditor General's refusal to conduct such audits was based on an erroneous legal interpretation that he had no such duty. The trial court held that the taxpayer had standing to bring the action. Section 10 did not give any inherent power to the Auditor General to audit the general assembly; there was no other constitutional provision authorizing such an audit. The Pennsylvania Fiscal Code did not provide any such authority. If there was any doubt as to whether 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 402 authorized the Auditor General to audit the general assembly, that was removed when 1970 Pa. Laws 442 (Act 151), as amended, 71 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 1189.1-1189.2, was enacted, detailing how the audits of affairs of the general assembly were to be conducted. Under Act 151, that power was given to the Commission, which hired an accounting firm to audit the accounts. Finally, the separation of powers doctrine prohibited such an audit. There was absolutely no support provided anywhere authorizing the Auditor General to conduct an audit of the general assembly.


OUTCOME: The preliminary objections of respondents were sustained. The taxpayer's petition for review was dismissed.

Potratz v. Dep't of Envtl. Prot.

CASE SUMMARY:

Concentric Network Corp. v. Commonwealth


No. 290 F.R. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


897 A.2d 6; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 185


December 14, 2005, Argued  
April 13, 2006, Decided  
April 13, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Concentric Network Corp. v. Commonwealth, 922 A.2d 883, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 1155 (Pa., May 31, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 0207354 and 0207356. Board of Finance and Revenue.
Concentric Network Corp. v. Commonwealth, 877 A.2d 542, 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 322 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2005)



DISPOSITION:  The court denied and dismissed the taxpayer's exceptions. Pursuant to the parties' stipulations of fact, the taxpayer was entitled to receive a refund certain sales and use taxes, but all other claims for refund were denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, denied appellant taxpayer, an Internet service provider, a refund of sales and use taxes paid on purchases of equipment and of access to wirelines. The taxpayer appealed. The reviewing court affirmed the Commonwealth's determination; the taxpayer filed exceptions.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer bought equipment to route customer data to its intended destination and to analyze incoming data. It did not own its own wirelines, but bought services from telecommunications carriers. The court held that as the operations performed by the equipment did not fall within the definition of "manufacturing" in the Pennsylvania Tax Reform Code of 1971, 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 7101-10004, the equipment purchases were taxable. That equipment purchases by cable companies and facilities-based Internet service providers were not taxed did not violate the Uniformity Clause of the Pennsylvania Constitution (Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1), because such entities were regulated to a different degree than non-facilities-based Internet service providers like the taxpayer. As the taxpayer was not a "public utility" with regards to its provision of Internet services, it could not claim a refund under the theory that it provided public utility Internet services. Under 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7201(rr)(3)(B), its purchase of wireline access to transport its customers' data to and from the Internet was taxable. The taxes were not proscribed by the federal Internet Tax Freedom Act, 47 U.S.C.S. § 151.


OUTCOME: The court denied and dismissed the taxpayer's exceptions. Pursuant to the parties' stipulations of fact, the taxpayer was entitled to receive a refund certain sales and use taxes, but all other claims for refund were denied.

Telvil Constr. Corp. v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 207 C.D. 2005, No. 330 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


896 A.2d 651; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 131


September 9, 2005, Submitted  
March 16, 2006, Decided  
March 16, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied May 15, 2006.
Reargument denied by Telvil Constr. Corp. v. Zoning Hearing Bd., 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 251 (Pa. Commw. Ct., May 15, 2006)
Related proceeding at Telvil Corp. v. Zoning Hearing Bd., 2009 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 403 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 04-02157. Common Pleas Court of the County of Chester. Shenkin, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township and appellee corporation cross-appealed the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), which reversed the decision of the township's zoning hearing board denying relief, and gave specific direction to the township board of supervisors concerning its pending review of the corporation's land development plan. The township challenged the trial court's interpretation of Valenti.


OVERVIEW: The corporation asserted the unconstitutionality of the township's 1991 ordinance. The appellate court concluded that the trial court erred in its application of Valenti and of 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 10508(4). Nothing in the statutory language suggested that a landowner was entitled to enjoy the selective application of ordinance changes beneficial to its pending application while ignoring adverse changes in favor of an old ordinance. Allowing a hybrid application of the least restrictive provisions of old and new ordinances simply because of the timing of a land development application served no legitimate purpose. In addition, the corporation's evidence in support of the invalidity of the township's 1991 ordinance consisted entirely of testimony by its engineer, who opined that neither sand mound sewage systems nor drip irrigation systems required a lot size of 100,000 square feet. The corporation's evidence did not establish that the larger lot requirement produced the exclusionary effect prohibited by the state supreme court. Nor did this evidence establish that the lot size requirement was unreasonable, arbitrary or not sufficiently related to proper zoning purposes.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court was reversed.

COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


900 A.2d 430; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 318

March 10, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Gleeson v. State Bd. of Med., 2007 Pa. LEXIS 363 (Pa., Feb. 21, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] MD-037565. State Board of Medicine.



DISPOSITION: Order of the State Board of Medicine in the above-captioned matter affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State Board of Medicine (Pennsylvania) reversed the decision of its hearing examiner and ordered that petitioner licensee be given a public reprimand, pay a civil penalty, and complete a course in medical ethics for knowingly aiding, assisting, procuring, or advising an unlicensed person to practice medicine in Pennsylvania. The licensee petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The licensee held a license to practice medicine in Pennsylvania. He owned a medical facility in Pennsylvania. He traveled to Michigan to obtain additional training after reviewing a doctor's detailed website. That doctor then traveled to Pennsylvania to be present as the licensee performed the surgical procedure. A news crew videotaped the licensee performing the procedure. The doctor was there, was scrubbed, and was wearing a surgical gown. The doctor was also holding a surgical device and was shown on the videotape performing an invasive procedure. However, the doctor was not licensed to practice medicine in Pennsylvania. A hearing examiner found that the licensee should not be disciplined for knowingly allowing the doctor to practice unlicensed medicine in Pennsylvania. The state medical board reversed and ordered that the licensee be disciplined. On further review, the trial court found that the licensee was subject to discipline under the Medical Practice Act of 1985, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 63, § 422.41(7) because he knowingly allowed an unlicensed doctor to practice medicine in Pennsylvania and the doctor was acting as more than a consultant at the time of the infraction.


OUTCOME: The trial court affirmed the state medical board's order.

Pa. Bankers Ass'n v. Pa. Dep't of Banking


No. 42 M.D. 2005, No. 98 M.D. 2005, No. 157 C.D. 2005, No. 158 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


893 A.2d 864; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 91


November 16, 2005, Argued  
March 1, 2006, Decided  
March 1, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied March 31, 2006.
Appeal granted by, Motion granted by Pa. Bankers Ass'n v. Pa. Dep't of Banking & Trumark Fin. Credit Union, 591 Pa. 729, 920 A.2d 835, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 763 (2007)
Appeal dismissed by Pa. Bankers Ass'n v. Pa. Dep't of Banking, 948 A.2d 790, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 908 (Pa., 2008)
Reversed by, Remanded by Pa. Bankers Ass'n v. Pa. Dep't of Banking, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1544 (Pa., Sept. 24, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. App-2003-01, App-2003-02, and App-2004-01. Department of Banking.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent credit unions notified respondent Pennsylvania Department of Banking of their intent to expand their fields of membership to geography-based community credit unions under 17 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 501(e)(2). The Department allowed petitioner banks to intervene in order to oppose the request. Later, the Department dismissed the banks as intervenors and permitted conversion of membership fields for two credit unions. The banks sought review.


OVERVIEW: The banks argued they would be harmed if tax-exempt credit unions could offer bank-like services in regions of Pennsylvania without the same regulatory scrutiny that was applied to banks. The Department, after allowing the banks to intervene, dismissed them for failing to prove standing during the hearings. The banks claimed that under 1 Pa. Code § 35.31, intervenor status, once granted, could not be revoked and no further proof of standing could be compelled. The court held that § 35.31 did not support this claim. Without an express statutory direction, a financial institution that was not governed by the Pennsylvania Credit Union Code had to satisfy traditional tests for standing to challenge administrative actions under the Code. As the banks and credit unions were regulated by separate statutes, the banks, in seeking to intervene in the credit union administrative proceedings, could proceed to a hearing on averments of competitive injury, but under Pa. Code § 35.28(a), they had to prove some possible harm. There was no proof of possible harm because the banks did not establish that they had an interest that would be directly affected by the Department's conversion orders.


OUTCOME: The orders dismissing the banks from further participation in the administrative proceedings and revoking their status as intervenors were affirmed.

In re Condemnation of 23.015 Acres


No. 2583 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


895 A.2d 76; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 90


November 15, 2005, Argued  
February 28, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by In re Condemnation of 23.015 Acres, 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 197 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Apr. 21, 2006)
Appeal denied by Condemnation of 23.015 Acres v. Showalter, 590 Pa. 670, 912 A.2d 839, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2299 (Pa., Nov. 28, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 00-07052-25-6. Common Pleas Court of the County of Bucks. Judge Goldberg, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury awarded appellant landowners $ 325,000 for property taken through condemnation by appellee school district. The landowners challenged a judgment of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Bucks (Pennsylvania) denying their motion for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: The landowners owned property adjacent to a school. When negotiations concerning the purchase of the property between the landowners and the school district broke down, the school district acquired the properly by condemnation, and the jury awarded the landowners $ 325,000 for the property. On appeal, the court held that the trial court erred by admitting evidence of the parties' pre-condemnation negotiations for the sale of the property, which included the school district's offers of $ 280,000 and $ 260,000, because such evidence was prohibited by Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 26, § 1-705(2)(i). The two written communications indicated that the school district offered the property owners $ 280,000 and $ 260,000 as part of a proposed settlement in advance of condemnation proceedings. The trial court also erred by not permitting the landowners' expert appraiser to value the property using the subdivision development method. The expert valued the property as a whole but arrived at the ultimate value of $ 690,000 by starting with the values of individual lots and adding and subtracting other values and expenses, calculated over the projected time period needed to subdivide and sell the lots.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying the landowners a new trial was reversed and the case was remanded for a new trial.

Meggett v. Pa. Dep't of Corr.


No. 68 M.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


892 A.2d 872; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 66


August 12, 2005, Submitted  
February 13, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.
Meggett v. Pa. Dep't of Corr., 856 A.2d 277, 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 623 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff inmate sought summary judgment under Pa. R. App. P. 1532 in his action alleging a regulation of defendants Pennsylvania Department of Corrections and Board of Probation and Parole, regarding the length and styling of an inmate's hair violated his right to freedom of religion and equal protection of the laws. Defendants filed a cross-motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: After defendants required the inmate to cut off his dreadlocks, he filed suit, alleging that the hair regulation, which limited "Afro styles" to four inches, violated his constitutional rights. The court granted defendants summary judgment on the inmate's freedom of religion claim because defendants' infringement on defendant's rights was permissible, as it advanced a valid penological objective and did so reasonably. A security officer explained in an affidavit that limiting hair length eliminated the use of long hair to conceal and move contraband, aided staff in the identification of inmates, and advanced inmate hygiene, and the inmate offered no evidence in rebuttal. The court also granted defendants summary judgment on the inmate's claim that the regulation discriminated against African-Americans because the "Afro style" could be worn by a person of any race. The regulation permitted a variety of hairstyles that did not fall below the collar and clarified that to meet that standard, Afro styles could not exceed four inches, based on differences in hair texture. The standards achieved the same end in keeping hair fairly, but not unreasonably, short for all inmates.


OUTCOME: The inmate's motion for summary relief was denied. Defendants' cross-motion was granted.

Stackhouse v. Commonwealth


No. 1244 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


892 A.2d 54; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 60


December 13, 2005, Argued  
February 8, 2006, Decided  
February 8, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Stackhouse v. Commonwealth, 588 Pa. 760, 903 A.2d 539, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1323 (Pa., July 26, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 2784 S 2000. Common Pleas Court of the County of Dauphin. Evans, J.
Stackhouse v. Pa. State Police, 574 Pa. 558, 832 A.2d 1004, 2003 Pa. LEXIS 1776 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a state employee, appealed an order from the Common Pleas Court of the County of Dauphin (Pennsylvania), which sustained the preliminary objections of defendants, the Pennsylvania State Police and individual state officials, on the basis of sovereign immunity and dismissed a complaint alleging invasion of privacy.


OVERVIEW: The employee alleged that her personal affairs were improperly investigated in connection with her application for a promotion. She sought declaratory and injunctive relief preventing the use of information gathered as a result of the investigation, as well as preventing any further investigation until such time as appropriate training, guidelines, policy, restrictions, and procedures were developed. She also sought monetary damages from an official for failing to take corrective action. The court held that any duty on the part of the official to supervise the investigation was a duty within the scope of his employment, as to which he was absolutely immune from liability in accordance with 1 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2310. Immunity also applied with respect to training and other corrective action because sovereign immunity precluded mandatory injunctions to compel affirmative action by the state. Declaratory relief regarding matters barred by immunity would be improper. The trial court erred, however, in dismissing the request for injunctive relief restraining the use of the investigative report; this request was not barred by sovereign immunity because it sought to restrain conduct.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the portion of the complaint that sought injunctive relief restraining the use of the investigative report and remanded for further proceedings. The court affirmed the dismissal order in all other respects.

Sims v. Berks County Bd. of Assessment Appeals


No. 1664 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


891 A.2d 816; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 30


December 15, 2005, Submitted  
February 3, 2006, Decided  
February 3, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Sims v. Berks County Bd. of Assessment Appeals, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1999 (Pa., Oct. 18, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed his assessment appeal challenging a decision by appellee county board of assessment appeals to apply the established predetermined ratio, instead of the common level ratio, to calculate the assessment value of the taxpayer's property.


OVERVIEW: The board rejected the taxpayer's appeal of an assessment on his real property, finding the assessment value was equal to the property's fair market value. The board determined that the county's predetermined ratio (PR) under § 1.1 of the General County Assessment Law (GCAL), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 5342.1, was 100 percent, so application of the PR to the fair market value did not establish a different property value for assessment purposes. The trial court rejected the taxpayer's argument that the common level ratio (CLR) of 86.3 percent, rather than the PR of 100 percent, should have been applied. The taxpayer raised the same argument on appeal. The court declined the taxpayer's invitation to reject the established Park Terrace methodology for determining whether a CLR varied by more than 15 percent from the PR and it found that, since the CLR of 86.3 percent did not vary by more than 15 percent from the PR of 100 percent, the PR applied. Since the county's CLR fell within the 85 to 115 percent window of the county's 100 percent PR, § 9(a.1) of the GCAL, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 5350(a.1), required the board to use the PR to determine the assessment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision made by the board.

Twp. of S. Whitehall v. Karoly


No. 992 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


891 A.2d 780; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 25


December 15, 2005, Argued  
January 27, 2006, Decided  
January 27, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Twp. of S. Whitehall v. Karoly, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1693 (Pa., Aug. 31, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 2002-C-3043. State Agency, Common Pleas Court of the County of Lehigh. Judge Lavelle, S.J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner appealed the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee township's petition for counsel fees and costs in the amount of $ 28,493. The underlying litigation involved the enforcement of a township zoning ordinance against the property owner.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the property owner raised numerous issues. The appellate court concluded that 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 10617.2 and South Whitehall Township, Pa., Zoning Ordinance § 12.9(b)(1) did not deny equal protection of the law or fail to provide due process. Thus, both provisions were constitutional. The rational basis test requirements were met where: (1) enforcement of a zoning ordinance to ensure that the health, safety, morals, and general welfare of the community were protected was a legitimate state interest, and (2) the provisions were designed to promote the early resolution of zoning violations. In addition, the property owner was not the prevailing party because the township was successful on three of the four counts. Also, the fee award was proper commencing with the enforcement notice. Finally, the property owner presented no evidence to challenge the accuracy and reasonableness of the hours charged. Thus, he waived his right to challenge the reasonableness of the fees charged. Because the counts were tried in a single proceeding, it was the property owner's burden to establish a basis for segregating the hours spent on the successful and unsuccessful counts.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court was affirmed.

Jones v. City of Philadelphia


No. 795 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


890 A.2d 1188; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 23


June 8, 2005, Argued  
January 25, 2006, Decided  
January 25, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Jones v. City of Phila., 589 Pa. 741, 909 A.2d 1291, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2066 (Pa., Oct. 25, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. October Term 2001 No. 3641. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia, Judge Rau, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted appellant city's petition for an interlocutory appeal with regard to its challenge of the order of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which denied its motion for summary judgment in a suit brought by appellee, an arrestee, against the city and appellees, 11 police officers. The arrestee sought money damages for personal injuries incurred from alleged excessive force used by the officers.


OVERVIEW: The trial court's denial of the city's motion for summary judgment concluded that the city could be liable if the State of Pennsylvania courts created a cause of action under Pa. Const. art. I, § 8 and that it was beyond the legislature's power to immunize government defendants from that judicially created cause of action. The trial court also determined that the city was not immune from the suit because the legislature did not have the power under the constitution to take away a state constitutional right. The city maintained on appeal that it was immune from the suit alleging excessive force under Pennsylvania's Political Subdivision Tort Claims Act. 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8541. The court acknowledged that the Pennsylvania Constitution did afford greater protection than the Fourth Amendment under certain circumstances but that such greater protection did not extend to where the government uses excessive force during a seizure. The court noted that the arrestee had an alternative remedy of pursuing such as a claim under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 but that creating a new cause of action under Pa. Const. art. I, § 8 for excessive force was not supported by caselaw.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court order and granted summary judgment in favor of the city.

 

1905 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Kelley v. State Emples. Ret. Bd.


850 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


890 A.2d 1173; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 22


December 14, 2005, Argued  
January 24, 2006, Decided  
January 24, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by Kelley v. State Emples. Ret. Bd., 591 Pa. 675, 916 A.2d 1104, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2292 (2006)
Affirmed in part and reversed in part by Kelley v. State Emples. Ret. Bd., 2007 Pa. LEXIS 1988 (Pa., Sept. 26, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 2002-02. State Employees' Retirement Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant, a retiree, petitioned for review of a decision of the Pennsylvania State Employees' Retirement Board (SERB), which denied the retiree's request to convert his 14.5250 years of service credit earned as a Pennsylvania senator from Class A to either Class D-4 or Class AA.


OVERVIEW: The retiree served as a state senator from 1974 until 1988. The retiree later served as a judge. An amendment to 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. 5101-5956 added two new classes of service, AA and D-4. When he reached mandatory retirement age, the retiree sought to have his service credits transferred to one of the new categories. The appellate court held that the trial court properly denied Class D-4 status as the retiree was not a member of the General Assembly during the relevant time periods under 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5306.2(a). However, the appellate court held that the trial court erred in denying Class AA status. The retiree was similarly situated with individuals who were included in Class AA status as the retiree paid Class A contributions during a 20-year period of outstanding investment performance. The legislature essentially gave the additional earnings on the retiree's contributions to other state employees. The appellate court held that this was not reasonably related to accomplishing the purpose of the amendment. Therefore, SERB violated the retiree's equal protection rights by denying his request to convert to Class AA service.


OUTCOME: The denial of the retiree's request to convert his Class A service to Class D-4 service was affirmed. However, the denial of the retiree's request to convert his Class A service to Class AA service was reversed.


 

1906 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Merion Publ'ns, Inc. v. Commonwealth


No. 792 F.R. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


890 A.2d 436; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 11


November 14, 2005, Argued  
January 11, 2006, Decided  
January 11, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Merion Publ'ns, Inc. v. Commonwealth, 590 Pa. 9, 911 A.2d 917, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2245 (Pa., Nov. 21, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 0224392. State Agency, Board of Finance and Revenue.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Board of Finance and Revenue in the above-captioned matter is hereby REVERSED. Judgment shall become final unless exceptions are filed within 30 days of this order pursuant to Pa. R.A.P. 1571(i).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner publisher appealed an order by respondent Board of Finance and Revenue (Pennsylvania) that denied its request for relief from a sales and use tax deficiency; the publisher claimed that because its publications were direct mail advertising literature or materials, pursuant to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 7204, it was not liable for taxes on the fees paid to independent printers.


OVERVIEW: The publisher produced a variety of publications that were devoted to particular segments of the medical and healthcare industry, which were distributed free of charge. The publisher's revenues were derived from charges paid by those advertising in the publications. Following an audit, the Board determined that the publisher was liable for the use tax on the printers' charges for magazines because the publisher was advertising for other companies rather than on its own behalf. Consequently, the Board concluded that all printers' charges were subject to tax under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 7202(a), (b). The court held that the "direct mail advertising" exemption in Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 7204 applied to advertising literature and materials that were delivered directly to the intended recipient once placed in the United States mail; the advertising materials did not need to be mailed by the vendor. Accordingly, the Board erred in concluding otherwise and taxing the printing charges paid by the publisher.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed.


 

1907 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Grant v. Blaine


No. 1630 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


890 A.2d 32; 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 5


November 23, 2005, Submitted on Briefs  
January 6, 2006, Decided  
January 6, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. A.D. No. 502-2002, Common Pleas Court of the County of Greene, Judge Nalitz, J.
Grant v. Blaine, 582 Pa. 1, 868 A.2d 400, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 359 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a prison conditions litigation action, appellant inmate appealed an order of the Greene County Common Pleas Court (Pennsylvania) denying his request to proceed in forma pauperis. The case had been remanded from the Pennsylvania Supreme Court, which had decided that the denial of the inmate's request to proceed in forma pauperis was final and appealable.


OVERVIEW: The inmate's request for in forma pauperis status was denied under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6602(a)(2)(ii) because the average monthly deposits for the six month period preceding the petition exceeded the amount of the filing fee. The high court found that 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6602(a) unconstitutionally violated that court's rulemaking authority under Pa. Const. art. V, § 10(c) because 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6602(a) contravened the mandate in Pa. R. Civ. P. 240. On remand, the appellate court found that it was unable to conduct an effective appellate review of the case. Because 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6602(a) was declared unconstitutional, the proper standard for determining whether the inmate was entitled to in forma pauperis status was set forth in Pa. R. Civ. P. 240. Thus, a new decision following the standards set forth in Pa. R. Civ. P. 240 was required before the appellate review could be conducted. Furthermore, the trial court was required to note the actual amount of the filing fee so that the appellate court would be able to determine whether the inmate had sufficient funds in his prison bank account to proceed with his lawsuit.


OUTCOME: The order was vacated, and the case was remanded. On remand, the trial court was to note the actual amount of the filing fee when it issued a new decision regarding whether the inmate was entitled to in forma pauperis status.

Marcavage v. Rendell


No. 195 M.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


888 A.2d 940; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 755


September 13, 2005, Argued  
December 22, 2005, Decided  
December 22, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied January 20, 2006.
Motion granted by, Motion denied by Marcavage v. Rendell, 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 616 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Nov. 15, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.



DISPOSITION: Respondents' preliminary objections to Counts I, II, III and IV of Petitioners' amended petition for review are overruled. Respondents are directed to file an answer to the amended petition within thirty days of the date of this order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought a declaration that an amendment to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2710 (Act 143) was unconstitutional as violative of the legislative process mandated by Pa. Const. art. III, §§ 1, 2, 3 and 4. They also sought to enjoin enforcement of Act 143. Respondents, the Commonwealth and various officials, filed preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer to petitioners' amended petition for review filed in the court's original jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: Respondents argued that petitioners failed to state a claim for which relief could be granted. Two officials argued that petitioners' challenge to the constitutionality of Act 143 was non-justiciable. The court concluded that the constitutionality of Act 143 was justiciable because Pa. Const. art. III, §§ 1, 2, 3 and 4 were mandatory directives governing the manner of passing legislation by the Pennsylvania General Assembly and not mere general guidelines. Act 143 began as a bill regarding the criminal offense of agricultural crop destruction, but as passed it expanded the scope of the offense of ethnic intimidation. Petitioners argued that the title of the bill was misleading because the concept of "ethnicity" in the title could not reasonably include "sexual orientation, gender or gender identity" protected by the amendment. The court concluded that a determination of whether the original purpose of the bill was changed by the amendment or whether its title was deceptive and failed to clearly put a reasonable person on notice of the actual contents of the bill could not be made at the preliminary stage of the litigation and therefore required further proceedings.


OUTCOME: Respondents' preliminary objections to petitioners' amended petition for review were overruled. Respondents were directed to file an answer to the amended petition within 30 days.

 

1909 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



City of Philadelphia v. Rendell


No. 305 M.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


888 A.2d 922; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 747


March 2, 2005, Argued  
December 20, 2005, Decided  
December 20, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court were the preliminary objections filed by respondents, the Governor, Edward G. Rendell, and the Philadelphia Parking Authority (the Authority), to a complaint filed by petitioners, the City of Philadelphia and its Mayor, John F. Street (collectively, the City), which challenged an amendment to the Parking Authority Law, 53 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5501-5517 (2001). The court was exercising its original jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: The amendment supplanted the power of a mayor of a first-class city to appoint a parking authority governing board and placed that appointment authority in the governor. The core of the City's challenge to the statutory amendment was that it interfered with the City's home rule powers. The court noted that that issue was already determined by the Pennsylvania Supreme Court, which held that the Parking Authority Law was legislation extrinsic to the City's home rule powers and, thus, its amendment to transfer control of the Authority from the City to the Governor did not infringe upon the City's home rule powers. The issue at bar before the court was whether another amendment to the Parking Authority Law, namely Acts 22 and 9, which extended the Authority's administration of on-street parking in Philadelphia for 10 years, required a different conclusion. Deciding that it did not, the court sustained the preliminary objections. The court found no authority for the City's premise that parking was inherently a municipal function and concluded that on-street parking duties assigned to the Authority were not municipal functions governed by home rule.


OUTCOME: The court sustained all preliminary objections and dismissed the complaint.

Oz Gas, Ltd. v. Warren Area Sch. Dist.


1550 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


886 A.2d 336; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 686; 167 Oil & Gas Rep. 788


October 19, 2005, Argued  
November 9, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by Oz Gas, Ltd. v. Warren Area Sch. Dist., 587 Pa. 733, 901 A.2d 500, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 986 (2006)
Appeal granted by Oz Gas, Ltd. v. Warren Area Sch. Dist., 587 Pa. 734, 901 A.2d 500, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 987 (2006)
Appeal granted by Oz Gas, Ltd. v. Warren Area Sch. Dist., 587 Pa. 734, 901 A.2d 500, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 988 (2006)
Reversed by Oz Gas, Ltd. v. Warren Area Sch. Dist., 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2882 (Pa., Dec. 27, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. A.D. 242 of 2003. Common Pleas Court of the County of Warren. Judge Morgan, J.
Oz Gas, Ltd. v. Warren Area Sch. Dist., 2004 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 343 (2004)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of the 37th Judicial District in Warren County (Pennsylvania), which granted motions for summary judgment filed by appellee local taxing authorities on whether the taxpayer was entitled to a refund under § 1 of the Refund Law, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 5566b, for taxes paid on oil and gas interests and which denied the taxpayer's cross-motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer paid taxes to the authorities for three years on its oil and gas interests under § 201(a) of the General County Assessment Law, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 5020-201(a). However, a controlling decision was rendered that § 201(a) did not authorize counties to tax oil and gas interests. The taxpayer filed a complaint for refund under § 1 of the Refund Law. On cross-motions for summary judgment, the trial court held that the Refund Law was not independently retroactive and that the controlling decision did not meet the criteria for retroactive application. It granted summary judgment to the authorities. The taxpayer appealed, arguing that the Refund Law was independently retroactive. The court agreed. The Refund Law, under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 5566b(b), clearly and unambiguously allowed a refund of taxes that were assessed based on an interpretation of a statutory provision that was later held to be erroneous, as long as a written claim for refund was filed within three years of the assessment. Since the taxpayer sought a refund within three years for taxes assessed under an erroneous interpretation of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 5020-201(a), it was entitled to a refund.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order granting the authorities' summary judgment motions and denying the taxpayer's cross-motion for summary judgment.

 

1911 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Doe v. Miller


No. 230 M.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


886 A.2d 310; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 639


September 16, 2005, Submitted  
October 27, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Doe v. Miller, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1271 (Pa., July 18, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court in its original jurisdiction was the preliminary objection in the nature of a demurrer filed by the Pennsylvania Attorney General, representing respondent, the Commissioner of the Pennsylvania State Police, to the civil class action filed by petitioner inmates, who were subject to the provisions of what was commonly referred to as Megan's Law II, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 9791-9799.7.


OVERVIEW: The Commissioner argued that the inmates failed to state a cause of action for which relief could be granted. Specifically, the Commissioner argued that, as Megan's Law II involved neither fundamental rights nor proceeded on suspect lines, it was accorded a strong presumption of validity and would be upheld so long as there was some plausible reason for the legislative action. The court concluded that the inmates failed to aver the prerequisite for substantive due process review because they did not specify which property right or other protected interest was deprived by Megan's Law II. The classification created by Megan's Law II--convicted sexual offenders as a category of felons--was not based on a suspect class. Nor did the classification affect any fundamental rights. As such, Megan's Law II was subject to the rational basis test. The court found that both prongs of the rational basis test were satisfied. First, Megan's Law II sought to promote the legitimate state interest of public safety and welfare pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9791(a). Second, the classification of convicted sexual offender was reasonably related to enhancing public safety and welfare.


OUTCOME: The Commissioner's preliminary objection in the nature of a demurrer was sustained and the inmates' action was dismissed with prejudice.

 

1912 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Chuk v. State Emples. Ret. Sys.


No. 145 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


885 A.2d 605; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 632


September 15, 2005, Argued  
October 24, 2005, Decided  
October 24, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 2000-18. State Employees' Retirement Board.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Pennsylvania State Employees' Retirement Board of December 22, 2004, in the above-captioned matter is hereby affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee sought to convert 14 years of service with a county to respondent state retirement system for purposes of his retirement pension. The State Employees' Retirement Board (Pennsylvania) denied the request. The employee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employee worked for a county before becoming a state employee. While he was able to convert the years of service with that county to his pension with the state retirement system, he also sought to convert 14 years of service with another county as well. The hearing examiner found that 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5303.1 limited the employee's right of conversion to that pension plan in which he was a contributor "immediately prior" to becoming a state employee. The board held that 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5303.1(a) limited the transfer to a single "retirement plan," i.e., the one in which the employee was a contributor at the moment of transfer. The appellate court agreed. The employee's vested rights in the prior county pension system were unaffected; his annuity account continued to accrue interest until he reached age 60. To convert county service time to the state retirement system, all of that county service time had to have been rolled into the single retirement system in which the employee was a contributor "immediately prior" to his transfer to state employment.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

DaimlerChrysler Corp. v. Commonwealth


No. 279 F.R. 2004, No. 468 F.R. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


885 A.2d 117; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 624


September 13, 2005, Argued  
October 20, 2005, Decided  
October 20, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at DaimlerChrysler Corp. v. Commonwealth, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 1346 (Pa., June 25, 2007)



DISPOSITION: Orders of the Board of Finance and Revenue dated March 23, 2004, at No. 0304762, and May 25, 2004, at Nos. 0321912, 0322004, and 0322019, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Board of Finance and Revenue (Pennsylvania) entered two orders that denied petitioner seller's requests for refunds of sales tax paid on various vehicles that the seller was required to repurchase from dissatisfied customers under the state's automobile lemon law. The seller appealed.


OVERVIEW: The seller filed requests for sales tax refunds it had paid on various vehicles that it was required to repurchase from dissatisfied customers under the state automobile lemon law. The appellate board, in separate cases, rejected requests for refunds where the original purchaser had paid the sales tax more than three years before the date the seller filed the refund petition with the appellate board. The seller appealed. The Board of Finance and Revenue (Board) consolidated the appeals for disposition. Seller argued before the Board that its due process rights were violated by enforcing Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 10003.1(a)'s three-year statute of limitations for filing a refund request; it explained that the statute placed the seller in the anomalous position of having the statute of limitation on its right to a refund expire before the right to request that refund even accrued. The Board denied the appeals. On further review, the appellate court found that the statute was a statute of repose, and, once the time for filing a refund expired, there was no longer a "right" to process, even in cases where the refund right did not accrue within the limitations period.


OUTCOME: The Board's orders were affirmed.

Free Speech, LLC v. City of Philadelphia


No. 1372 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

Free Speech, LLC v. City of Phila., 2005 Phila. Ct. Com. Pl. LEXIS 382 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a limited liability company, billboard owners, and a billboard advertiser (billboard owners), appealed from the denial by the Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania) of their motion for a special and preliminary injunction enjoining appellees, a City, the acting commissioner of its Department of Licenses and Inspections, and the commissioner of its Department of Revenue, from enforcing a billboard excise tax.
