Konidaris v. Portnoff Law Assocs.


No. 2428 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


884 A.2d 348; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 521


May 4, 2005, Argued  
September 16, 2005, Decided  
September 16, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Application for reargument denied November 2, 2005.
Appeal granted by Konidaris v. Portnoff Law Assocs., 588 Pa. 760, 903 A.2d 539, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2575 (2006)
Appeal granted by Konidaris v. Portnoff Law Assocs., 588 Pa. 760, 903 A.2d 539, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1307 (2006)
Reversed by, in part, Remanded by Konidaris v. Portnoff Law Assocs., 598 Pa. 55, 953 A.2d 1231, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 1291 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 03-006341. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Judge Horgos, J.
Roethlein v. Portnoff Law Assocs., 2005 Phila. Ct. Com. Pl. LEXIS 621 (2005)



DISPOSITION: The order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, dated September 17, 2004, is hereby AFFIRMED in part and REVERSED in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant delinquent taxpayers challenged the addition by appellee law firm of attorney collection fees to delinquent tax claims. The taxpayers appealed the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which denied their motion for summary judgment and granted the law firm's motion for partial summary judgment. The trial court's certified order for appeal by permission was granted.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the issue was the constitutionality of a retroactive amendment to the Municipal Claims and Tax Liens Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, §§ 7101-7505, which permitted recovery of reasonable attorney collection fees in addition to delinquent property tax. The appellate court concluded that the taxpayers had an accrued cause of action for declaratory judgment by the time they filed suit, and that the requested declaration would be unavailable if the amendment was applied retroactively. However, the request for declaratory relief contained a fatal jurisdictional flaw where the taxpayers neither joined nor served any taxing authority. In addition, application of the amendment to the taxpayers' unjust enrichment cause of action would remove any argument that enrichment was unjust, thereby effectively extinguishing the cause of action. Thus, the amendment affected this cause of action in a way prohibited by Pa. Const. art. I, § 11, and the trial court erred when it concluded otherwise. Finally, no cause of action for fraud accrued before the amendment was enacted because the taxpayers did not plead reliance on the law firm's representations.


OUTCOME: The case was remanded for dismissal of the declaratory judgment count. The grant of partial summary judgment was affirmed as to the unjust enrichment claims relating to attorney's fees actually received by the law firm after enactment of the amendment, but was reversed as to attorney's fees actually received before enactment of the amendment. The order of the trial court was affirmed as to the fraud count.

 

1916 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Pennock v. Lenzi


No. 1788 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


882 A.2d 1057; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 520


June 8, 2005, Argued  
September 15, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Pennock v. Lenzi, 897 A.2d 462, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 508 (Pa., 2006)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Pennock v. Lenzi, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 7182 (U.S., Oct. 2, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 2003 CV 848. Common Pleas Court of the County of Dauphin. Turgeon, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff parents appealed the judgment from the Common Pleas Court of the County of Dauphin (Pennsylvania), which dismissed their wrongful death and survival actions with prejudice because they were barred by the two-year statute of limitations, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5524(2).


OVERVIEW: The parents' teenage son died in 1995 after contracting both rotavirus and staphylococcus infections. Nearly eight years later, they filed suit alleging that their son's death was caused by prolonged exposure to sewage sludge from a neighboring farm. They asserted that they could not reasonably know until 2001, when they read an article discussing the connection between sewage sludge and infections, how their son had contracted the infections that caused his death. Relying on the Pennsylvania supreme court's ruling in Pastierik, the trial court dismissed the suit, finding that the discovery rule, which tolled the statute of limitations until a time when a tort victim could discover his injury and its cause, did not apply to wrongful death and survival claims. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that as a matter of constitutional law, statutes of limitation go to matters of remedy, not to destruction of fundamental rights. Because of the clear notice that death provides, combined with the ability to perform an autopsy to discover its cause, the court held that the two years prescribed by the legislature afforded a decedent's representatives a reasonable opportunity to bring suit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

1917 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Graham Packaging Co., LP v. Commonwealth


No. 652 F.R. 2002


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


882 A.2d 1076; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 518


June 9, 2005, Argued  
September 15, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Board of Finance and Revenue.



DISPOSITION:  The order of the Board was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Pennsylvania Board of Finance and Revenue denied petitioner taxpayer's request for a refund of the sales tax it paid in connection with the renewal of various licenses for "canned" (i.e., non-custom) computer software. The taxpayer petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer used computer software programs in connection with its business, and paid a software company for a two-year renewal of multiple licenses to use various canned computer software programs that it had previously purchased. The taxpayer petitioned for a refund of the sales tax paid in connection with the license renewals, which the Board denied. The taxpayer argued that 1997 amendments to the Pennsylvania Tax Reform Code of 1971 repealed the tax on canned software. The court noted that while the 1997 amendments deleted custom computer software from the definition of a "sale at retail," thereby eliminating it as a taxable service, they did not indicate that "tangible personal property" no longer included canned computer software. Under the "essence of the transaction" test, which the court adopted, canned software was tangible property because the purchaser acquired an electronic copy of a computer program that was stored on a computer's hardware. The Pennsylvania Department of Revenue's statement of policy, 61 Pa. Code § 60.19, properly interpreted the Code by stating that the sale of canned software remained subject to tax as the sale of tangible personal property.


OUTCOME: The order of the Board was affirmed.

DeWeese v. Weaver


No. 567 M.D. 2002


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


880 A.2d 54; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 407


April 5, 2005, Argued  
July 26, 2005, Decided  
July 26, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed without opinion by Deweese v. Cortes, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1841 (Pa., Sept. 28, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: DeWeese v. Weaver, 824 A.2d 364, 2003 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 396 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2003)



DISPOSITION:  The court granted petitioners' motion for summary judgment, denied the Secretary's motion for summary judgment, and declared Act 57 of 2002 to be unconstitutional and void.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, the House Minority Leader and Member of the House Rules Committee and the Democratic Whip of the House of Representatives, sought declaratory and injunctive relief, alleging the enactment of Act 57 of 2002 violated Pa. Const. art. III, § 1, 2, and 4. Respondent Secretary of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania sought to dismiss the suit. Both parties sought summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Act 57 of 2002 began as an Senate Bill (S.B.) 1089 and was introduced in the Pennsylvania Senate as an act amending the DNA Detection of Sexual and Violent Offenders Act (DNA Act). The house made amendments, the senate concurred with the amendments, and finally the house and senate passed the bill, which then included a provision for joint and several liability for negligence. The bill was then signed by the governor. Petitioners then filed suit alleging that the bill was unconstitutional. The court disposed of the Secretary's preliminary objections, finding that in order to pass muster, each subject in an act must be "germane" to the other subjects in the act and that it could not say that requiring DNA samples from incarcerated sex offenders bore a "proper relation" to joint and several liability for acts of negligence. The court then granted summary judgment in favor of petitioners, finding that it had quite properly required that both parts of S.B. 1089, regarding the codification and repeal of the DNA Act and joint and several tort liability, needed to relate to one single overarching subject in order to comply with the provisions of Pa. Const. art. III, § 3.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioners' motion for summary judgment, denied the Secretary's motion for summary judgment, and declared Act 57 of 2002 to be unconstitutional and void.

 

1920 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


D.C. v. Sch. Dist.


No. 444 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


879 A.2d 408; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 399


March 2, 2005, Argued  
July 20, 2005, Decided  
July 20, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 3675 September Term 2002. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Judge Jones, J.
D.C. v. Sch. Dist. of Phila., 2004 Phila. Ct. Com. Pl. LEXIS 80 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile students challenged a decision from the Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which denied their motion for summary judgment and granted a cross-motion for summary judgment by appellee school district on the students' class action for a declaration that § 2134 of the Public School Code of 1949 (Code), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 24, § 21-2134, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: After the students were adjudicated delinquent, they were returned to the district. Under § 2134(c) of the Code, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 24, § 21-2134(c), they were sent directly to alternative classrooms instead of regular classrooms. They brought a class action challenging the constitutionality of § 2134(c), arguing it violated procedural due process, equal protection, and the special legislation provisions of Pa. Const. art. III, § 32. Summary judgment was granted to the district and the students appealed. The court found that the Code did not violate special legislation because Pa. Const. art. III, § 20, allowed the classification of school districts and stated that such classifications were general, not specific, legislation. However, the students had a procedural due process right to a hearing to challenge an alternative classroom assignment since Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 24, § 21-2134(c) created an irrebuttable presumption that returning delinquent students were barred from regular classrooms. The students were entitled to the informal hearing procedures that already existed in 22 Pa. Code § 12.8(c) before a decision on whether were to be placed in alternative classrooms.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed summary judgment to the extent that it held that the Code was not unconstitutional special legislation. However, it otherwise reversed summary judgment. The court instead invalidated § 2134(c) of the Code as unconstitutional.

 

1921 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Smith v. Cortes


No. 810 M.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


879 A.2d 382; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 392


May 2, 2005, Argued  
July 19, 2005, Decided  
July 19, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Smith v. Cortez, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 827 (Pa., May 24, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.



DISPOSITION: The preliminary objection of the Honorable Pedro A. Cortes, Secretary of State of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, is sustained and the petition of Bruce L. Smith and all others similarly situated is dismissed with prejudice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Secretary of State of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania filed preliminarily objections to a petition for review filed by petitioner notary and all others similarly situated, which sought payment from the Secretary for expenses incurred when the notary and others in the same class completed an education requirement for notary publics.


OVERVIEW: Amended Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 57, § 151(c) required that notary public applicants complete three hours of approved training. The Secretary took the amendment to mean that when current notaries filed for renewal of their terms, they were required to have completed the education requirement. The notary complied with the requirement prior to his renewal date. The state supreme court then ruled that the Secretary's policy was invalid and that only new notaries needed to fulfill the education requirement. The notary filed suit against the Secretary, arguing that the money he had to pay for the three hours of education constituted a taking under both the Fifth Amendment and Pa. Const. art. 1, § 10. Applying the Penn Central criteria, the court disagreed. The notary failed to detail any specific facts that constituted a significant economic impact on those who had been required to attend the required education before the Secretary's policy was invalidated. Further, the Secretary's decision was designed to follow the amended statute to promote the education of notary publics. Finally, as to claims against the Secretary himself, he was entitled to immunity under 1 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2310.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the preliminary objection and dismissed the notary's complaint with prejudice.

 

1922 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


County of Mercer v. Amundsen


No. 2235 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


879 A.2d 366; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 356


May 4, 2005, Argued  
July 13, 2005, Decided  
July 13, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by County of Mercer v. Amundsen, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 412 (Pa., Apr. 4, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 2004-2688. Common Pleas Court of the County of Mercer. Thomson, S.J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the county and its board of commissioners, appealed the judgment in mandamus of the Court of Common Pleas of Mercer County (Pennsylvania), which essentially determined that appellee county controller did not need to disperse part of a loan to a former county nursing home, and that the commissioners were not authorized to make the loan under the County Code, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 16, §§ 101-2399.73.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the central issue was whether the express authority to make an appropriation included the power to loan money. Relying on Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 16, § 2169, the commissioners argued that the county had the power to make an appropriation to a former county nursing home. The appellate court concluded that the term "appropriation" was generic, describing a process and not specifying a form. The term embraced the action taken by the commissioners. The loan was expressly authorized by Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 16, § 2169 where the loan was made from public moneys and was set aside for an authorized public purpose--the support of a nonprofit corporation organized to give medical care to dependents of the county. In addition, while the County Code and the Pennsylvania Constitution limited the purpose of the appropriation in question, neither limited the form of the appropriation. Neither the Pennsylvania Constitution nor the County Code limited the power to place conditions or restrictions on the appropriation, such as gradual repayment. Thus, the trial court fell into error when it concluded otherwise.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court, granting judgment in mandamus, was reversed. The case was remanded for the entry of judgment in favor of the commissioners.

In re Springfield Sch. Dist.


No. 2762 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


879 A.2d 335; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 344


June 9, 2005, Argued  
July 11, 2005, Decided  
July 11, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from Nos. 01-13876 & 01-13875 Common Pleas Court of the County of Delaware Judge Zetusky, J.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County dated December 6, 2004 is AFFIRMED in part and REVERSED in part. The Order is affirmed as to the property located at 674 Baltimore Pike, Springfield Township. The Order is reversed as to the property located at 134 Baltimore Pike, Springfield Township. As to the latter property, the matter is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion. Jurisdiction relinquished.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a school district and a township, in consolidated appeals from real estate assessments, sought review of a decision of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Delaware (Pennsylvania), which declined appellants' request that the assessed values of two commercial properties be increased.


OVERVIEW: The two properties were a car dealership and a furniture store. Appellants challenged the trial court's conclusion that their expert witness testimony regarding the dealership was of insufficient weight to sustain the burden of overcoming the prima facie validity of its assessment. As to the furniture store property, appellants challenged the trial court's conclusion that the method by which assessments to appeal was determined was discriminatory and resulted in non-uniform taxation, contrary to Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1. The court reversed in part. The trial court considered the testimony from appellants' expert, and stating three reasons, found that it was not credible. Its statement of reasons was sufficient to support the trial court's finding. However, the trial court erred in finding § 1 tax uniformity violation. Appellants had the right to appeal the assessments under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 5350i, and the law placed no restrictions on the "methodology" employed in determining whether to appeal. Further, the evidence established that the furniture store's fair market value exceeded its assessment. It was not discrimination to appeal the incorrect assessment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision regarding the appeal of the dealership assessment. The court reversed the trial court' order as to the furniture store assessment and remanded that matter for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.

Concentric Network Corp. v. Commonwealth


No. 290 F.R. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


877 A.2d 542; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 322


April 4, 2005, Argued  
June 17, 2005, Decided  
June 17, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by Concentric Network Corp. v. Commonwealth, 2006 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 185 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Apr. 13, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 0207354 & 0207356. State Agency Board of Finance and Revenue Common Pleas Court of the County of Judge.



DISPOSITION: In accordance with the above, the decision of Board of Finance and Revenue, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporation, an Internet service provider (ISP), appealed the order of the Pennsylvania Board of Finance and Revenue, which affirmed the decision of the Pennsylvania Department of Revenue (DOR) Board of Appeals denying the corporation's petition for a refund of sales and use tax.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the corporation raised several issues. The appellate court concluded that the data transport services (DTS) at issue met the definition of telecommunications service in Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 7201(rr)(3) so as to constitute taxable tangible property because the corporation used the lines to transmit by wire digital signals containing the customer's message or information. In addition, the purchases of the DTS were not exempt under the resale exclusion of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 7201(k)(8) because the data lines, modems, and routers could not be incorporated into "other tangible personal property" as required by Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 7201(i)(2) where Internet access was an intangible. Also, the DTS were not exempt under the public utility exception because ISPs were not public utilities defined under Pennsylvania law and were not subject to any form of regulation as a public utility by the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission. Finally, the DOR did not violate the Internet Tax Freedom Act, Pub. L. No. 105-277, Div. C., Title XI, §§ 1100-1104, Oct. 21, 1998, 112 Stat. 2681-719, because ISPs were treated the same as telecommunications carriers and cable operators.


OUTCOME: The Board's decision was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. Reynolds


No. 1405 C.D. 2004, No. 1406 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


876 A.2d 1088; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 319


March 1, 2005, Argued  
June 16, 2005, Decided  
June 16, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. M-0551-03. State Agency Common Pleas Court of the County of Chester. Judge Wood, S.J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated appeals, appellant animal owner and appellant Pennsylvania Game Commission challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), which partially granted and partially denied the owner's motion under Pa. R. Crim. P. 588 for return of her property in the form of animals that the commission seized incident to criminal charges of which the owner was acquitted.


OVERVIEW: A commission officer executed a search warrant at the owner's residence and seized serval cats, fennic foxes, kinkajous, and lemurs. The owner was cited on criminal charges of possessing exotic wildlife without a dealer permit. She was acquitted on all charges, so she filed a motion for return of the animals. The trial court determined that the cats and foxes were exotic wildlife and would not be returned, but that the kinkajous and lemurs were not and would be returned. The owner appealed, arguing that her acquittal collaterally estopped the commission from arguing the animals were contraband and that none of the animals were contraband. On cross-appeal, the commission argued that the kinkajous and lemurs were contraband per se and should not have been returned. The court found that the owner's acquittal had no preclusive effect in her subsequent civil action for return of property. As to the parties' dispute over whether the animals were contraband per se, preliminary findings were first required on whether the owner had either a federal license or state permit to possess the animals, and whether the state regulations were preempted by the federal laws.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order in its entirety and remanded for findings on preliminary questions related to whether the owner was a licensed dealer of the animals before deciding whether the animals were contraband per se.

Reading Sch. Dist. v. Dep't of Educ.


No. 2278 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


875 A.2d 1218; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 298


April 4, 2005, Argued  
June 6, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by Reading Sch. Dist. v. Dep't of Educ., 2005 Pa. LEXIS 2531 (Pa., Nov. 21, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Reading Sch. Dist. v. Dep't of Educ., 855 A.2d 166, 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 586 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner school district sought review of a determination of an assessment bureau that six of its schools failed to make annual yearly progress (AYP) under the federal No Child Left Behind Law (Act), 20 U.S.C.S. § 6301 et seq. Respondent Department of Education dismissed the appeal. The school district appealed.


OVERVIEW: The appeal to the Department was dismissed upon a motion which argued that the appeal violated the Department's new policy for the appeal of determinations of failure to achieve AYP. That policy allowed appeals based only on assertions that (1) the data upon which the Department made the determination were incorrect, (2) significant growth had been made toward meeting the goals of the Act, or (3) an unforeseen circumstance beyond a district's control prevented it from achieving AYP. The school district challenged the validity of the Department's implementation of the Act as it applied to the school district. The court found that this was proper since the Department was acting pursuant to powers granted to it by the Act. The Department violated the school district's due process rights when it limited the grounds upon which it could appeal the bureau's determination by intentionally misinterpreting the Act. The Department redefined the meaning given to an acronym in the Act to suit its own purpose of limiting the appeals of its decisions under the Act. Accordingly, the Department's appeal policy was an unconstitutional restraint on due process.


OUTCOME: The order dismissing the appeal was vacated, and the case was remanded.

Dunn v. Bd. of Prop. Assessment, Appeals & Review


No. 864 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


877 A.2d 504; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 295


February 3, 2005, Argued  
June 1, 2005, Decided  
June 1, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Application for reargument denied July 28, 2005.
Appeal granted by, in part Dunn v. Bd. of Prop. Assessment, 590 Pa. 620, 913 A.2d 863, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2452 (2006)
Affirmed by, Application denied by Dunn v. Bd. of Prop. Assessment, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2905 (Pa., Dec. 27, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] GD99-523. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Wettick, J.
Dunn v. Allegheny County Prop. Assessment Appeals & Review, 794 A.2d 416, 2002 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 168 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers appealed the order of the Allegheny County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania) dismissing all counts of the taxpayers' second amended class action complaint that challenged a court order increasing property tax assessments by two percent and that asserted that appellee Allegheny County Board of Property Assessment, Appeals, and Review improperly implemented the increases.


OVERVIEW: The trial court determined that a freeze in property tax assessments instituted, in part, by the Board was unlawful. The trial court later imposed a two percent increase in the assessed fair market value for all properties in the county for each of the 1999 and 2000 tax years. The taxpayers filed the instant class action seeking declaratory and injunctive relief. The trial court properly found that the taxpayers could not maintain a class action as they failed to pursue an adequate statutory remedy for a refund of the purportedly improperly collected taxes. The legislature provided an adequate statutory method, under Pennsylvania Refund Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 5566b et seq., and under the Pennsylvania General County Assessment Law, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 5020-101 et seq., for seeking a tax refund for improperly paid taxes. As a result, a class action remedy was not available. Likewise, because of this statutory remedy, the taxpayers could not seek a refund under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 based on a violation of due process and equal protection.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

County of Lebanon v. Pa. Labor Rels. Bd.


No. 1890 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


873 A.2d 859; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 243; 177 L.R.R.M. 2265


April 5, 2005, Argued  
May 6, 2005, Decided  
May 6, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by County of Lebanon v. Pa. Labor Rels. Bd., 585 Pa. 691, 887 A.2d 1243, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 2428 (2005)
Appeal denied by County of Lebanon v. Pa. Labor Rels. Bd., 585 Pa. 691, 887 A.2d 1243, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 2432 (2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. PF-R-02-113-E. State Agency PA Labor Relations Board.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A firefighters' union, on behalf of employees comprising petitioner county's hazardous materials (hazmat) team, filed a petition for representation seeking to represent them as "firefighters" under Act 111, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 43, §§ 217.1-217.12. A hearing examiner dismissed the petition, but respondent Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board reversed. The county sought review.


OVERVIEW: The evidence before the hearing examiner indicated that the employees, certified hazardous material responders, generally performed administrative work or responded to hazardous material calls. They also occasionally responded to fire scenes where there were existing or potential problems with hazardous materials. The hearing examiner found the employees had no legislative authority to act as firefighters. On appeal, the Board found that because they were trained and expected to respond to certain types of fires, they were "firefighters" under Act 111. The appellate court disagreed. Under the Pennsylvania Hazardous Material Emergency Planning and Response Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 35, §§ 6022.101-6022.307, hazmat employees had a limited statutory authority, if any, to act as firefighters because their primary purpose was to suppress the release of hazardous materials. The mere reference in training standards to situations where any given hazmat team might encounter a fire and should have training to deal with those situations was insufficient to legislatively authorize hazmat employees to be firefighters.


OUTCOME: The order of the Board was reversed.

In re Mun. Reapportionment of Haverford


No. 2781 C.D. 2004, No. 4 C.D. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


873 A.2d 821; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 233


February 2, 2005, Argued  
April 29, 2005, Decided  
April 29, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by In re Mun. Reapportionment of the Twp. of Haverford, 587 Pa. 702, 897 A.2d 462, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 278 (Pa., Mar. 21, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 04-80312 and 04-005992. Common Pleas Court of the County of Delaware. Judge Zetusky, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney challenged orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania) that dismissed her petitions under 53 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 904 (§ 904 petition) and under 53 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 906 (§ 906 petition) to challenge an ordinance by respondent township to approve an election district reapportionment plan by respondent township board of elections.


OVERVIEW: The attorney challenged an ordinance approving the board's plan to reapportion election districts. The trial court dismissed the attorney's petitions and she appealed. The court found, as to the § 904 petition, that once the board, as the township's legislative body, acted to reapportion the districts, the court lacked authority under 53 Pa. Cons. Stat § 904 to reapportion the township by any judicial order. Also, the trial court properly held that the procedural challenge to the ordinance was untimely. The trial court properly dismissed those portions of the attorney's § 906 petition that challenged the constitutionality of the reapportionment plan based on deviations because the maximum deviation between legislative districts was below 10 percent. Such a minor deviation failed to make out a prima facie case of discrimination. However, because alleged pleading deficiencies were not a ground for dismissal, the court had to vacate the trial court's order dismissing the § 906 petition with respect to claims that the ordinance was constitutionally infirm because its districts were not compact and contiguous and did not incorporate distinctive geographical features in the township.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed dismissal of the § 904 petition. The court vacated dismissal of the claims in the § 906 petition that asserted violations of geographical boundaries standards and violations of the constitutional compact and contiguous standard and remanded for further proceedings on those claims, but otherwise affirmed dismissal of the § 906 petition.

Commonwealth v. Brandon


No. 524 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


872 A.2d 239; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 205


February 1, 2005, Argued  
April 12, 2005, Decided  
April 12, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Commonwealth v. Brandon, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 2561 (Pa., Nov. 22, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. C.A. 03-62. Common Pleas Court of the County of Butler. Horan, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Butler County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania) denying defendant's motion to dismiss defendant's summary conviction for violating Lancaster Township, Pa., Ordinance No. 50, which prohibited the use of spot lamps in Lancaster Township.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was accused of violating Lancaster Township, Pa., Ordinance No. 50, which prohibited individuals from projecting a powerful, concentrated beam of light across another person's land. Defendant was spotlighting deer. The trial court did not err in finding that Lancaster Township, Pa., Ordinance No. 50 was not preempted by the Pennsylvania Game and Wildlife Code, 34 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 101 et seq. Even if the field of hunting was preempted by the Code, preemption of Lancaster Township, Pa., Ordinance No. 50 did not occur as Lancaster Township, Pa., Ordinance No. 50 did not even mention hunting. The Code did not preempt the field of spotlighting. Lancaster Township, Pa., Ordinance No. 50 was not unconstitutionally vague. Spotlamp was defined as an illuminating device, either fixed or movable, intended to project a powerful concentrated beam of light. A person of reasonable intelligence was able to understand the difference between a flashlight and a spotlamp. Lancaster Township, Pa., Ordinance No. 50 was not overbroad. It was reasonable to interpret Lancaster Township, Pa., Ordinance No. 50 as being limited to conduct that was not authorized by a landowner.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

Wagaman v. Attorney General


No. 634 M.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


872 A.2d 244; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 206


February 4, 2005, Submitted  
April 12, 2005, Decided  
April 12, 2005, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [**1] This Court sustains the Respondent's preliminary objection that the Attorney General is not a proper party to this action and dismiss Petitioners' "Civil Class Action" with prejudice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmates filed an original petition for review against respondent attorney general, alleging that Megan's Law II, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 9791-9799.7, violated the Equal Protection Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, Pa. Const. art. I, §§ 9, 26, and Pa. Const. art. IX, § 1. The attorney general filed preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer seeking dismissal of the petition for review.


OVERVIEW: The petition alleged that Megan's Law II was based on conclusions drawn from flawed premises. The attorney general argued, inter alia, that he was not a proper party since he did not have any responsibility to administer or enforce Megan's Law II and that the only other way he could be named as a proper party was if his own actions were at issue, which was not the case in the instant matter. The court found that, while the attorney general had a statutory duty pursuant to the Commonwealth Attorneys Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 71, §§ 732-101 to 732-506, to uphold and defend the constitutionality of all statutes so as to prevent their suspension or abrogation, his duty to uphold the laws of the state, standing alone, did not suffice to render him a proper party in the instant action. In order to bring suit against the attorney general, the attorney general must have been the official who was charged with the enforcement and administration of Megan's Law II and, clearly, under the provisions of the statute, it was the Pennsylvania State Police who were charged with administering and enforcing the provision of Megan's Law II. The attorney general was not a proper party.


OUTCOME: The preliminary objection in the nature of demurrer was sustained, and the petition for review was dismissed with prejudice.

Popowsky v. Pa. PUC


No. 2497 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


869 A.2d 1144; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 137


November 3, 2004, Argued  
March 14, 2005, Decided  
March 14, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Popowsky v. Pa. PUC, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 101 (Pa., Mar. 7, 2006)
Appeal denied by Popowsky v. Pa. PUC, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 99 (Pa., Mar. 7, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. R-00027982, R-00027982C0001-C0017, C0019-C0022. Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner consumer advocate sought review of an adjudication by respondent Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission (PUC), which approved a surcharge proposed by intervenor water utility as an amendment to its tariff for wastewater collection service.


OVERVIEW: The utility proposed the surcharge as the mechanism for funding infrastructure improvements to its wastewater collection systems. The advocate contended that any rate increase occasioned by utility's investment in its wastewater systems should not be automatic but, rather, should be established in a base rate proceeding. The PUC interpreted 66 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1307(a) as allowing for such a surcharge. The court reversed. The surcharge would have been used to recover capital costs for new facilities. 66 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1315 required a showing that such facilities would be used and useful prior to recovering the capital costs. Because a Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1307(a) surcharge provided no opportunity for such a showing, a surcharge could be used to fund capital improvements. The PUC's approval of the surcharge under § 1307(a) would allow the utility to recover its capital expenditures with only an initial and cursory review. Further, the surcharge was not authorized by § 1307(g) because it dealt only with projects involving the distribution of water. To recover the costs for the improvements, the utility was required to file a base rate case pursuant to 66 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1308.


OUTCOME: The PUC's decision was reversed.

Smolow v. Hafer


No. 208 M.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


867 A.2d 767; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 56


December 6, 2004, Argued  
February 9, 2005, Decided  
February 9, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Smolow v. Hafer, 2008 Pa. LEXIS 2049 (Pa., Nov. 19, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of Original Jurisdiction.
Smolow v. Hafer, 353 F. Supp. 2d 561, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 987 (E.D. Pa., 2005)



DISPOSITION: Respondent's preliminary objections sustained; amended complaint dismissed with prejudice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner property owner, individually and on behalf of all persons and entities similarly situated, commenced a class action against respondents, Pennsylvania's treasurer and treasury department. The property owner sought the payment of interest on property recovered pursuant to the Disposition of Unclaimed and Abandoned Property Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, §§ 1301.1-1301.28a. Respondents filed preliminary objections to the complaint.


OVERVIEW: In their preliminary objections, respondents raised numerous issues. The court concluded that pursuant to the plain language of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 1301.21, a person aggrieved by a governmental decision pertaining to a claim could commence an action in the Commonwealth Court to establish the claim, and the action was to be tried de novo without a jury. In addition, where the statutory procedure was not followed, any putative class member who did not commence an action within the statutorily prescribed time was not entitled to recover interest. The mechanism for an aggrieved person to challenge an action of respondents under the Unclaimed Property Law was personal to "persons aggrieved," and could not be transferred to others by way of a class action. Also, the delivery of the property owner's stock to respondents did not constitute a taking. It was the property owner's abandonment of his property, not the action of respondents, which caused his pecuniary loss. Finally, the Unclaimed Property Law did not expressly provide for the payment of interest.


OUTCOME: Respondents' preliminary objections to the property owner's complaint were sustained, and the complaint was dismissed, with prejudice.

Vees v. Carbon County Bd. of Assessment Appeals


No. 2433 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


867 A.2d 742; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 51


December 8, 2004, Argued  
February 8, 2005, Decided  
February 8, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Vees v. Carbon County Bd. of Assessment Appeals, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2775 (Pa., Dec. 18, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 02-2527. Common Pleas Court of the County of Carbon. Judge: Nanovic, II, J.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of the order of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Carbon (Pennsylvania), which approved the decision from appellee county board of assessment appeals that increased the assessed value of the taxpayers' property for the 2003 tax year upon the petition of appellee school district.


OVERVIEW: The county performed a county wide reassessment effective for tax year 2001. The property at issue, which consisted of 85 acres of unimproved land, was assigned a fair market value of $ 92,250.00. In April 2002, the taxpayers purchased the property for $ 170,000.00. The school district then appealed to the county board of assessment appeals, contending that the property's fair market value should be $ 170,000.00. The board then increased the property's fair market value to $ 161,900.00. After application of the county's predetermined ratio of 50 percent, the trial court set the assessed value of the taxpayers' property at $ 80,950.00. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that the school district's use of the statutory appeal mechanism available uniformly to all interested parties through Pa. Stat. tit. 72, § 5453.706 did not amount to deliberate, purposeful discrimination in violation of either Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1 or U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1 because the filing of a tax assessment appeal by a school district did not amount to an improper spot assessment and the application of the county's predetermined ratio satisfied the constitutional mandate requiring uniformity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.

LaRock v. Bd. of Supervisors


No. 1997 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


866 A.2d 1208; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 34



January 26, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 3389-C of 2002. Common Pleas Court of the County of Luzerne. Ciavarella, Jr., J.
Levan v. Larock, 784 A.2d 287, 2001 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 755 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2001)



DISPOSITION: Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, residents and taxpayers of a township, appealed the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County (Pennsylvania), which granted a petition for a curative amendment to appellee property owners, permitting them to conduct a quarry-mining operation and other related activities in an A-1 Conservation District. In so doing, the trial court reversed the decision of appellee township board of supervisors.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, appellants argued that the board's decision to deny the curative amendment was supported by substantial evidence. Specifically, they argued that any exclusion of mining was partial and, therefore, the case did not concern a de jure exclusion. The appellate court found that Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 10603, taken as a whole, weighed and balanced several different interests in requiring zoning ordinances to reflect the policy goals of the community, and that Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 10603(i) set out the reasonable development of minerals as one such interest. However, the statute required a balancing of interests to determine whether the township zoning ordinance was reasonable. The trial court did not consider any of the factors or balance the interests set out in Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 10603, but rather focused only on the bare percentage of land that had not been already mined and was still available for mining in the Industrial District, found it to be negligible, and automatically granted site specific relief. This was error.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court was vacated and the matter was remanded to the trial court with directions to remand the matter to the board for evaluation of the relevant statutory factors.

Pittsburgh Fire Fighters, Local No. 1 v. Yablonsky


No. 497 M.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


867 A.2d 666; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 30; 176 L.R.R.M. 3181


December 8, 2004, Argued  
January 25, 2005, Decided  
January 25, 2005, Filed



DISPOSITION: Defendants' preliminary objections sustained. Complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner firefighters' labor union filed a declaratory judgment action against respondents, a city, its mayor, several state officials, and a limited liability company, after the city adopted a fiscal recovery plan pursuant to the Municipalities Financial Recovery Act (Act 47), 1987 Pa. Laws 246, as amended, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, §§ 11701.101-.501. Respondents filed preliminary objections to the union's second amended complaint.


OVERVIEW: The city adopted a financial recovery plan, including future changes in the city's collective bargaining agreement (CBA) with the union. The union filed suit pursuant to the Declaratory Judgment Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 7501-7541, seeking a declaration that § 252 of Act 47, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 11701.252, was invalid and did not apply to arbitration awards entered pursuant to the Act of June 24, 1968, 1968 Pa. Laws 237, No. 111, as amended, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 43, § 217.4, which governed collective bargaining between the city and the union. Respondents attacked the union's complaint on legal sufficiency, ripeness, and jurisdictional grounds. The court sustained the objections. The non-city respondents could not be held liable for the city's plan. The fact that the city had helped the coordinator prepare the plan did not render it invalid. There was no ripe and justiciable controversy; the court could not determine whether the plan would actually impact the CBA until after the collective bargaining and arbitration processes. The court did not have original jurisdiction under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 761(a)(1).


OUTCOME: The court sustained respondents' objections and dismissed the union's complaint.

Roselle v. DOT, Bureau of Driver Licensing


No. 850 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


865 A.2d 308; 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 3


November 19, 2004, Submitted  
January 4, 2005, Decided  
January 4, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 04-00309. Common Pleas Court of the County of Chester. Platt, J.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to the Pennsylvania Driver's License Compact, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1581, appellant Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing (DOT), suspended appellee licensee's driving privileges for one year due to his conviction of driving under the influence of alcohol (DUI) in Delaware. The Chester County Common Pleas Court (Pennsylvania) granted the licensee's appeal. The DOT sought review.


OVERVIEW: The DOT submitted a copy of a traffic complaint, issued by an alderman's court in Delaware, which indicated that the licensee pled guilty to DUI. Because the complaint did not come from Delaware's licensing authority, the trial court concluded the DOT failed prove an out-of-state conviction under the Compact. The appellate court disagreed. Even an out-of-state conviction report that did not strictly comply with the Compact's requirements required the DOT to suspend the person's license if the conviction qualified for suspension under the Compact. Under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1586, an out-of-state conviction for any level of impaired driving was punishable in Pennsylvania; therefore, Newark, Del., Mun. Code § 20-57(a) (prohibiting driving while under the influence of alcohol) was substantially similar to the offense described the Compact and to Pennsylvania DUI laws. The DOT was not obliged to publish the Compact in the Pennsylvania Code or Pennsylvania Bulletin in order for it to become effective. The licensee argued that he was placed in Delaware's First Offender Program and thus was not "convicted" of DUI, but he failed to meet his burden to prove this claim.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

Nelson v. State Bd. of Veterinary Med.


No. 1216 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


863 A.2d 129; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 875


September 17, 2004, Submitted on briefs  
December 7, 2004, Decided  
December 7, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at, Remanded by Nelson v. State Bd. of Veterinary Med., 2007 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 702 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Dec. 17, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 1193-57-03 State Agency State Board of Veterinary Medicine.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner veterinarian (doctor) appealed the ruling from respondent State Board of Veterinary Medicine (Pennsylvania), which held that the doctor had demonstrated "professional incompetence" by verbally harassing an animal patient's owner in violation of § 21(20) of the Veterinary Medicine Practice Act (Act), 63 P.S. § 485.1-485.35. The Board ordered a public reprimand of the doctor and ordered him to complete a continuing education course.


OVERVIEW: The dog owner's 17-year old dog was blind in one eye and had difficulty breathing. The owner, a 76 year-old woman, had made several appointments for a euthanasia procedure in accordance with the doctor's long-standing recommendation, but she changed her mind each time she arrived at the doctor's office. Finally, the doctor performed the procedure, with the owner in attendance. The dog howled and collapsed, and the dog owner yelled at the doctor accusing him of killing her dog. She filed a complaint with the Department of State, Bureau of Enforcement and Investigations (Department) to complain about the manner in which her dog had been euthanized. When a Department investigator met with the doctor, the doctor phoned the dog owner and yelled at her rudely. The Department issued an order alleging that the doctor's conduct in verbally harassing the owner violated the Act. The Board found that the doctor had demonstrated "professional incompetence." On appeal, the court reversed, finding that the doctor's rude behavior did not equal professional incompetence under the Act, rejecting the Board's attempt to treat unprofessional conduct and professional incompetency as identical concepts.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the Board's ruling.

Boyertown Area Sch. Dist. v. Dep't of Educ.


No. 369 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


861 A.2d 418; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 817


June 10, 2004, Argued  
November 10, 2004, Decided  
November 10, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] State Agency Department of Education.
Boyertown Area Sch. Dist. v. Dep't of Educ., 797 A.2d 421, 2002 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 266 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner school districts filed suit against respondents, the Pennsylvania Department of Education and the Acting Secretary of Education, challenging the Department's withholding of portions of payments of the districts' state educational subsidies pursuant to § 1725-A (Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 24, § 17-1725-A) of the Pennsylvania Charter School Law.


OVERVIEW: The Secretary withheld payment based on the districts' alleged failure to make statutorily required payments to certain charter schools. The action was initially reversed because the Department's withholding of payments under § 1725-A(a)(5) of the Pennsylvania Charter School Law was an "adjudication," thereby obliging the Secretary to grant the districts a hearing pursuant to 2 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 504. After this ruling, the Department established an eight-step procedure to provide school districts with notice and an opportunity to be heard on the merits of such withholding. The districts did not participate in this procedure, and the Secretary again withheld the funds. The appellate court held the remand order did not contemplate the districts' forced participation in an informal paper process that the Department crafted after the remand. The remand order required nothing less than what § 504 allowed, which included a stenographic record of the proceedings. The opportunity for a party to develop an evidentiary record for judicial review was a fundamental part of the hearing process.


OUTCOME: The Secretary's order was reversed and the case was remanded to the Department for further proceedings.

Rising Sun Entm't, Inc. v. Pa. Liquor Control Bd.


No. 1748 C.D. 2002


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


860 A.2d 1193; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 810


March 1, 2004, Submitted on Briefs  
November 5, 2004, Decided  
November 5, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 3607 May Term 2001. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Judge CARRAFIELLO.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: An administrative law judge (ALJ) found that appellant bar had permitted lewd, immoral, or improper entertainment on its premises and that it had illegally discounted alcoholic beverages. The Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board affirmed the ALJ's decision. The Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) affirmed the Board's decision. The bar appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the Board argued that the bar waived its free expression challenge by failing to raise the issue below. The appellate court concluded that the issue of free expression was litigated sufficiently in the trial court so as to create a record adequate for appellate review. Issuing a liquor license on the condition that a licensee would not permit lewd, immoral, or improper entertainment on its premises did not violate the free expression provisions of either the United States or Pennsylvania Constitutions. The Twenty-First Amendment to the United States Constitution gave the states the absolute power over the conditions under which liquor was sold within their borders and even over whether it could be sold at all. Thus, the ban on lewd, improper, or immoral entertainment in the form of topless dancing in establishments licensed by the Board did not infringe on the rights guaranteed by Pa. Const. art. 1, § 7. However, the bar offered evidence that the language in 40 Pa. Code § 1301 was subject to more than one possible reading. The regulation was vague and unenforceable because it did not establish what a licensee was permitted to do and what was forbidden.


OUTCOME: The decision of the trial court was affirmed on the issue of lewd, immoral, or improper entertainment and reversed on the issue of discounting alcoholic beverages.

Northern Tier Solid Waste Auth. v. Dep't of Revenue


No. 876 M.D. 2002


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


860 A.2d 1173; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 807


September 9, 2004, Argued  
November 5, 2004, Decided  
November 5, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by, Application denied by N. Tier Solid Waste Auth. v. Commonwealth, 585 Pa. 462, 889 A.2d 38, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 2763 (2005)
Application denied by N. Tier Solid Waste Auth. v. Commonwealth, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 354, 919 A.2d 184 (Pa., Feb. 20, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.
N. Tier Solid Waste Auth. v. Dep't of Revenue, 825 A.2d 793, 2003 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 387 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Petitioner's Motion for summary relief is DENIED and DISMISSED. Further, the Motions for summary judgment filed by Respondents and by Intervenor and GRANTED in part and DENIED in part; specifically, the Motions are GRANTED as to Counts I and V and DENIED as to Counts III and IV.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners county solid waste authorities filed a motion for summary relief, seeking a declaration of exemption from a waste disposal fee and an injunction against its collection. Respondents, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Department of Revenue and Department of Environmental Protection (DEP), and intervenor waste industries association, each sought summary judgment declaring that the authorities were subject to the waste disposal fee.


OVERVIEW: The authorities operated landfills under permits issued by the DEP. They asserted that they were municipal corporations entitled to a tax exemption as provided by the Municipality Authorities Act, 53 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5601-5622, and that the disposal fee imposed by 27 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6301 of Act 90, 27 Pa. Const. Stat. §§ 4101-4113, 6201-6306, was a tax despite its designation as a "fee." Respondents argued that the plain language of Act 90 did not exempt the authorities, as the fee was on the disposal of solid waste, not on the solid waste itself. The court agreed with respondents and found that the practice under Act 90 was the same for all practical purposes as the practice under the pre-existing statutes, the Municipal Waste Planning, Recycling and Waste Reduction Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, §§ 4000.101-4000.1904, and the Environmental Stewardship and Watershed Protection Act, 27 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 6101-6113; the authorities were collecting and remitting the Act 90 disposal fees to the DEP, which did not treat publicly owned and privately owned landfills differently. The tax exemption provided under the Municipal Authorities Act did not apply to Act 90.


OUTCOME: The court denied and dismissed petitioners' motion and granted summary judgment to respondents and intervenor, declaring that the authorities were subject to the waste disposal fee.

Johnston v. Twp. of Plumcreek


No. 174 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


859 A.2d 7; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 708


May 21, 2004, Submitted on Briefs  
September 28, 2004, Decided  
September 28, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Johnston v. Twp. of Plumcreek, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 1218 (Pa., June 8, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 2003-0429. Common Pleas Court of the County of Armstrong, Judge VALASEK.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant residents challenged an order of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Armstrong (Pennsylvania), which dismissed their complaint. The residents brought suit challenging the constitutionality of a township ordinance requiring them to connect to a public water system. The trial court sustained the preliminary objections filed by appellees, a water authority, two townships, and a county.


OVERVIEW: The trial court determined the residents failed to state a cause of action in their complaint. The residents asserted that as a result of the terrorist attacks on September 11, 2001, and the nation's war on terrorism, there was a real and present danger of terrorist attacks on public water systems and that an ordinance mandating such a connection violated their substantive due process rights guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend. XIV and their right to life guaranteed by Pa. Const. art. I, § 1. The court disagreed and noted that the residents' complaint failed to allege a direct, real, or immediate threat of harm, that it merely provided conjecture, and that no authority was cited to support their proposition that the right to privacy included the right to refuse to connect to a public water supply. The court pointed out that the ordinance did not force the ingestion of water from an unknown source, as it only required the residents to hook up to the water authority's system, not use the water. The court also established that the appropriate standard for considering the constitutionality of the ordinances was the minimum rationality standard.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.

Bergdoll v. Commonwealth

COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


858 A.2d 185; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 698

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorneys sought a declaration voiding amendments to Pa. Const. art. I, § 9 (Face to Face Amendment) and Pa. Const. art. OUTCOME: The court overruled the General Assembly's objection as to the justiciability of the attorneys' claims but then sustained the remaining preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer and dismissed the attorney's petition.

Stein v. DOT, Bureau of Driver Licensing


No. 271 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


857 A.2d 719; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 650


May 28, 2004, Submitted  
September 8, 2004, Decided  
September 8, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended September 10, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 2003-C-1513. Common Pleas Court of the County of Lehigh. Judge WALLITSCH.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing, suspended appellant licensee's driving privilege after he refused to submit to chemical testing in violation of 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1547. The Common Pleas Court of the County of Lehigh, Pennsylvania, affirmed. The licensee sought further review.


OVERVIEW: An officer testified that the licensee's car crossed over the center line and drifted into the oncoming lane of traffic before making a sharp left-hand turn. The officer stopped the car; the licensee emitted a strong odor of alcohol, had bloodshot and glassy eyes, slurred his speech, and failed sobriety tests. The officer arrested him for driving under the influence; the licensee refused to submit to chemical testing. The licensee argued that the officer lacked authority to stop him outside his jurisdiction. The appellate court disagreed. Under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8953(a)(4), the officer properly left his jurisdiction to arrest the licensee because the chief of the police departments of his town and the town where the arrest occurred had authorized the officers of the other department to enforce the traffic laws on the street in question. Further, the officer was in "hot pursuit" under § 8953(a)(2), and under § 8953(a)(5), the licensee presented a clear and present danger to persons or property. The licensee's erratic driving gave the officer probable cause to stop him, and even if it did not, his privileges could be suspended as suspension was a civil proceeding.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Lawson v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd. (Temple Univ.)


No. 2172 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


857 A.2d 222; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 649


June 9, 2004, Argued  
September 7, 2004, Decided  
September 7, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Lawson v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd. (Temple Univ.), 2005 Pa. LEXIS 240 (Pa., Feb. 11, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. A02-3146. Workers' Compensation Appeal Board.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Workers' Compensation Appeal Board (Pennsylvania) affirmed the decision of a workers' compensation judge (WCJ) that approved a settlement agreement between petitioner claimant and respondent employer and awarded counsel fees of 20 percent of the claimant's award, while denying the attorney's application for counsel fees of 33 1/3 percent. The attorney appealed the fee award.


OVERVIEW: The claimant testified that she had agreed to a counsel fee of 33 1/3 percent, and the attorney testified to the amount of work he had performed on the claimant's behalf. The WCJ found that the attorney failed to show good cause for allowing a counsel fee greater than the 20 percent provided by Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77, § 998. The attorney alleged that § 998 violated Pa. Const. art. V, 10(c). The court held that the Board did not err in affirming the WCJ's determination that the documentary material and arguments proffered by the attorney did not support an attorney fee in excess of 20 percent. And as Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77, § 998 did not bar a WCJ from permitting a higher fee agreement if the attorney showed that such a fee was commensurate with the attorney's efforts, and § 998 was not inconsistent with any other rule promulgated by the Pennsylvania Supreme Court that regulated the conduct of attorneys, it did not violate Pa. Const. art. V, 10(c) by usurping the supreme court's authority to regulate attorney conduct and legal practice, including counsel fees.


OUTCOME: The decision of the Board was affirmed.

Pa. Tpk. Comm'n v. Commonwealth


No. 641 M.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


855 A.2d 923; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 574; 175 L.R.R.M. 2491


May 3, 2004, Argued  
July 30, 2004, Decided  
July 30, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Pa. Tpk. Comm'n v. AG, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 996 (Pa., June 19, 2006)



DISPOSITION: Commission's motion for summary judgment granted; Union's motion for summary judgment denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Pennsylvania Turnpike Commission moved for summary judgment seeking a declaration that the First-Level Supervisor Collective Bargaining Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 43, §§ 1103.101-.701, was unconstitutional as a special law. Respondent union moved for summary judgment on the basis that the Act was constitutional. Respondent Commonwealth of Pennsylvania also sought a judgment in its favor in opposition to the Commission.


OVERVIEW: The Act applied only to the Commission and not to other public employers. It provided that the Commission was to engage in collective bargaining with its first-level supervisors and that collective bargaining was to begin at least six months before the start of the fiscal year of the Commission. The Commission asserted that the Act violated Pa. Const. art. III, § 32, which prohibited the enactment of special laws. The Commission argued that the Act was unconstitutional because there was no rational reason to treat the Commission differently from all other public employees. Applying equal protection principles, the court agreed. The Act singled out the Commission and did not pertain to any other public employer. The court could discern no rational reason to treat first-level supervisors of the Commission differently from first-level supervisors employed by any other public employer. Thus, the court agreed with the Commission that the Act violated Pa. Const. art. III, § 32, which made the Act unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The Commission's motion for summary judgment was granted, and the union's motion for summary judgment was denied.

Dauphin County Soc. Servs. for Children & Youth v. Dep't of Pub. Welfare


No. 2631 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


855 A.2d 159; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 575


March 30, 2004, Argued  
July 30, 2004, Decided  
July 30, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] State Agency Department of Public Welfare. Appealed From No. 21-03-273.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Dauphin County Social Services for Children and Youth (SSCY) sought review of an order of respondent Pennsylvania Bureau of Hearings and Appeals (Bureau) of the Department of Public Welfare (DPW), which granted a motion by intervenor, an indicated sexual abuse perpetrator, to compel discovery.


OVERVIEW: A SSCY investigation indicated that intervenor had sexually abused a three-year-old. His counsel requested a hearing, and intervenor sought discovery. An initial motion compelling discovery was entered. Intervenor filed a second motion, asserting that he was entitled to discovery of additional information. The Bureau's second order provided intervenor with access to all information in the SSCY file relative to the investigation, including confidential information. The court reversed. The Bureau's reliance as a basis for its order on the right to confrontation found in Pa. Const. art. I, § 9 and the Sixth Amendment was misplaced. The right to confrontation applied only in criminal cases and the expunction proceeding was civil in nature. There was no dispute that intervenor had received all information due him under 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6340(b). However, under § 6340(a), he was not one of the types of persons entitled to receive confidential information identified in 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6339, and further, release of that information in the judicial context was limited to certain criminal proceedings. The Bureau's order allowing otherwise was improper.


OUTCOME: The order compelling discovery was reversed and the matter was remanded for further proceedings.

Glen-Gery Corp. v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


No. 155 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


856 A.2d 884; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 567


June 10, 2004, Argued  
July 28, 2004, Decided  
July 28, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied September 17, 2004. Appeal granted by, in part Glen-Gery Corp. v. Zoning Hearing Bd., 584 Pa. 132, 882 A.2d 461, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 1684 (2005)
Reversed by, Remanded by Glen-Gery Corp. v. Zoning Hearing Bd. of Dover Twp., 589 Pa. 135, 907 A.2d 1033, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1894 (Pa., Sept. 28, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 2003-SU-02356-08 Common Pleas Court of the County of York. Judge BLACKWELL.



DISPOSITION: (1) Intervenor's motion to dismiss Glen Gery's above-captioned appeal as moot is DENIED; (2) the December 22, 2003 order of the Court of Common Pleas of York County is AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation appealed the order of the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania), which denied the corporation's appeal from an order of appellee township zoning hearing board (ZHB) dismissing the corporation's procedural challenge to the validity of a zoning ordinance and a subdivision and land development ordinance. Appellee township filed a motion to dismiss the corporation's appeal.


OVERVIEW: Regarding the motion to dismiss, the corporation argued that under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 11006-A, a court could declare any ordinance invalid and set it aside regardless of whether the applicant had submitted plans or other material in the form required for final approval. The appellate court concluded that the corporation's proposal to use its property for non-coal surface mining and related accessory uses was sufficient to establish a right under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 11006-A to appeal the ZHB's denial of its challenge to the two ordinances. In addition, the ZHB did not violate the corporation's constitutional right to due process by holding a hearing solely on the jurisdictional issue because the jurisdictional issue had to be resolved before the ZHB could reach the merits of the challenge. Also, the ZHB did not err in determining that 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5571(c)(5) barred the challenge as untimely because the challenge was filed more than four years after the last previous enactment of the zoning ordinance and more than seven years after the last previous enactment of the subdivision ordinance. The statute unequivocally stated that it applied to land use ordinances.


OUTCOME: The motion to dismiss the corporation's appeal was denied. The order of the trial court was affirmed.

 

1949 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Shapiro v. State Bd. of Accountancy


No. 2452 C.D. 2003, No. 2453 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


856 A.2d 864; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 560


April 30, 2004, Submitted on Briefs  
July 26, 2004, Decided  
July 26, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Shapiro v. State Bd. of Accountancy, 582 Pa. 712, 872 A.2d 174, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 733 (2005)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Shapiro v. Pa. State Bd. of Accountancy, 126 S. Ct. 376, 163 L. Ed. 2d 163, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 5757 (U.S., 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From Nos. 0912-55-01 & 0916-55-01. State Agency: State Board of Accountancy.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: From 1988 through 1997, petitioner accountants and their corporations sold approximately $ 60 million worth of unsecured debentures to 7,000 investors nationwide. The accountants sought review of the order of the Pennsylvania State Board of Accountancy, which revoked the first accountant's certificate of certified public accountant and biennial license and revoked the second accountant's certificate of certified public accountant.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the accountants raised numerous issues. The appellate court concluded that Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 63, § 2203(d), which required hearings to be held within 90 days after the date on which an answer was filed, was directory rather than mandatory. Thus, the cases did not have to be dismissed just because the Board failed to hold a hearing within 90 days after the accountants filed their answers. The Board did not err in finding that the accountants' right to practice before the Pennsylvania Securities Commission (PSC) was revoked. The accountants were barred from registration as broker-dealers under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 70, § 1-301 and could not transact business as broker-dealers in Pennsylvania or appear before the PSC in that capacity. The Board's finding that the accountants engaged in unprofessional conduct under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 63, § 9.9a(a)(16) was not erroneous where the accountants violated 64 Pa. Code § 305.019 by offering and selling certificates to investors without disclosing that the offers and sales were made in willful violation of Pennsylvania law. Finally, the accountants were collaterally estopped from relitigating the facts of the PSC adjudication.


OUTCOME: The Board's order was affirmed.

 

1950 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Kerbeck Cadillac Pontiac, Inc. v. State Bd. of Vehicle Mfrs.


No. 1804 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


854 A.2d 663; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 550


June 8, 2004, Submitted  
July 22, 2004, Decided  
July 22, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] State Agency State Board of Vehicle Manufacturers, Dealers and Salespersons.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an out-of-state new vehicle dealer, petitioned for review of an order of respondent Pennsylvania State Board of Vehicle Manufacturers, Dealers, and Salespersons, which determined that the dealer violated Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 63, § 818.32(c), imposed a civil penalty in the amount of $ 1,000, and directed that the dealer cease and desist from participation in vehicle shows, off-premise sales or exhibitions in Pennsylvania.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the dealer argued that (1) Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 63, § 818.32(c) did not preclude it from displaying vehicles at an event unless there were 49 similar dealers at the event; (2) the Commonwealth did not establish that less than 50 new vehicle dealers participated at the event; and (3) Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 63, § 818.32(c) violated the Commerce Clause of the United States Constitution. The appellate court concluded that the Board did not err when it determined that Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 63, § 818.32(c) prohibited the dealer from displaying its vehicles at the event because the statute clearly stated that an out-of-state new vehicle dealer could participate in shows where there were at least 50 new vehicle dealers participating as exhibitors. The Board's finding was supported by substantial evidence where the dealer was the only new vehicle dealer to participate at the event from 1999 through 2002. Because in-state new vehicle dealers were equally as burdened as out-of-state new vehicle dealers, the statute did not facially discriminate against interstate commerce. The statute was enacted for the legitimate purpose of protecting the Pennsylvania consumer from fraud and deception.


OUTCOME: The Board's order was affirmed.

 

1951 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Skonieczny v. Dep't of Cmty. & Econ. Dev.


No. 543 C.D. 2004, No. 585 C.D. 2004, No. 595 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


853 A.2d 1172; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 543


June 25, 2004, Submitted  
July 20, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Skonieczny v. Dep't of Cmty. & Econ. Dev., 2005 Pa. LEXIS 1060 (Pa., May 20, 2005)



DISPOSITION:  [**1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners appealed from an order of respondent Department of Community and Economic Development (Pennsylvania), which dismissed each of their individual actions that challenged the debt proceedings of a borough, and which were held under the Local Government Unit Debt Act, 53 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 8001-8271.


OVERVIEW: A borough enacted an ordinance authorizing the incurrence of indebtedness pursuant to the Debt Act with respect to changes to its sanitary collection and treatment system. The three petitioners filed individual complaints, challenging the legality of the debt proceedings. Each of the actions were dismissed, and appeals were taken. The court deferred to the Department's interpretation of 53 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8022, wherein it held that a simultaneous incurrence and exclusion of debt was acceptable, and it found that preliminary cost estimates pursuant to 53 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8006 were not required because the costs of the original project were not involved, but rather, a refunding of the financing. The lack of a hearing on two of the petitioners' claims of fraud was proper, as allegations to support the claims were insufficient. The mayor's failure to sign the ordinance at the meeting was not a fatal defect because it was not vetoed. The borough was not required to provide one of the petitioners with the full text of the ordinance under 53 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8003(a), and accordingly, her claim that it was not lawfully enacted failed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the Department.

Burns v. Public Sch. Emples. Ret. Bd.


No 19 C.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


853 A.2d 1146; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 532


June 11, 2004, Argued

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner former teacher appealed the order of respondent Public School Employees' Retirement Board, which denied him a disability annuity due to his failure to apply within the statutorily mandated period.


OVERVIEW: The teacher worked as a public school teacher for 25 years. He was diagnosed with Multiple Sclerosis in 1993 and depression in 1995. His condition progressively worsened, and a few months before his last day of work in 1999, he met with a counselor from the Public School Employees' Retirement System (PSERS) to discuss his retirement alternatives. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that the provisions of the Public School Employees' Retirement Code, 24 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 8102- 8535, which defined "active member" and "inactive member" informed an ordinary person of their meanings and that the fact that the Code granted certain benefits to vestees and certain other rights to active and inactive members did not make it unconstitutionally vague.
