Popowsky v. Pa. PUC


No. 1995 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


853 A.2d 1097; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 495


March 31, 2004, Argued  
July 13, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended July 14, 2004.
Appeal granted by Popowsky v. Pa. PUC, 582 Pa. 667, 868 A.2d 454, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 250 (2005)
Affirmed by Popowsky v. Pa. PUC, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 2261 (Pa., Nov. 21, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From No. C-00015377, C-20028177 & C-20028361. State Agency Public Utility Commission.



DISPOSITION:  [**1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, adopted the recommendation of an administrative law judge to dismiss complaints filed against a water utility. The complainants requested the Commission to order the utility to install, at the utility's sole expense, water mains and a storage tank. The Commission held that his relief was not available to the complainants. Petitioner consumer advocate sought review.


OVERVIEW: On review, the consumer advocate raised several issues challenging the Commission's decision that township residents had to contribute to the cost of extending the utility's main to the township. The consumer advocate argued that where a need for public utility service was demonstrated, a utility had to invest in an extension of service even where the utility would experience a negative return on this investment. This shortfall would have to be borne by the utility's shareholders or by its existing customers, who already generated profits for the utility. The appellate court disagreed, holding that the consumer advocate could not show the Commission had exercised its statutory authority unlawfully. The standards adopted by the Commission, after much care and research, balanced the competing interests affected by a service extension. The line extension regulations did not conflict with 66 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1501 and, thus, had the force and effect of law. Further, they were properly applied. The Commission did not relieve the utility from having to extend service in the township, but only from having to do so on the conditions sought by the complainants.


OUTCOME: The Commission's adjudication was affirmed.

Dorsey v. Pa. Bd. of Prob. & Parole


No. 2813 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


854 A.2d 994; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 493


June 4, 2004, Submitted on Briefs  
July 9, 2004, Decided  
July 9, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Dorsey v. Pa. Bd. of Prob. & Parole, 2004 Pa. LEXIS 3235 (Pa., Dec. 22, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. Parole No. 9888-V. State Agency PA Board of Probation and Parole.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate appealed the order of respondent Pennsylvania Board of Probation and Parole, which denied the inmate's request for administrative relief and upheld the decision of the Board recalculating his maximum expiration date to April 14, 2005.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the inmate argued that the Board incorrectly recalculated his maximum date and that Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 61, § 331.21a was void because it was unconstitutionally vague. The precise question was the effect of an intervening recommitment for technical violations only: was the street time prior to that technical recommitment insulated from forfeiture if a new crime was committed when the parolee was then re-paroled, or was that street time subject to forfeiture? Pennsylvania case law held that such street time was subject to forfeiture. In addition, the appellate court concluded that while it was true that Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 61, § 331.21a(a) did not specifically address the sanction for new criminal actions committed while on re-parole, that factor alone did not create a constitutional infirmity, particularly where there was no reason that the standard would be any different from that applied to an initial parole. The clear intent of the provision was to discourage the commission of new crimes while on either parole or re-parole by imposing a costly sanction upon offenders.


OUTCOME: The Board's order was affirmed.

Diwara v. State Bd. of Cosmetology


No. 2246 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


852 A.2d 1279; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 490


June 7, 2004, Argued  
July 1, 2004, Decided  
July 1, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 33206-45-01, 33235-45-01, 33244-45-01 & 33710-45-01. State Agency State Board of Cosmetology.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner shop owners filed a consolidated petition for review of an order of respondent Pennsylvania State Board of Cosmetology, which affirmed individual orders by a hearing examiner that the shop owners had violated Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 63, § 508, by operating cosmetology shops without licenses.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the shop owners argued that the Pennsylvania Beauty Culture Law did not apply to hair braiding, that the definition of cosmetology did not include the work performed by the shop owners, and that the application of the Law violated the shop owners' right to equal protection and substantive due process under the United States and Pennsylvania Constitutions. The appellate court concluded that the Board did not err in concluding that hair braiding was encompassed in the definition of cosmetology as set forth in Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 63, § 507 because braiding and adding extensions to hair qualified as embellishment, beautification, dressing or arranging of hair. In addition, the services provided by the shop owners met the definition of cosmetology. Also, the shop owners did not meet their burden in establishing that the Law was unconstitutional in its application because it was impossible for the appellate court to determine whether or not cosmetology schools provided curriculum rationally related to the shop owners' profession where none of the shop owners provided any testimony regarding the nature of their businesses.


OUTCOME: The Board's order was affirmed.

Conrad v. Commonwealth


631 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


856 A.2d 199; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 637


January 23, 2004, Submitted on Briefs  
June 29, 2004 *Previous Non-reported Opinion*, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 02-7621-17-6 Common Pleas Court of the County of Bucks. Judge CLARK, S.J.
Conrad v. DOT, Bureau of Driver Licensing, 2003 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 871 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Dec. 5, 2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing, appealed the order of the Bucks County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania) that found that appellee driver was not required to comply with the Pennsylvania Ignition Interlock Law, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7001 et seq. The appellate court initially affirmed. It subsequently granted the Department's application for reconsideration.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to the driver's second drunk driving conviction, the Department notified the driver that he was required to have all vehicles owned by him equipped with an ignition interlock system in order for his operating privileges to be restored at the end of his one-year license suspension. Pursuant to Pennsylvania caselaw, the Department was not permitted to require the driver to install the device in all of his vehicles. The driver asserted that, irrespective of the Department's authority, his plea bargain stated that he would not be subject to ignition interlock requirements. The trial court and district attorney in the driver's criminal case lacked the authority to approve the plea bargain with such a provision. Finally, the driver argued that the Department was prohibited from considering his acceptance of accelerated rehabilitative disposition as a conviction for the purposes of the Pennsylvania Ignition Interlock Law, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7001 et seq. The issue was unpreserved, as the trial court did not address whether the Department's imposition of the Law was impermissibly retroactive.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed insofar as the driver was restricted to an ignition interlock restricted license as a condition of the restoration of his operating privilege following his service of the one-year suspension. The judgment was otherwise affirmed.

Alliance Home v. Bd. of Assessment Appeals


No. 595 C.D. 2002


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


852 A.2d 428; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 473


March 31, 2004, Argued  
June 15, 2004, Decided  
June 15, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by, in part Alliance Home of Carlisle v. Bd. of Assessment Appeals, 580 Pa. 562, 862 A.2d 590, 2004 Pa. LEXIS 2963 (2004)
Reversed by Alliance Home of Carlisle v. Bd. of Assessment Appeals, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 844 (Pa., Apr. 17, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 01-5659 Civil Term. Common Pleas Court of the County of Cumberland. Judge BAYLEY.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County, dated January 31, 2002, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant institution challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County (Pennsylvania), which denied its request to extend a charitable real estate tax exemption to a parcel of property on which its independent living section was located. The institution had brought suit against appellees, a Board of Assessment Appeals, a school district, a borough, and a county, to challenge the original denial of the institution's request.


OVERVIEW: The Board of Assessment Appeals denied the institution's request after concluding that it was res judicata based on prior litigation with the institution that ended with a denial of the request. The institution had again petitioned the Board of Assessment Appeals after the enactment of the Institutions of Purely Public Charity Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 10, §§ 371-385, which provided that an institution was entitled to a presumption under the Act that it was an institution of purely public charity and the parcel consisting of the independent living apartments was exempt from real estate taxes as a purely public charity. The trial court, and the court, agreed that the institution was entitled to the rebuttable presumption under the Act, but that the institution was still not entitled to the exemption because there was no evidence that it was donating a substantial portion of its services to the residents living in the independent living apartments. As a result, the institution did not meet the definition of a purely public charity. The court noted that it did not matter that the facilities were all located on the same property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

1958 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Kleinfelter v. Comm'rs of the County of Dauphin


No. 2639 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


850 A.2d 894; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 421


March 31, 2004, Argued  
June 4, 2004, Decided  
June 4, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended June 7, 2004.
Appeal denied by Teamsters Local 776 v. Kleinfelter, 2004 Pa. LEXIS 3335 (Pa., Dec. 29, 2004)



DISPOSITION:  [**1] Article 10, Section 9 STRICKEN from arbitration award issued.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a judge representing the judges of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania) (judges), sought review of an arbitration award in a contract dispute between the judges and a bargaining unit of court-appointed professional employees. The judges sought to have a clause that required mandatory, non-binding arbitration of non-economic matters for members of the bargaining unit stricken from the award.


OVERVIEW: Respondent county commissioners bargained on behalf of the judges with regard to the collective bargaining agreement (CBA). The judges objected to the inclusion of any clause requiring mandatory arbitration on non-economic matters in the CBA because such a procedure would violate the statutory and constitutional authority of the courts to hire, supervise, discipline and discharge personnel they appointed. The issue went to arbitration and resulted in an arbitration award that mandated advisory arbitration of non-economic matters for members of the bargaining unit. The court struck the clause. While Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 16, § 1620 vested the commissioners with exclusive bargaining authority, that authority did not diminish the right of courts to hire, supervise and discharge their employees, and no collective bargaining agreement could interfere with the judges' authority to do so. The fact that the arbitration clause in question was merely advisory did not negate the fact that the procedure interfered with the judges' inherent authority by inquiry into their decision making process. As the clause violated both statutory and prior case law, it was void.


OUTCOME: The clause requiring advisory arbitration of non-economic personnel matters was stricken from the CBA.

Krichmar v. State Bd. of Vehicle Mfrs., Dealers & Sales Persons


No. 2705 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


850 A.2d 861; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 413


April 16, 2004, Submitted on Briefs  
May 28, 2004, Decided  
May 28, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 1266-60-2002. State Board of Vehicle Manufacturers, Dealers and Salespersons.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner vehicle salesman sought review of an order of respondent State Board of Vehicle Manufacturers, Dealers and Salespersons (Pennsylvania), which fined him $ 1,000 and suspended his vehicle salesperson's license for an indefinite period, but not less than six months, and set conditions for reinstatement.


OVERVIEW: The suspension and fine were imposed after the salesman pleaded guilty to insurance fraud. Reinstatement could be made only after a showing that the salesman was sufficiently remorseful and had become sufficiently rehabilitated to justify reinstatement. That restriction was based on responses the salesman made to questions posed during his disciplinary hearing where he did not admit guilt but indicated he pleaded guilty because he wanted to avoid a lengthy prison sentence and because he wanted to avoid the expenses associated with trial. The salesman's appeal concerned the length of the suspension and the requirements for reinstatement. The court affirmed the Board's decision, holding that (1) the Board had authority under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 63, § 818.21 to either revoke or suspend a license and the authority to revoke included the lesser power to suspend the license indefinitely for at least six months; (2) there was no statutory limit on conditions for reinstatement and the requirement for credible proof of remorse and rehabilitation was reasonable; and (3) the conditions for reinstatement were within the Board's authority and were not impermissibly vague.


OUTCOME: The Board's decision was affirmed.

Mager v. State Emples. Ret. Bd.


No. 1677 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


849 A.2d 287; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 377


February 3, 2004, Argued  
May 11, 2004, Decided  
May 11, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Mager v. State Emples. Ret. Bd., 2004 Pa. LEXIS 2215 (Pa., Sept. 24, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 2002-08 State Agency State Employees Retirement Board.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner parents petitioned the Pennsylvania State Employees' Retirement System (SERS) to change the beneficiary on their deceased son's account. The SERS directed that his death benefit be paid to his last named beneficiary, his former wife. The parents appealed. Respondent Pennsylvania State Employees' Retirement Board denied the request and directed that the decedent's death benefit be paid to his former wife. The parents appealed.


OVERVIEW: Before his death, the decedent was employed by the Pennsylvania Department of Transportation and was a member of the SERS. When he got married, the decedent designated his then wife to be his beneficiary. Later, the decedent and his wife divorced. After his divorce, the decedent named his parents as his will beneficiaries and transferred property to them. His parents provided him with care and support for his alcoholism. The decedent's ex-wife did not visit or support him. The last nomination of beneficiary form in the decedent's SERS file designated his ex-wife as principal beneficiary of his SERS retirement account. The parents claimed that 20 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6111.2 applied, and that the parents should therefore by operation of law be the beneficiary of the SERS retirement account. The court found that applying 20 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 6111.2 retroactively to the matter would impair constitutionally protected contractual rights. Notwithstanding the possible inequity of the result, the only writing in evidence was the beneficiary nomination form designating the decedent's former wife as the beneficiary of his retirement benefits. Therefore, the Board's decision was proper.


OUTCOME: The determination was affirmed.

Beattie v. Allegheny County


No. 1008 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


847 A.2d 185; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 301


February 4, 2004, Argued  
April 15, 2004, Decided  
April 15, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by Beattie v. Allegheny County, 582 Pa. 702, 871 A.2d 193, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 646 (2005)
Affirmed by Beattie v. Allegheny County, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 1952 (Pa., Oct. 11, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. GD 01-11149. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Judge WETTICK.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers challenged the decision of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny, which, in their class action, sustained preliminary objections filed by appellees, a county and others. The taxpayers' complaint, which was filed in equity, challenged the constitutionality of the county's property assessment under the Uniformity Clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1, and sought declaratory, injunctive, and monetary relief.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers asserted that the county had failed to follow a standard adopted by it in its Assessment Standards and Practices Ordinance and that the assessment was not uniform. Affirming the trial court's decision, the court held that (1) the trial court did not have equity jurisdiction over the taxpayers' Uniformity Clause challenge because, even if it assumed that the taxpayers raised a substantial constitutional question and established a discriminatory effect, they failed to establish that no adequate legal remedy existed where they had not alleged that the Board of Assessment Appeals could not re-evaluate the total millage in an assessment appeal and where the statutory remedy could accommodate a multitude of tax assessment issues; (2) because Pennsylvania's administrative process for challenging tax assessments provided the taxpayers with an adequate state remedy, the trial court did not have subject matter jurisdiction to consider the taxpayers' 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 action; and (3) the taxpayers claims that the county failed to comply with standards imposed by ordinance presented a question of law and could be addressed in an appeal to the Board.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision.

 

1962 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Austin


No. 2656 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


846 A.2d 798; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 299


February 6, 2004, Submitted  
April 14, 2004, Decided  
April 14, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 602 C 2003. Common Pleas Court of the County of Westmoreland. Judge LOUGHRAN.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Westmoreland County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania) convicting defendant of the summary offense of harboring a dangerous dog in violation of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 3, 459-502-A.


OVERVIEW: The victim, whose business adjoined the property where defendant kept his dog, was walking on the rear section of her property when she heard rustling behind her. The victim turned to see defendant's dog just as it lunged at her and bit her leg. The dog knocked the victim to the ground and inflicted four puncture wounds. There was sufficient evidence to convict defendant of harboring a dangerous dog in violation of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 3, § 459-502-A. The evidence established beyond a reasonable doubt that the dog attacked the victim without provocation pursuant to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 3, § 459-502-A(a)(1)(iii), thereby demonstrating a propensity to attack human beings without provocation pursuant to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 3, § 459-502-A(a)(2)(ii), and that defendant was the owner of the dog pursuant to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 3, § 459-502-A(a)(3). Defendant argued on appeal that the Commonwealth had to prove that his dog inflicted a severe injury. Nothing in Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 3, § 459-502-A required a showing that the injury was severe.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

PPL Holtwood, LLC v. Pike County Bd. of Assessment & Revision of Taxes


Nos. 1668, 1690, 1691, and 1692 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


846 A.2d 201; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 251


March 1, 2004, Argued  
April 6, 2004, Decided  
April 6, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by PPL Holtwood, LLC v. Pike County Bd. of Assessment & Revision of Taxes, 2004 Pa. LEXIS 2401 (Pa., Oct. 15, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 1151 Civil-99, 453 Civil-99, 304 Civil-00, 305 Civil-00. Pike. THOMSON.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant utility company sought review from an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Pike County (Pennsylvania), which ordered the company to pay additional penalties and interest to appellee county boards of assessment and revision of taxes, based on the utility's original unpaid tax bills for a particular hydroelectric generating station, rather than on the final tax bills.


OVERVIEW: The station was located in two counties, which were governed by the Fourth to Eighth Class County Assessment Law (FECCAL), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, §§ 5453.101 to -.706, as well as the General County Assessment Law (GCAL), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, §§ 5020-1 to -602. The company appealed the assessments in each county, which resulted in increased collective assessments and a fair market value that was approximately 41 times greater than the combined fair market value prior to the appeal. During the pendency of the company's appeals, which were consolidated to one court, the company failed to pay additional taxes due. The question before the court was which assessment the penalties and costs were to be based on. The court used principles of statutory construction to determine that the trial court erred in determining that those amounts were to be based on the face amount of the unpaid tax bills. Such an interpretation would have resulted in different treatment, based on the differing procedure in § 17 of the Second Class County Assessment Law, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, §§ 5452.1 to -.20, specifically at Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 5452.17. It would have also led to an unreasonable result.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court and remanded the matter for calculation of the penalties and interest owed.

 

1964 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


English v. Commonwealth


No. 462 M.D. 2002


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


845 A.2d 999; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 240


January 16, 2004, Submitted  
March 31, 2004, Decided  
March 31, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: English v. Commonwealth, 816 A.2d 382, 2003 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 50 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2003)



DISPOSITION:  [**1] Preliminary objections of Commonwealth in the nature of a demurrer sustained. Petition for review dismissed with prejudice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania and several of its officers and officials, filed preliminary objections to petitioner's request for review, in the nature of an action in mandamus and bill of equity. The petition challenged the constitutionality of Act 77 of the Second Class County Code, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 16, §§ 6101-B to 6173-B, which created the Allegheny Regional Asset District (RAD).


OVERVIEW: RAD was an independent, special purpose unit of local government covering the geographic region of Allegheny County, and Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 16, § 6152-B authorized Allegheny County to levy a sales and use tax to carry out the purposes of RAD. Sustaining the Commonwealth's objections, the court held that (1) the allegations of the petition did not support a claim that Act 77 violated Pa. Const. art. I, § 2 by precluding citizens from challenging RAD through the agenda initiative and voter referendum process; (2) petitioner's allegations that amending the bill four times, adding 13 lines to its title, and adding 29 pages to its text were insufficient to warrant an inquiry by the court into the manner in which Act 77 was enacted under Pa. Const. art. III. §§ 1 and 3; (3) Act 77 did not authorize RAD to levy taxes, but, rather, the exclusive power to levy the RAD tax was vested in Allegheny County, pursuant to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 16, § 6152-B(a); accordingly, there was no violation of Pa. Const. art. III, § 31; and (4) the allegations failed to support a conclusion that any classification of taxpayers subject to the RAD tax was unreasonable under Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the preliminary objection in the nature of a demurrer and dismissed the petition, with prejudice.

 

1965 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Mankin v. DOT, Bureau of Driver Licensing


No. 691 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


845 A.2d 249; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 220


September 19, 2003, Submitted on Briefs  
March 24, 2004, Decided  
March 24, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 02-26163. Common Pleas Court of the County of Montgomery. Judge SMYTH.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed without prejudice to DOT's authority to issue restricted license upon restoration of Plaintiff's driving privileges.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing notified appellee licensee that his license was being suspended for one year and that he was required to have an ignition interlock system installed on all of his vehicles. The Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania) affirmed the license suspension but reversed the ignition installations. The Bureau appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court determined that the sole authority for ordering the installation of the ignition interlock device was vested with the trial court and, therefore, the Bureau did not have the authority to order the licensee to install an ignition interlock device. The appellate court affirmed the trial court's decision to sustain the licensee's appeal of the Bureau's directive to install an ignition interlock device on each of his vehicles, but on different grounds. The Ignition Interlock Law, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 7001-7003, after Mockaitis, did not require the installation of an ignition interlock device on a specific vehicle. It required the recidivist driving while intoxicated licensee to obtain a restricted license from the Bureau as a condition precedent to restoration of driving privileges. The Bureau retained the authority, pursuant to 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3805, to require the licensee to obtain a restricted license upon restoration of his driving privileges after suspension.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

1966 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


McDonald v. Commonwealth


No. 327 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


845 A.2d 221; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 211


August 22, 2003, Submitted  
March 16, 2004, Decided  
March 16, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 02-09788. Common Pleas Court of the County of Chester. Judge OTT.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee driver challenged appellant Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing's order that he install ignition interlock devices on his vehicles before his operating privileges were restored after a one-year court imposed suspension. The Common Pleas Court of the County of Chester (Pennsylvania) upheld the suspension, but sustained the driver's appeal of the ignition interlock requirement. The Department appealed.


OVERVIEW: The driver's license was suspended as a result of his conviction for driving under the influence (DUI). The driver was convicted of DUI on three prior occasions. The court ordered the mandatory one-year suspension of the driver's operating privileges but did not order the installation of an ignition interlock system. By notice mailed to the driver, the Department notified the driver that his operating privileges were suspended and that in order to restore the privileges he would have to equip each of his vehicles with an ignition interlock system or remain ineligible for restoration for an additional one-year period. On appeal, the court found that the Pennsylvania Supreme Court had invalidated the 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 7002(b) (current version at 75 Pa. Const. Stat. Ann. § 3805) requirement that the trial court order installation of an ignition interlock system on each vehicle owned by the person to be effective upon the restoration of operating privileges. No other provision was in effect at the time of the driver's conviction and notice of suspension that would authorize the Department to act independently to order the installation of an ignition interlock system.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed to the extent that it sustained the driver's appeal from the Department's notice that he was required to obtain an ignition interlock restricted license if he sought reinstatement of his operating privilege after the expiration of the suspension of his operating privilege. The order was affirmed to the extent that it sustained the driver's appeal from notice of a requirement that he install ignition interlock systems.

Finnegan v. DOT, Bureau of Driver Licensing


No. 621 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


844 A.2d 645; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 213


January 30, 2004, Submitted

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 02-5150 Civil Term. Common Pleas Court of the County of Cumberland. Judge OLER.
Finnegan v. DOT, Bureau of Driver Licensing, 2003 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 868 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Dec. 4, 2003)



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing, sought review of an order from the Common Pleas Court of the County of Cumberland (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee licensee's challenge to the agency's requirement that the licensee install an ignition interlock system as a condition to having his driving privileges restored. The court affirmed the order, and the agency sought reconsideration.


OVERVIEW: The licensee pled guilty to a third offense of driving under the influence. Upon accepting the plea, the trial court did not require the licensee to install an ignition interlock system pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7002(b). The agency suspended the licensee's operating privilege as a consequence of his conviction and informed him that he was required to equip all vehicles owned by him with an ignition interlock system in order for his operating privilege to be restored at the end of that period. Although the agency had authority under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7003(2) to issue interlock-restricted licenses, the court concluded that the agency did not have the independent authority to require installation of interlock devices.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order.

 

1968 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Rebel v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd. (Emery World Wide Airlines #150)


No. 1351 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


844 A.2d 653; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 214


February 2, 2004, Argued  
March 16, 2004, Decided  
March 16, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by, Vacated by Rebel v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd. (Emery World Airlines #150), 2005 Pa. LEXIS 1605 (Pa., Aug. 5, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. A02-1444 State Agency Workers' Compensation Appeal Board.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner claimant filed a claim alleging that she sustained a work-related injury. A workers' compensation judge (WCJ) found that the claimant sustained a work-related injury and awarded her total disability benefits for one period of time and partial disability benefits thereafter. The Pennsylvania Workers' Compensation Appeal Board affirmed the decision. The claimant filed a petition for review.


OVERVIEW: The claimant contended that the WCJ and the Board erred in calculating her average weekly wage (AWW) under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77, § 582(d) and, alternatively, that § 582 violated her right to equal protection of the laws under both the Pennsylvania and the United States Constitutions. More specifically, she asserted that her AWW was artificially low because she missed a substantial amount of work during the four consecutive 13-week periods due to maternity leave. She also argued that § 582(d) diminished her right to workers' compensation benefits based solely on her gender. On appeal, the court found that the claimant was continuously employed during the period of her maternity leave for purposes of calculating her AWW under § 582(d). Consequently, because she was continuously employed by her employer for the four 13-calendar-week periods immediately preceding her work injury, the WCJ and the Board did not err in calculating her AWW under § 582(d). Further, § 582 did not violate the claimant's right to equal protection of the law under either the United States or Pennsylvania Constitutions.


OUTCOME: The order of the Board was affirmed.

Warren County Human Servs. v. State Civ. Serv. Comm'n (Roberts)


No. 376 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


844 A.2d 70; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 195


June 27, 2003, Submitted on Briefs  
March 8, 2004, Decided  
March 8, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Warren County Human Servs. v. State Civ. Serv. Comm'n, 863 A.2d 1152, 2004 Pa. LEXIS 2953 (Pa., 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 22814. State Civil Service Commission.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county employer appealed the order of respondent State Civil Service Commission (Pennsylvania), which granted respondent employee's challenge to his removal as a caseworker because of a 1980 conviction for aggravated assault in violation of the Child Protective Services Law, 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 6301-6385.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the employer argued that the Commission erred in finding that 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6344(c) violated Pa. Const. art. I, § 1, and in concluding that the employer failed to present evidence establishing just cause for the removal of the employee. The appellate court found that 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6344(c) prohibited the hiring of applicants previously convicted of certain enumerated crimes; however, it did not ban existing employees from continuing to work in the child-care field, despite having a similar conviction. Because 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6344(c), as it related to aggravated assault, created limitations that had no temporal proximity to the time of hiring, it did not bear a real and substantial relationship to the Commonwealth's interest in protecting children and was unconstitutional. In addition, the employer failed to present any evidence establishing just cause for the employee's removal. The employer admitted that but for 23 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6344, the employee would not have been removed from his position, as his work was exemplary--demonstrating that his remote conviction did not reflect upon his present abilities to perform the duties of a caseworker.


OUTCOME: The Commission's order was affirmed.

Hines Nurseries, Inc. v. Plumstead Twp. Bd. of Supervisors

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied April 19, 2004. Reversed by, Appeal granted by Hines Nurseries, Inc. v. Plumstead Twp. Bd. of Supervisors, 580 Pa. 357, 861 A.2d 260, 2004 Pa. LEXIS 2469 (Pa., Oct. 20, 2004)

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenor property owner sought review of a decision of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Bucks (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a decision by respondent Plumstead Township Board of Supervisors to attach certain conditions to a subdivision application filed by plaintiff applicant. OUTCOME: The trial court's order was affirmed.

 

1971 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Kopko v. Miller


751 M.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


842 A.2d 1028; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 131


December 9, 2003, Argued  
February 20, 2004, Decided  
February 20, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Kopko v. Miller, 2006 Pa. LEXIS 41 (Pa., Feb. 28, 2006)



DISPOSITION:  [**1] Respondent's application for summary relief granted; Petitioners' application for summary relief denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, sheriffs, deputy sheriffs, and the district attorneys of four counties, filed a petition for review in the nature of a complaint in equity against respondent, the Commissioner of the Pennsylvania State Police. The Commissioner had ruled that sheriffs and their deputies were not investigative or law enforcement officers entitled to certain wiretap training. The parties all filed applications for summary relief.


OVERVIEW: Five of the deputies had applied for wiretap investigation training pursuant to the requirements of the Wiretapping and Electronic Surveillance Control Act (Wiretap Act), 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5701-5781. The applications were rejected based on the Commissioner's determination that under the Wiretap Act, the deputies were not eligible for the training. The court agreed. At common law, sheriffs possessed broad powers, but the authority to conduct wiretap investigations was statutory in nature and did not extend to those common law powers. Sheriffs in Pennsylvania had only limited law enforcement functions. Their main functions were court-related, although the Pennsylvania Supreme Court had recognized limited law enforcement functions, such as effectuating warrantless arrests for offense committed in their presence. Neither the sheriffs, nor their deputies had the power or right to investigate any of the predicate offenses for obtaining a wiretap order. Therefore, they could not be considered investigative or law enforcement officers under the Wiretap Act, and they were ineligible for the wiretap training offered by the State Police.


OUTCOME: The court granted the Commissioner's application for summary relief and denied the application filed by petitioners.

Freedman v. DOT, Bureau of Driver Licensing


No. 1141 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


842 A.2d 494; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 113


December 10, 2003, Argued  
February 10, 2004, Decided  
February 10, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 2003-C-0336. Common Pleas Court of the County of Lehigh. Judge LAVELLE, S.J.



DISPOSITION: Vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing, appealed the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County (Pennsylvania), which sustained the appeal of appellee licensee from the requirement that he install ignition interlock devices on all vehicles that he owned before his operating privilege could be restored. The trial court allowed the appeal nunc pro tunc.


OVERVIEW: The licensee pled guilty to driving under the influence (DUI) of alcohol. This was the licensee's second DUI conviction. Neither the Commonwealth nor the licensee appealed the judgment of sentence. Thereafter, the Department notified the licensee that his operating privilege was suspended for one year and that prior to the restoration of his operating privilege he would need to have ignition interlock devices installed on his vehicles. The licensee did not take an appeal from this suspension notice within the statutory period. On appeal, the Department argued that the trial court erred when it allowed the licensee to appeal nunc pro tunc. The appellate court found that the restoration of driving privileges, i.e., whether and when repeat DUI offenders were entitled to conditional restoration of their operating privileges, was not a function of the court, but rather the unique authority and responsibility of the Department. Because there was no contention by the licensee that other circumstances warranted allowance of a nunc pro tunc appeal, the licensee failed to timely challenge the Department's imposition of the ignition interlock requirement.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court was vacated and remanded with instructions to quash the licensee's statutory appeal as untimely.

 

1973 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Roddey v. County Council of Allegheny


No. 2507 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


841 A.2d 1087; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 57


December 9, 2003, Argued  
February 5, 2004, Decided  
February 5, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. GD 03-1594 Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Judge JAMES.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Application to reinstate automatic supersedeas denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the elected Chief Executive (CE) of Allegheny County, appealed two orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania). The first order dismissed the CE's complaint against appellees, the Allegheny County Council and the county sheriff. The second order denied the CE's motion for reconsideration. The CE also sought to reinstate the automatic supersedeas that arose as a result of the CE's appeal on the merits.


OVERVIEW: The Council adopted three resolutions regarding an operating budget, a capital budget, and a grants, special accounts, and agency fund budget. The CE exercised his power under 302 Pa. Code § 1.7-705 by vetoing certain items in all three budget resolutions. Specifically, he used his veto power to reduce allocations to certain items--a reduction veto. On appeal, the CE raised several issues. The appellate court concluded that the language in the Home Rule Charter was clear and plain. The CE could exercise a line-item veto, but not a reduction line-item veto. Thus, the Charter granted to the CE only the power to strike certain items entirely, and not the power to reduce the amounts appropriated for individual line items in a budget resolution. In addition, the CE's attempt to exercise a power that the Charter did not grant to him resulted in a nullity of the vetoes he sought to effectuate by reduction. Also, the Charter gave the CE the power to veto only items in the annual operating and capital budgets. The trial court did not err in concluding that the CE's vetoes were improper.


OUTCOME: The orders of the trial court were affirmed. The CE's application to reinstate automatic supersedeas was denied.

 

1974 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


J & K Trash Removal, Inc. v. City of Chester


No. 1740 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


842 A.2d 983; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 48


December 8, 2003, Argued  
January 23, 2004, Decided  
January 23, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied March 19, 2004. Appeal denied by City of Chester v. J & K Trash Removal, Inc., 581 Pa. 708, 867 A.2d 524, 2005 Pa. LEXIS 45 (Pa., Jan. 12, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 2001-8556. Common Pleas Court of the County of Delaware. Judge PROUD.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a case involving a business privilege tax, appellant corporation appealed the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which denied its motion for summary judgment and determined that appellee city was entitled to tax all of the corporation's gross receipts. The common pleas court authorized an immediate appeal of the interlocutory order. The appellate court granted the corporation permission to appeal.


OVERVIEW: The corporation operated a trash hauling business. It maintained an office within the city and conducted its business both inside and outside of the city. The city enacted a business privilege tax on the gross volume of business transacted within the "territorial limits" of the city. On appeal, the corporation argued that the common pleas court committed an error of law when it denied the corporation's motion for summary judgment because the language of City of Chester, Pa., Tax Ordinance § 307.02 did not authorize the city to seek back taxes, penalties, and interest from the corporation on receipts from the gross volume of business transacted outside the city. The appellate court concluded that the language of the ordinance at issue was different from the code scrutinized in the Gilberti case. The common pleas court did not address the difference between the ordinance and the code when it denied the corporation's motion for summary judgment on the basis of Gilberti. Thus, under the city ordinance, the corporation was not liable for the business privilege tax of the city for the years in question on gross receipts earned outside the territorial limits of the city.


OUTCOME: The order of the common pleas court was reversed, and the case was remanded to the common pleas court to enter an appropriate order granting the corporation's motion for summary judgment.

Cinquina v. DOT, Bureau of Driver Licensing


No. 1640 C.D. 2003 1641 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


840 A.2d 525; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 23


November 7, 2003, Submitted  
January 9, 2004, Decided  
January 9, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Amended February 10, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed From Nos. 03-1832 & 03-1833 Common Pleas Court of the County of Delaware. Judge ZETUSKY.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Department of Transportation, Bureau of Driver Licensing (PennDOT), appealed a judgment by the Common Pleas Court of the County of Delaware (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee licensee's appeal of PennDOT's requirement that ignition interlock devices be installed on his vehicles before it would restore his driving privilege.


OVERVIEW: After the licensee was convicted of three separate incidents of driving under the influence of alcohol, the PennDOT sent him a series of letters suspending his driving privilege and requiring the installation of ignition interlock devices on all vehicles that the licensee owned before his license would be restored. The licensee claimed that the PennDOT lacked independent authority to require installation of ignition interlock devices without a court order. The appellate court held that, pursuant to 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 7002(3), the only authority that the PennDOT had was to issue interlock restricted licenses. Nowhere did the remaining Ignition Interlock Device Act, 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 7001-7003, grant the PennDOT the independent authority to require installation of interlock devices. As a result, the trial court properly sustained the licensee's appeal.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Caln Nether Co., L.P. v. Bd. of Supervisors


No. 33 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


840 A.2d 484; 2004 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 10


November 3, 2003, Argued  
January 8, 2004, Decided  
January 8, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Caln Nether Co., L.P. v. Bd. of Supervisors, 856 A.2d 835, 2004 Pa. LEXIS 1746 (Pa., 2004)
Appeal denied by Caln Nether Co., L.P. v. Zoning Hearing Bd., 857 A.2d 680, 2004 Pa. LEXIS 2069 (Pa., 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 01-10419. Common Pleas Court of the County of Chester. Judge SHENKIN.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner challenged an order of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Chester (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the rejection of the landowner's challenge to a township's ordinance as exclusionary by appellee board of supervisors. The landowner contended that the ordinance failed to provide for "new and used car dealership" use, but the board concluded that the ordinance permitted such use under the "retail store" use classification.


OVERVIEW: The landowner contended that the use provisions of the ordinance resulted in the de jure or de facto exclusion of a new and used car dealership. Affirming the rejection of the landowner's claim, the court held that: (1) the landowner's appeal was not subject to dismissal merely because its Pa. R. App. P. 1925(b) statement addressed numerous issues; (2) a car dealership fell within the plain meaning of the term "retail store," and thus the landowner did not meet its heavy burden of proving the ordinance was unlawfully exclusionary; (3) the ordinance was not de facto exclusionary because the permanent outdoor merchandise storage limitation and the prohibition on front year display did not effectively prohibit car dealership use; (4) pursuant to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53 § 10908(6), evidence that the landowner contended should have been admitted at the hearing was irrelevant and, therefore, properly excluded; and (5) the landowner did not establish grounds for disqualification because, after examining each supervisor regarding potential bias, the landowner failed to produce evidence to show the supervisors exhibited bias in their conduct during the hearings in the matter.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision rejecting the landowners' constitutional challenge to the zoning ordinance.

 

1977 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


S. Union Twp. v. Dep't of Entvl. Prot.


No. 678 M.D. 2002


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


839 A.2d 1179; 2003 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 926


September 10, 2003, Argued  
December 24, 2003, Decided  
December 24, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by S. Union Twp. v. Dep't of Entvl. Prot., 2004 Pa. LEXIS 1637 (Pa., July 22, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.



DISPOSITION: Respondents' preliminary objections sustained; amended petition for review dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, a commonwealth and its department of environmental protection, and intervenor waste industry association filed preliminary objections in the state's highest court (Pennsylvania) to an amended petition filed by petitioners, various local governmental entities and their residents, alleging that portions of the Pennsylvania Environmental Resources Code (Chapter 63), specifically 27 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 6301-6306, were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners argued that the initiation of disposal fees under Chapter 63 violated their due process rights. The appellate court held that as petitioners challenged legislative, rather than official action, their procedural due process claim failed. Chapter 63 was not overruled under the doctrine of stare decisis as petitioners passed the fee on to the waste generators. Previous caselaw was not controlling, as petitioners did not aver an inability to pass the disposal fee on to the generators of the waste. The $ 0.50 per month increase in the monthly fee paid by the individual petitioners did not constitute a substantial impairment of a contractual relationship. Assuming that the surcharge was a substantial impairment of constitutional significance, petitioners still would not prevail, as the impairment was outweighed by the necessity of the regulation and the benefits to the public good. To the extent the surcharge could be deemed an impairment, it was nominal, leaving the hurdle to be cleared by Chapter 63 low. Petitioners did not plead facts to show that the disposal fee was not imposed uniformly. Finally, Chapter 63 was clear and not in the least vague.


OUTCOME: The preliminary objections were sustained. The amended petition was dismissed.

 

1978 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Bixler v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd. (Walden Books)


No. 866 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


837 A.2d 1278; 2003 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 919


October 3, 2003, Submitted  
December 17, 2003, Decided  
December 17, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Bixler v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd. (Walden Books), 2004 Pa. LEXIS 2400 (Pa., Oct. 14, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. A02-1793 State Agency Workers' Compensation Appeal Board.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner workers' compensation claimant petitioned for review of an order of respondent Pennsylvania Workers' Compensation Appeal Board that affirmed the decision of a workers' compensation judge (WCJ) granting the review petition of respondent employer.


OVERVIEW: The claimant argued that the treatment of her self-employment earnings differently than the earnings of a claimant who was concurrently employed violated her equal protection rights. The appellate court held that § 309(e) of the Pennsylvania Workers' Compensation Act, 1915 Pa. Laws 736, as amended, specifically Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77, § 582(e), did not violate the Equal Protection Clause. As a self-employed individual was neither an employee nor an employer under the Act and was not required to participate in the workers' compensation system, it would be inequitable to require an employer to include self-employment earnings in a claimant's wage loss benefits. Thus, the classification relating to self-employed claimants was rationally related to the state's legitimate goal of balancing the interests of employees and employers under the workers' compensation system. Moreover, the government had a legitimate interest in excluding self-employment earnings from concurrent employment wage calculations to ensure that employers were only required to pay benefits for verifiable wages and reliably-reported income.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

 

1979 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


City of Philadelphia v. Phila. Parking Auth.


No. 616 M.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


837 A.2d 1267; 2003 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 915


November 4, 2003, Argued  
December 15, 2003, Decided  
December 15, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected December 17, 2003. Reargument denied January 14, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY: Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.



DISPOSITION: Matter not within Commonwealth Court's original jurisdiction; remanded to Common Pleas Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Philadelphia Parking Authority (PPA) filed preliminary objections challenging a suit filed by appellee City of Philadelphia seeking monetary damages and specific performance for breaches of two contracts, after the Common Pleas Court (Pennsylvania) transferred the suit to the appellate court, holding that the appellate court had exclusive original jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: The City argued that the suit was not within the appellate court's original jurisdiction. The appellate court held that the PPA was a local agency for jurisdiction purposes. What made an authority a local agency was not who appointed the board members, but who created the authority. Despite the fact that Pennsylvania Act 22, 53 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5501 et seq., changed who appointed board members to the PPA, the Act did not change the fact that the City created the PPA or that in first class cities, only the city could create a parking authority. Section 5504(1) (53 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5504(1)) provided that a parking authority was created if a legislative body desired to organize such an authority, and it adopted a resolution or ordinance signifying intention to do so. "Legislative body" was defined as the council of a city or borough and the board of commissioners of a first class township. Because the PPA was created by the City, it was, by definition, a local authority. Because the PPA was a local authority and not part of the "commonwealth government," and because prior caselaw remained controlling, the suit did not fall within the appellate court's original jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The case was remanded to the trial court.

 

1980 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


City Council of Reading v. Eppihimer


No. 2921 C.D. 2002


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


835 A.2d 883; 2003 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 844


June 4, 2003, Argued  
November 17, 2003, Decided  
November 17, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed From No. 01-3624. Common Pleas Court of the County of Berks. Judge SCHMEHL.



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Two defendants, the mayor of Reading, Pennsylvania, and its managing director, appealed an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Berks County, Pennsylvania, that granted the complaint in declaratory judgment filed by plaintiff City Council. The appeal concerned whether the City Council had certain authority regarding two staff positions.


OVERVIEW: The appellate issues were (1) whether the Mayor and Managing Director alone possessed administrative and executive authority over Reading's merit personnel system, (2) whether the Home Rule Charter for The City of Reading, Pennsylvania authorized City Council to appoint only the City Clerk, (3) whether the employment authority of the Mayor and Managing Director was confined to the "Administrative Service" or "Administrative Branch," and (4) whether the separation of powers doctrine required that City Council had power to hire and fire for the positions of City Council Chief of Staff and City Council Legislative Coordinator. The court concluded that the Managing Director's suspension and removal powers as described in § 406(c) of the Charter would apply to these two positions. Second, the City Council lacked power over the two newly created positions. Third, the Mayor and Managing Director had personnel responsibilities for all non-exempt career service employees in the City's government. Finally, in Pennsylvania, because the State Constitution did not dictate the separation of powers at the local government level, there was no Constitutional separation of powers at that level.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court was reversed in part.

 

1981 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Donahue v. Public Sch. Emples. Ret. Sys.


No. 325 M.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


834 A.2d 655; 2003 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 752


September 10, 2003, Argued  
October 21, 2003, Decided  
October 21, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Donahue v. Public Sch. Emples. Ret. Sys., 2004 Pa. LEXIS 2171 (Pa., Sept. 22, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.



DISPOSITION: Respondents' preliminary objection in the nature of a demurrer sustained and the petition for review dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner retired teachers filed a petition for review asserting a class action for a judgment declaring unconstitutional amendments to the Public School Employees' Retirement Code, 24 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 8101-8535, and the State Employees' Retirement Code, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 5101-5956. Respondents, the retirement systems, their boards of trustees, and their chairpersons, asserted a demurrer and challenged the teachers' standing.


OVERVIEW: 2001 Pa. Laws 9 amended both the Public School and State Employees' Retirement Codes to allow current employees to increase the amount of their contributions and receive an increased amount upon retirement. Upon election to the new class, the employees' prior service was credited under the new membership class. Members of either system who retired before the effective date of the Act were not eligible to elect a new class of membership. The teachers argued that the amendments violated equal protection under the Pennsylvania and U.S. Constitutions. The court held that the rational basis test was the correct standard for the equal protection inquiry under both constitutions, since the legislation at issue burdened neither a fundamental right nor a suspect class, and the fact that the legislators amended the retirement system in which they participated provided no basis for heightened judicial scrutiny. The legislation did not violate equal protection, as limiting participation in new service classes to members actively employed and contributing was a rational means to achieve valid legislative objectives.


OUTCOME: The respondents' preliminary objection in the nature of a demurrer was sustained and the petition for review was dismissed.

 

1982 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Brown v. Blaine


No. 822 C.D. 2003


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


833 A.2d 1166; 2003 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 742


September 12, 2003, Submitted on Briefs  
October 15, 2003, Decided  
October 15, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Brown v. Beard, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 32574 (W.D. Pa., Dec. 13, 2005)
Subsequent appeal at, Petition denied by Brown v. Blaine, 610 Pa. 601, 20 A.3d 489, 2011 Pa. LEXIS 814 (2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: Brown v. Blaine, 80 Fed. Appx. 284, 2003 U.S. App. LEXIS 13667 (3d Cir. Pa., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate sought review of an order of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Greene (Pennsylvania), which sustained the preliminary objections of appellees, prison officials and guards, and dismissed the inmate's complaint for failure to state a claim upon which relief could be granted. The complaint alleged violations of 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 and due process rights, libel, and civil conspiracy.


OVERVIEW: The inmate filed grievances regarding his placement in a restricted housing unit. Subsequently, he was found guilty of two misconduct charges and was transferred to a long-term segregation unit (LTSU). The inmate sued, claiming that he had been retaliated against for filing the grievances. The trial court dismissed the case. On appeal, the court held that the inmate had stated a claim for retaliation because the claim was made under U.S. Const. amend. I and Pa. Const. art. I, § 26, and not 37 Pa. Code § 93.9, which created no private remedy. The inmate's due process claims, which related to the retaliation and the disciplinary hearing's procedure, were properly dismissed because his confinement in a LTSU did not impose an atypical and significant hardship on him in relation to ordinary prison life, and thus, no liberty interest was triggered. The libel claim was properly dismissed because even if sufficiently pleaded, it did not fall within any of the exceptions to immunity and could not be maintained under 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8522(b). Finally, the conspiracy claim was properly dismissed because the inmate failed to plead sufficient facts or the necessary predicate illegal act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the portions of the trial court's order that sustained the preliminary objections to the due process, libel, and conspiracy claims. The court reversed the portion of the trial court's order that sustained the objection to the inmate's retaliation claim.
